The Two Sunni Kalam Schools: Ash'ari and Maturidi
Author introduces the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools and their Sunni affiliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical emergence of the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools and their shared identity as the two wings of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari and Maturidi schools are the two wings of Sunni theology.

This passage from the introduction of Tabṣirat al-Adilla sets the stage by identifying the two major Sunni kalam schools that arose in the 3rd and 4th centuries AH. The author, Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, establishes that both schools belong to Ahl al-Sunna and are considered its two wings. Understanding this foundational point is crucial for studying the rest of the work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the schools
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the names, founders, and locations of the two schools.
2
Identify the relationship
Notice how the author describes the relationship between the two schools and Ahl al-Sunna. What phrase does he use?
He says 'they are the two wings of the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna'.
3
Reflect on the significance
Think about why the author emphasizes this unity. How does this affect the study of kalam?
It shows that differences between the schools are within the Sunni fold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم اللہ الرحمن الرحيم
مقدمة
الحمد لله رب العالمين والصلاة والسلام على أشرف المرسلين وخاتم النبيين سيدنا محمد وعلى آله وأصحابه الطيبين الطاهرين، وبعد:
ففي القرنين الثالث والرابع من الهجرة النبوية المشرفة، نشأت مدرستان من مدارس علم الكلام الإسلامي، الأولى في البصرة، وتسمى بالمدرسة الأشعرية نسبة لأبي الحسن الأشعري المتوفى سنة ٣٢٤ه.
والثانية في بلاد ما وراء النهر، وتسمى بالمدرسة الماتريدية نسبة لأبي منصور الماتريدي المتوفى سنة ٣٣٣هـ.
والمدرستان تنتميان لمذهب أهل السنة والجماعة، بل هما في أصح الأقوال جناحا مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة.
ولكل مدرسة رجالها الذين كان جُلُّ همهم نشر الإسلام، والدفاع عن العقيدة الإسلامية بالنقل والعقل.
ويعتبر أبو المعين ميمون بن محمد النسفي المتوفى ٥٠٨هـ أهم رجال المدرسة الماتريدية لما يلي:
أ- أنه جعل من همه نصرة مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة ونشره.
ب- عمل جاهدًا على إبطال مذاهب الخصوم بالنقل والعقل.
ج- اعتمد في مناقشاته على اللغة العربية الفصيحة مع التوسط في العبارة حتى يتمكن القارئ من فهمه.
د- ابتعد كل البعد عن الاشتغال بالجدل العقيم، والأدلة الغامضة المشوشة للعقول.
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Introduction. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, and blessings and peace be upon the noblest of messengers and the seal of the prophets, our master Muhammad, and upon his family and companions, the good and pure. To proceed: In the third and fourth centuries of the noble Prophetic Hijra, two schools of Islamic kalam emerged. The first was in Basra, called the Ash'ari school, attributed to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (d. 324 AH). The second was in Transoxiana, called the Maturidi school, attributed to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (d. 333 AH). Both schools belong to the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah; indeed, in the most correct opinions, they are the two wings of the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah. Each school has its men whose main concern was spreading Islam and defending the Islamic creed through transmitted and rational proofs. Abu al-Mu'in Maymun ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi (d. 508 AH) is considered the most important of the Maturidi school's men for the following reasons: a) He made it his concern to support and spread the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah. b) He worked hard to invalidate the doctrines of opponents through transmitted and rational proofs. c) He relied in his discussions on eloquent Arabic with moderation in expression so that the reader could understand him. d) He completely avoided engaging in futile argumentation and confusing proofs that disturb minds.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is the introduction (muqaddima) of the book. It begins with the basmalah and praise. Then it states that in the 3rd and 4th centuries AH, two schools of Islamic kalam emerged: the Ash'ari school in Basra (founded by Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, d. 324 AH) and the Maturidi school in Transoxiana (founded by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, d. 333 AH). Both schools belong to Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah and are considered the two wings of that madhhab. Each school had scholars who spread Islam and defended the creed using textual and rational proofs. The introduction then focuses on Abu al-Mu'in Maymun ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi (d. 508 AH), who is considered the most important figure of the Maturidi school for the following four reasons: a) He made it his concern to support and spread the school of Ahl al-Sunna; b) He worked hard to invalidate the doctrines of opponents using transmitted and rational proofs; c) He relied on eloquent Arabic and moderate expression for reader comprehension; d) He completely avoided futile argumentation and confusing proofs.

Key Terms
الأشعرية
الماتريدية
أهل السنة والجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidi school is separate from Ahl al-Sunna or is a later innovation.
Correction: The author explicitly states that both schools belong to Ahl al-Sunna and are its two wings, affirming their Sunni orthodoxy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools?
A) They are opposing schools
B) They are both branches of Mu'tazila
C) They are the two wings of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah
D) The Maturidi school is a sub-school of the Ash'ari school

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari and Maturidi schools are the two recognized Sunni theological schools, together forming the orthodoxy of Ahl al-Sunna.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that there are not one but two Sunni theological schools? Does this challenge any assumption you held about the unity of Sunni theology?
?
The source calls the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools 'the two wings of Ahl al-Sunna'. A bird cannot fly with one wing. Are you personally relying on only one wing in your understanding of faith? How can you intentionally learn from both traditions?
?
If you were to defend the Sunni identity against those who claim only one school is valid, could you confidently use the wording from this lesson? What would it mean for your relationship with fellow Muslims if you truly embraced them as co-equal wings?

Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi: The Defender of Sunni Creed
Author lists four reasons for al-Nasafi's prominence in the Maturidi school.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi is considered the most important figure of the Maturidi school and his four key contributions.

Core Idea
Al-Nasafi combined rational and textual proofs, eloquent Arabic, and avoided futile debate.

After establishing the two schools, the author introduces Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi as the most important figure of the Maturidi school. This passage lists four reasons for his importance, highlighting his dedication to supporting Sunni doctrine, refuting opponents, using clear Arabic, and avoiding confusing arguments. These traits made his works enduring references in Maturidi kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about al-Nasafi
Read the Arabic list of reasons carefully. Note the four points (أ, ب, ج, د).
2
Analyze each reason
For each reason, consider what it tells us about al-Nasafi's methodology and priorities.
Focus on 'supporting and spreading,' 'invalidating opponents,' 'eloquent Arabic,' and 'avoiding futile argumentation.'
3
Reflect on the overall picture
How do these four reasons together make him the most important? What does this say about the Maturidi school's approach?
It shows a balanced approach of defense, clarity, and rational engagement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم اللہ الرحمن الرحيم
مقدمة
الحمد لله رب العالمين والصلاة والسلام على أشرف المرسلين وخاتم النبيين سيدنا محمد وعلى آله وأصحابه الطيبين الطاهرين، وبعد:
ففي القرنين الثالث والرابع من الهجرة النبوية المشرفة، نشأت مدرستان من مدارس علم الكلام الإسلامي، الأولى في البصرة، وتسمى بالمدرسة الأشعرية نسبة لأبي الحسن الأشعري المتوفى سنة ٣٢٤ه.
والثانية في بلاد ما وراء النهر، وتسمى بالمدرسة الماتريدية نسبة لأبي منصور الماتريدي المتوفى سنة ٣٣٣هـ.
والمدرستان تنتميان لمذهب أهل السنة والجماعة، بل هما في أصح الأقوال جناحا مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة.
ولكل مدرسة رجالها الذين كان جُلُّ همهم نشر الإسلام، والدفاع عن العقيدة الإسلامية بالنقل والعقل.

In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Introduction. Praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds, and blessings and peace be upon the noblest of messengers and the seal of the prophets, our master Muhammad, and upon his family and companions, the good and pure. To proceed: In the third and fourth centuries of the noble Prophetic Hijra, two schools of Islamic kalam emerged. The first was in Basra, called the Ash'ari school, attributed to Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (d. 324 AH). The second was in Transoxiana, called the Maturidi school, attributed to Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (d. 333 AH). Both schools belong to the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah; indeed, in the most correct opinions, they are the two wings of the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah. Each school has its men whose main concern was spreading Islam and defending the Islamic creed through transmitted and rational proofs. Abu al-Mu'in Maymun ibn Muhammad al-Nasafi (d. 508 AH) is considered the most important of the Maturidi school's men for the following reasons: a) He made it his concern to support and spread the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'ah. b) He worked hard to invalidate the doctrines of opponents through transmitted and rational proofs. c) He relied in his discussions on eloquent Arabic with moderation in expression so that the reader could understand him. d) He completely avoided engaging in futile argumentation and confusing proofs that disturb minds.
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage is the introduction (muqaddima) of the book. It begins with the basmalah and praise. Then it states that in the 3rd and 4th centuries AH, two schools of Islamic kalam emerged: the Ash'ari school in Basra (founded by Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, d. 324 AH) and the Maturidi school in Transoxiana (founded by Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, d. 333 AH). Key Terms
أبو المعين النسفي
النقل والعقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Nasafi was merely a commentator or transmitter of earlier Maturidi thought.
Correction: The passage shows he was an active defender and refuter of opponents, using both transmitted and rational proofs, indicating original scholarly work.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what method did al-Nasafi use to invalidate the doctrines of opponents?
A) Only transmitted proofs
B) Only rational proofs
C) Both transmitted and rational proofs
D) Mystical unveiling

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi is the most important Maturidi scholar because he actively supported Sunni doctrine, refuted opponents with clear rational arguments, and avoided confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nasafi made it his personal mission to support and spread the truth. What is your personal mission in learning this creed? Are you merely accumulating knowledge, or will you take a concrete step this week to actively defend what you learn, even in a small way? Write down one specific action you will take.
?
He combined transmitted proofs (revelation) and rational proofs (reason) in defending his faith. Do you tend to rely more on one than the other in your own understanding? Are you willing to engage both your scripture and your intellect, even when they seem to conflict? What would that look like in your next study session?
?
Al-Nasafi avoided futile argumentation and confusing proofs. Think of a recent discussion about religion where you became frustrated or confused. What can you learn from his example to keep your future conversations about faith constructive and clear? Commit to one change in how you approach disagreements.

Editor's Methodology: Ta‘līq and Dirāsah
Editor describes his two-part methodology: ta‘līq and dirāsah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two components of the editor's methodology for studying Tabsirat al-Adilla: the commentary (ta‘līq) and the study (dirāsah).

Core Idea
The editor's methodology has two parts: commentary on the text and a study of its context.

Before diving into the theological content of Tabsirat al-Adilla, it is important to understand how the editor approaches the manuscript. This passage from the editorial preface outlines the two main aspects of his work: ta‘līq, which involves clarifying and connecting the text, and dirāsah, which introduces the author and the work and offers comparative analysis. This lesson will guide you through these two aspects as described by the editor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the editor's description. Then read the translation to ensure you understand the literal meaning.
Pay attention to the distinction between the two parts: ta‘līq and dirāsah.
2
Identify the two components
Find where the editor defines ta‘līq and dirāsah. Note what each entails: ta‘līq includes clarifying terms, linking parts, adding evidence; dirāsah includes introducing the author and work, and presenting a comparative overview.
The editor also mentions when detailed analysis is necessary.
3
Consider the purpose
Reflect on why the editor chose this methodology. How does each part contribute to understanding the manuscript?
This is not explicitly in the passage but is implicit in the structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة الأدلة لأبي المعين النسفي ج
والفرق، والمفردات، والمصطلحات الكلامية والفلسفية والعلمية، وتوضيح المبهمات، وربط أجزاء المخطوط بعضها ببعض، وربطها بالمؤلَّفات الأخرى للمؤلف أو غيره، وإضافة أدلة أخرى، مع بيان الرأي في الأفكار المعروضة كلما أمكن.
الدراسة : ونعني بها التعريف بكل ما يتصل بالمؤلف - بكسر ما قبل الآخر - والمؤلَّف - بفتح ما قبل الآخر - كما نعني بها طرح عرض موجز للنص المحقق إن أمكن دون إسهاب وتحليل بعض قضاياه مع المقارنة بالمثيل لها في الزمن السابق واللاحق لمؤلّف المخطوط لبيان التأثير والتأثر.
والتحليل التفصيلي ضروري إذا كانت صفحات المخطوط قليلة، أما إذا كانت كثيرة فيتعذر الأمر.
والله ولي التوفيق.
أ.د/ محمد الأنور حامد عيسى
Tabsirat al-Adilla by Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi j. And [commentary on] differences, vocabulary, kalam and philosophical and scientific terms, clarification of ambiguities, linking parts of the manuscript to each other, linking them to other works of the author or others, adding other evidences, with statement of opinion on the presented ideas whenever possible. The study: we mean by it the introduction of everything related to the author—with kasr before the last letter—and the work—with fath before the last letter—as we also mean by it presenting a brief overview of the edited text if possible without verbosity and analysis of some of its issues with comparison to similar ones in the time before and after the manuscript's author to show influence and being influenced. And detailed analysis is necessary if the manuscript's pages are few, but if they are many then it is difficult. And Allah is the grantor of success. Prof. Dr. Muhammad al-Anwar Hamid Isa.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is an editorial preface describing the methodology of the edition. It opens with the book title, 'Tabsirat al-Adilla by Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi j', then describes the first aspect: commentary (ta‘līq). The commentary includes addressing differences, vocabulary, technical terms from kalam, philosophy, and sciences; clarifying obscurities; connecting parts of the manuscript to each other and to other works by the same author or others; adding additional evidence; and giving an opinion on presented ideas whenever possible. The second aspect is the study (dirāsah), which the editor defines as introducing everything related to the author (with kasr on the middle letter of mu'allif) and the work (with fath on the middle letter of mu'allaf). It also involves offering a concise presentation of the edited text if feasible, avoiding prolixity, and analyzing some of its issues in comparison with similar works from earlier and later periods to demonstrate influence and influence received. The editor notes that detailed analysis is necessary when the manuscript pages are few; otherwise, it becomes impracticable. The passage ends with a basmalah-style invocation and the editor's name.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta‘līq)
دراسة (dirāsah)
مخطوط (manuscript)
محقق (edited text)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The editor's 'ta‘līq' here refers to the general kalam concept of commentary (like a gloss) without specific methodology.
Correction: The editor's 'ta‘līq' is a specific editorial methodology that includes clarifying terms, linking parts, adding evidence, and stating opinions on ideas. It is not just any commentary but a structured approach to the manuscript.

Checkpoint
According to the editor, what does 'al-dirasah' (الدراسة) involve?
A) Introducing the author and work and presenting a brief overview of the edited text with comparison to similar works.
B) Only listing the technical terms and clarifying ambiguities.
C) Detailed analysis of every page regardless of length.
D) Adding extra evidence from later authors.

Key Takeaway
The editor's methodology for studying Tabsirat al-Adilla consists of two distinct components: ta‘līq (commentary on the text) and dirāsah (study of the author, work, and comparative analysis).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a scholarly work, do you tend to focus only on the surface meaning (like ta‘līq) or do you also investigate the author's context and influences (dirāsah)? Which aspect do you neglect? Commit to studying one text this week using both methods.
?
The editor mentions that detailed analysis is necessary when pages are few. In your own learning, do you give time and detail to short but dense texts, or do you rush through them? How can you change that?
?
The editor includes 'stating an opinion on presented ideas' as part of ta‘līq. When you learn from a source, do you passively accept or critically engage with its ideas? Challenge yourself to form a well-reasoned opinion on one idea from your last reading.

The Intellectual Climate of the 5th Century and Al-Nasafi's Methodology
Describes the intellectual climate and al-Nasafi's response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rich intellectual environment of the 5th century AH shaped al-Nasafi's approach in Tabsirat al-Adilla, balancing reason and revelation.

Core Idea
Al-Nasafi's work was forged in the crucible of a vibrant intellectual era.

This passage sets the stage for al-Nasafi's magnum opus. It describes a century teeming with theological, philosophical, and mystical thought. Al-Nasafi's own eager intellect engaged with these currents, producing a work that is both rigorous and balanced. As you read, note how he interacts with opponents and grounds his method in both reason and transmitted sources.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the descriptive language about the 5th century and al-Nasafi's mentality.
The passage uses a cause-effect structure: climate + intellect → depth in works.
2
Identify al-Nasafi's method
Find where the author describes what al-Nasafi does in Tabsirat al-Adilla. Identify the steps: presenting opponents' views, explaining them, responding to objections, then providing his own proofs.
Look for the sequence 'يعرض... يشرحها... يرد... يأتي عليها'
3
Reflect on the balance of 'aql and naql
Note the final sentence: al-Nasafi relies on reason (al-'aql) and seeks guidance from transmitted sources (al-naql). Consider how this dual reliance is a hallmark of his methodology.
This balance is a key feature of Maturidi kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شك أن نظرة متأنية في فكر من تعرضنا لذكر أسمائهم تعطينا دلالة واضحة على أن القرن الخامس الهجري كان حافلا بالعمق الكلامي، والفلسفي والتصوفي، فإذا أضفنا إلى ذلك عقلية ناضجة، متطلعة دائما، محبة للعلم نهمة في البحث عنه، وهي عقلية أبي المعين ميمون النسفي خرجنا بنتيجة مؤداها أن تعمق النسفي في مؤلفاته وخاصة تبصرة "الأدلة لم ينشأ من فراغ، وإنما نشأ من تصارع فكره مع أفكار من عاصرهم، ومن سبقوه، ويتضح هذا جليا في "تبصرة الأدلة" حيث يعرض بأمانة كل آراء المخالفين ويشرحها ثم يرد على ما يوجه إليها من اعتراضات، ثم يأتي عليها كدأبه مبينا أدلته في إظهار الحق، معتمدا على العقل، ومستهديا أولا وأخرا بالنقل من القرآن والسنة.
There is no doubt that a careful look at the thought of those whose names we have mentioned gives us a clear indication that the fifth century AH was replete with theological, philosophical, and mystical depth. If we add to that a mature mentality, always aspiring, loving knowledge, eager in its pursuit—and that is the mentality of Abu al-Mu'in Maymun al-Nasafi—we arrive at the conclusion that al-Nasafi's depth in his works, especially Tabsirat al-Adilla, did not arise from a vacuum, but rather arose from the clash of his thought with the ideas of his contemporaries and those who preceded him. This is clearly evident in Tabsirat al-Adilla, where he honestly presents all the opinions of his opponents, explains them, then responds to the objections directed at them, then proceeds in his usual manner, setting forth his proofs to manifest the truth, relying on reason (al-'aql) and seeking guidance first and last from transmitted sources (al-naql) from the Qur'an and Sunnah.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the intellectual environment of the 5th century AH and its direct impact on the work of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi. The author argues that the sheer depth of kalam, philosophy, and Sufism in that century, combined with al-Nasafi's own eager and mature intellect, produced his profound writings. He specifically points to Tabsirat al-Adilla as an example: in it, al-Nasafi carefully presents the views of his opponents, explains them, answers objections, and then systematically provides his own proofs. His methodology balances reason (al-'aql) and revelation (al-naql). The key takeaway is that al-Nasafi's work was not created in isolation but through active engagement with the intellectual currents of his time.

Key Terms
تبصرة الأدلة
العقل
النقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Nasafi's work was purely derivative or simply a compilation of earlier views.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that his depth arose from active engagement—'clash of his thought'—with contemporaries and predecessors, and he systematically presents, explains, responds, and then provides his own proofs, showing original synthesis.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does al-Nasafi do in Tabsirat al-Adilla?
A) He only quotes earlier scholars.
B) He presents opponents' views and then refutes them with his own proofs.
C) He avoids discussing rival opinions.
D) He relies exclusively on reason without transmitted sources.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla is a product of his engagement with the diverse intellectual currents of the 5th century, harmonizing reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nasafi's depth came from engaging with opposing ideas. Are you actively seeking out intellectual challenges to your faith, or do you avoid them because they feel uncomfortable? What specific belief have you subjected to genuine scrutiny recently? If not, what is holding you back?
?
Al-Nasafi relied on both reason and revelation. Is your own understanding of Islam built on both, or do you lean on one at the expense of the other? What step will you take this week to strengthen your rational engagement with revealed texts?
?
You read that al-Nasafi honestly presented opponents' views before refuting them. When you encounter a viewpoint that differs from your own beliefs, do you first try to understand it accurately, or do you dismiss it emotionally? How can you emulate his honesty in your own discussions?

Nasafi's Methodology in Tabsirat al-Adilla
Describes Nasafi's methodology of presenting and refuting opponents' views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical context that shaped Nasafi's thought and the three-step methodology he used in Tabsirat al-Adilla to engage with opposing views.

Core Idea
Nasafi’s methodology combined reason and revelation, presenting opponents’ views fairly before refuting them.

This lesson introduces you to the intellectual environment of the 5th century Hijri and the approach of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi in his seminal work Tabsirat al-Adilla. By understanding the richness of his time and his method of engaging with opponents, you will appreciate how classical Muslim theologians balanced reason and revelation in defending the truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage along with its literal translation. Pay attention to the description of the 5th century and the three actions Nasafi performs.
2
Identify the three methodological steps
The passage outlines three steps Nasafi takes when dealing with opponents' views. Find them and note how each builds on the previous.
The steps are: presenting views, responding to objections, then presenting his own proofs.
3
Reflect on the balance of reason and revelation
Notice that Nasafi relies on both reason (al-‘aql) and revelation (naql). Think about why both are necessary in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شك أن نظرة متأنية في فكر من تعرضنا لذكر أسمائهم تعطينا دلالة واضحة على أن القرن الخامس الهجري كان حافلا بالعمق الكلامي، والفلسفي والتصوفي، فإذا أضفنا إلى ذلك عقلية ناضجة، متطلعة دائما، محبة للعلم نهمة في البحث عنه، وهي عقلية أبي المعين ميمون النسفي خرجنا بنتيجة مؤداها أن تعمق النسفي في مؤلفاته وخاصة تبصرة الأدلة لم ينشأ من فراغ، وإنما نشأ من تصارع فكره مع أفكار من عاصرهم، ومن سبقوه، ويتضح هذا جليا في تبصرة الأدلة حيث يعرض بأمانة كل آراء المخالفين ويشرحها ثم يرد على ما يوجه إليها من اعتراضات، ثم يأتي عليها كدأبه مبينا أدلته في إظهار الحق، معتمدا على العقل، ومستهديا أولا وأخرا بالنقل من القرآن والسنة.
There is no doubt that a careful look at the thought of those whose names we have mentioned gives us a clear indication that the fifth century Hijri was full of depth in kalām, philosophy, and Sufism. If we add to that a mature intellect, always inquisitive, loving knowledge and eager in seeking it—which was the intellect of Abu al-Mu'in Maymun al-Nasafi—we arrive at the conclusion that Nasafi's depth in his works, especially Tabsirat al-Adilla, did not arise in a vacuum, but arose from the clash of his thought with the ideas of his contemporaries and those who preceded him. This is clearly evident in Tabsirat al-Adilla, where he faithfully presents all the views of the opponents, explains them, then responds to the objections directed against them, then proceeds in his usual manner, presenting his proofs in manifesting the truth, relying on reason, and being guided first and last by transmission from the Qur'an and Sunnah.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an interpretative description of the intellectual environment and Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi's methodological approach in Tabsirat al-Adilla. The author argues that the 5th century was rich in kalām, philosophy, and Sufism, providing a stimulating backdrop. Nasafi's sharp intellect grew in this environment, and his works, particularly Tabsirat al-Adilla, reflect a dialectical engagement with opponents. The passage highlights three methodological features: 1) He faithfully presents and explains opponents' views. 2) He responds to objections raised against them. 3) He then presents his own proofs, relying on both reason (al-‘aql) and revelation (naql from Qur'an and Sunnah). This combination defines his balanced approach.

Verifying the Attribution of Tabsirat al-Adilla
Argument for authenticity via cross-referencing al-Tamhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars verify the authenticity of a classical text through cross-referencing and internal evidence.

Core Idea
Cross-references within an author's own works can confirm authorship.

This passage demonstrates a key methodology in Islamic textual scholarship: verifying that a work attributed to an author is genuinely his. The author investigates whether Tabsirat al-Adilla is truly by al-Nasafi by examining al-Nasafi's other writings, especially al-Tamhid. This lesson walks you through his argument and how he counters the objection of possible forgery.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Get a sense of the overall argument.
2
Identify the problem and initial evidence
The passage starts by asking if Tabsirat is genuinely by al-Nasafi. The first piece of evidence is that al-Nasafi refers to Tabsirat in his other work, al-Tamhid.
Notice that this is internal evidence from the author's own corpus.
3
Understand the objection
The author raises a potential objection: a clever forger might have inserted these references. Why is this a valid concern?
4
Examine the counter-argument
The author uses a comparative analysis of the theological discussions in both books, showing a consistent 'spirit' and that al-Tamhid is a summary of Tabsirat. This counters the forgery hypothesis.
This is a stronger argument because it relies on substantive content, not just references.
5
Reflect on the method
Think about why this combination of cross-referencing and textual comparison is powerful for authenticity verification.
This method is widely used in Islamic textual criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التحقق من نسبة التبصرة إلى أبي المعين:
بدايةً يكون السؤال الذي يطرح هل حقيقةً خلَّف أبو المعين ميمون النسفي مؤلفا باسم تبصرة الأدلة أو التبصرة؟
وللإجابة أخذنا جادين ننقب فيما وقع تحت أيدينا من مؤلفات النسفي، وغيره، وفي كتب التراجم وفهارس المكتبات، وقد توصلنا بالاستقراء إلى ما يلي: - :أولا تعود النسفي في بعض مسائله أن يحيل إلى كتاب أو أكثر من كتبه ولهذا وجدناه يُحِسُّ بالإيجاز في كتابه التمهيد، فيقول في أكثر من موضوع: إن هذه المسألة فيها كلام كثير ودلائل جمة ذكرت بعضها في كتاب تبصرة الأدلة (۱). لكن التداعي يواجهنا بسؤال وهو : ما المخرج؟ ألا يحتمل أن يكون هناك ناحل ناجح هو الذي قال ذلك فأوقعنا في الخطأ.
ولكي نجيب ليس أمامنا إلا استقراء المباحث الكلامية التي ذكرت في كلا الكتابين، ولقد قمت بذلك فاتضح لي أن روح – النسفي وراء كل مبحث بل كل جملة في كلا الكتابين، ووجدت أن كتاب التمهيد ما هو إلا تلخيص لبعض مباحث التبصرة قام به النسفي استجابة لمطلب كما يقول - فقد طلب مني من أزمع ارتقائه إلى أسني درجة الإمارة، والسياسة أن أكتب له عقيدة من سلف من مشائخ أهل السنة والجماعة وأبين ما كانوا عليه من المذاهب في علم التوحيد... فرأيت الأصوب في
(۱) راجع التمهيد للنسفي مخطوط رقم ۲۲۹۹۱ علم كلام بدار الكتب لوحة ١٣ و، 15 ظ، ١٦ ظ، ۳۲ ظ، ۳۳و، ظ.
Verification of the attribution of al-Tabsira to Abū al-Muʿīn:
At first, the question arises: Did Abū al-Muʿīn Maymūn al-Nasafī truly leave a work named Tabsirat al-Adilla or al-Tabsira?
To answer, we earnestly investigated what came into our hands of the writings of al-Nasafī and others, and in biographical works and library catalogs, and we concluded through induction to the following: First, al-Nasafī is accustomed in some of his issues to refer to one or more of his books, and for this reason we find him feeling brevity in his book al-Tamhīd, saying on more than one topic: 'This issue has much discussion and numerous proofs, some of which I have mentioned in the book Tabsirat al-Adilla (1).' But objection faces us with a question: What is the way out? Could it be that there is a successful forger who said that and thus led us into error?
To answer, we have no choice but to inductively examine the theological discussions mentioned in both books, and I did that, and it became clear to me that the spirit of al-Nasafī lies behind every discussion, indeed every sentence in both books. And I found that the book al-Tamhīd is nothing but a summary of some discussions of al-Tabsira, composed by al-Nasafī in response to a request, as he says: 'One who resolved to ascend to the highest rank of emirate and politics asked me to write for him a creed from the predecessors among the scholars of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʿa and to explain what doctrinal positions they held in the science of tawḥīd... So I saw the most correct thing is to...
(1) See al-Tamhīd by al-Nasafī, manuscript number 22991, ʿIlm Kalām at Dār al-Kutub, folios 13a, 15b, 16b, 32b, 33a-b.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a methodological argument defending the authenticity of Tabsirat al-Adilla as a genuine work of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi. The author begins by posing the central question: did al-Nasafi actually author a book called Tabsirat al-Adilla? He then describes his investigative method: examining al-Nasafi's other writings, biographical works, and library catalogs. The key evidence is that al-Nasafi, in his book al-Tamhid, frequently refers to a work called Tabsirat al-Adilla, saying he discussed certain proofs there in more detail. This cross-reference is strong internal evidence. However, the author anticipates an objection: could a clever forger have inserted these references to deceive? To counter this, he employs a comparative textual analysis ('induction of theological discussions') of both al-Tamhid and Tabsirat. He claims that the stylistic and doctrinal consistency between the two books is so thorough that the same authorial 'spirit' pervades every discussion and even every sentence. He further notes that al-Tamhid is explicitly a summary of Tabsirat, written at the request of a political figure. The passage concludes mid-sentence with 'I saw the most correct thing is to...', followed by a footnote citing the manuscript of al-Tamhid used as evidence.

Key Terms
التحقق من نسبة التبصرة
التمهيد
الاستقراء
ناحل ناجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that cross-references alone are insufficient and that a forger could easily fabricate them.
Correction: The author shows that internal consistency in content and structure (al-Tamhid as a summary) provides stronger evidence that the works share a single author.

Checkpoint
What is the main method used by the author to confirm that Tabsirat al-Adilla is genuinely by al-Nasafi?
A) Examining external manuscript catalogues
B) Comparing the theological discussions in both al-Tamhid and Tabsirat
C) Relying on the testimony of later biographers
D) Proving that the work was written for a political patron

Key Takeaway
Cross-referencing within an author's corpus, combined with comparative textual analysis, can effectively authenticate a work.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author invested significant effort to verify the authenticity of a single book. When you read Islamic texts, do you ever question their authorship or assess the strength of the chain of transmission? How much trust do you put in a text without such investigation?

?
The author used critical thinking to overcome a potential forgery objection. In your own learning, are you willing to challenge assumptions and follow the evidence, even if it leads to discomfort or a need to revise long-held views? The Position of Tabsirat Among Nasafi's Works
The author explains that three other works by Nasafi were written before Tabsirat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Tabsirat al-Adilla is a later comprehensive work that builds upon three earlier specialized treatises by Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Tabsirat is not Nasafi's first work—three earlier books paved its way.

This lesson clarifies the chronological and thematic position of Tabsirat within the corpus of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi. By examining references within Tabsirat itself, we discover that Nasafi wrote three specialized works before composing this comprehensive kalam treatise. Understanding this helps us appreciate Tabsirat as a mature synthesis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to identify the three earlier works mentioned.
Notice that the passage includes a footnote where Nasafi himself confirms one of them.
2
Understand the significance
Reflect on why listing earlier works matters for studying Tabsirat: it shows the author's development and the scope of his earlier writings.
3
Apply the insight
Consider how knowing the earlier works can help you contextualize arguments in Tabsirat, especially those referencing previous refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مكانة التبصرة بين كتب النسفي: إذا ما حاولنا بعد ذلك أن نبين ترتيب التبصرة بين مؤلفات النسفي الأخرى. وجدنا أنفسنا أمام ثلاثة كتب يفردها النسفي بمسائل محددة ويحيل إليها في كتاب التبصرة وهي : كتاب الإفساد لخدع أهل الإلحاد، وكتاب تصيد القواعد في علم الكلام، وكتاب إيضاح المحجة، ويعني هذا أن الكتب المذكورة صنفت قبل التبصرة (٤). (٤) يقول أبو المعين في التبصرة ص ۸۸٥ : وقد بينا بطلان القول بعصمة الإمام في كتابنا المصنف في الرد على الباطنية المترجم كتاب الإفساد لخدع أهل الإلحاد.
The place of al-Tabsira among the books of al-Nasafi: If we then try to explain the order of al-Tabsira among the other works of al-Nasafi, we find ourselves faced with three books that al-Nasafi devotes to specific issues and to which he refers in the book al-Tabsira, namely: Kitab al-Ifsad li-Khud' Ahl al-Ilhad, and Kitab Tasayyud al-Qawa'id fi 'Ilm al-Kalam, and Kitab Idah al-Mahajja. And this means that the mentioned books were authored before al-Tabsira (4). (4) Abu al-Mu'in says in al-Tabsira, p. 885: 'We have already explained the invalidity of the claim of the infallibility of the imam in our book authored in refutation of the Batiniyya, entitled Kitab al-Ifsad li-Khud' Ahl al-Ilhad.'
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage discusses the position of Tabsirat among the works of Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi. The author (likely a later scholar) explains that Nasafi wrote three other books on specific topics: one refuting the Batiniyya (al-Ifsad), another on the rules of kalam (Tasayyud al-Qawa'id), and a third called Idah al-Mahajja. By referring to these books in Tabsirat, it indicates that these three were written before Tabsirat. The footnote provides a direct quote from Nasafi himself in Tabsirat, confirming that he had already refuted the claim of imam's infallibility in his earlier work al-Ifsad. Thus Tabsirat is a later, comprehensive work that builds upon these earlier specialized treatises.

The Place of Tabsirat Among Nasafi's Works
Classifies Nasafi's works around Tabsirat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Tabsirat al-Adilla stands as the central encyclopedia among Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi's theological writings, with other works serving either as earlier specialized treatises or later abridgments.

Core Idea
Tabsirat al-Adilla is the master work; other books are either earlier references or later summaries.

This passage from the introduction to Tabsirat al-Adilla clarifies the book's position within Nasafi's oeuvre. By identifying which works he wrote before and after this encyclopedia, we gain insight into his scholarly development and the comprehensive nature of Tabsirat. Let us examine how Nasafi himself classified these writings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the references to specific book titles.
2
Identify the earlier works
Find the three books that Nasafi wrote before Tabsirat, which he refers to in Tabsirat.
They are Kitab al-Ifsad, Tasayyud al-Qawa'id, and Idah al-Mahajja.
3
Identify the later abridgments
Notice al-Tamhid and Bahr al-Kalam, which are likely summaries written after Tabsirat.
Nasafi often cross-references Tabsirat in these.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage concludes that Tabsirat is the most reliable, comprehensive, and voluminous of Nasafi's kalam works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ما حاولنا بعد ذلك أن نبين ترتيب التبصرة بين مؤلفات النسفي الأخرى. وجدنا أنفسنا أمام ثلاثة كتب يفردها النسفي بمسائل محددة ويحيل إليها في كتاب التبصرة وهي : كتاب الإفساد لخدع أهل الإلحاد، وكتاب تصيد القواعد في علم الكلام، وكتاب إيضاح المحجة، ويعني هذا أن الكتب المذكورة صنفت قبل التبصرة. أما التمهيد، وبحر الكلام فيبدو للمتأمل فيهما أنهما مختصران لبعض المسائل التي تحدث عنها النسفي في موسوعته "التبصرة"، ومن المرجح أنهما كتبا بعد التبصرة، لإفهام الناس عقيدة أهل السنة والجماعة باختصار، ومما يؤكد ذلك أن النسفي في التمهيد كثيرا ما كان يحيل إلى التبصرة كما أشرنا قبل ذلك . وكذا في بحر الكلام، ويظهر للمتأمل فيه أنه مختصر لبعض مسائل التبصرة. أما باقي الكتب المنسوبة إلى أبي المعين التي سبق الإشارة إليها فهي في الفروع، ولسنا في حاجة إلى وضعها في ترتيب خاص بالنسبة للتبصرة. ونخلص من هذا أن تبصرة الأدلة من أوثق الكتب التي كتبها أبو المعين النسفي، وأشملها للمباحث الكلامية وأضخمها حجما. أما باقي كتبه فهي تلخيصات لبعض ما تناوله كتاب التبصرة.
If we then attempt to clarify the order of al-Tabsira among al-Nasafi's other works, we find ourselves before three books which al-Nasafi devotes to specific issues and refers to in his book al-Tabsira: they are: Kitab al-Ifsad li-Khuda' Ahl al-Ilhad, Kitab Tasayyud al-Qawa'id fi 'Ilm al-Kalam, and Kitab Idah al-Mahajja. This means that the mentioned books were authored before al-Tabsira. As for al-Tamhid and Bahr al-Kalam, to one who reflects on them they appear to be abridgments of some issues which al-Nasafi discussed in his encyclopedia al-Tabsira, and it is likely that they were written after al-Tabsira, to make the creed of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a understood by people concisely. And what confirms that is that al-Nasafi in al-Tamhid frequently refers to al-Tabsira, as we have indicated before. And similarly in Bahr al-Kalam, and it appears to one who reflects that it is an abridgment of some issues of al-Tabsira. As for the remaining books attributed to Abu al-Mu'in which were previously mentioned, they are in furu' (positive law), and we have no need to place them in a particular order relative to al-Tabsira. And we conclude from this that Tabsirat al-Adilla is among the most reliable books written by Abu al-Mu'in al-Nasafi, the most comprehensive in kalam topics, and the largest in volume. As for his other books, they are summaries of some of what the book al-Tabsira covered.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage determines the chronological and thematic relationship between Tabsirat al-Adilla and Nasafi's other works. It begins by stating that three books—Kitab al-Ifsad li-Khuda' Ahl al-Ilhad, Kitab Tasayyud al-Qawa'id, and Kitab Idah al-Mahajja—are earlier because Nasafi refers to them in Tabsirat. Then it notes that al-Tamhid and Bahr al-Kalam appear to be abridgments written later to disseminate the creed concisely, and evidence is that Nasafi cross-references Tabsirat in al-Tamhid. Other books are in furu' and not directly relevant. The conclusion is that Tabsirat is the most reliable, comprehensive, and voluminous of Nasafi's kalam works, while his other books are summaries of its content. Thus, Tabsirat is the central encyclopedic work.

التبصرة
الإفساد
تصيد القواعد
إيضاح المحجة
التمهيد
بحر الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All of Nasafi's theological works are equally comprehensive and independent.
Correction: In fact, Tabsirat is the central encyclopedia, and his other works are either specialized treatises written earlier or concise abridgments written later, as this passage explains.

Key Takeaway
Tabsirat al-Adilla is the foundational encyclopedia of Nasafi's kalam, with earlier works feeding into it and later works summarizing it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that al-Tabsira is the most comprehensive and reliable work, while other books are just summaries. In your own pursuit of knowledge, do you tend to rely on summaries and abridgments, or do you seek out the original, deeper sources? What excuses do you make for choosing the easier path?
?
Nasafi wrote summaries after his encyclopedia to make the creed 'understood by people concisely'. But what about you—when you learn something deeply, do you take the effort to convey it clearly to others, or do you keep it to yourself? Whom have you neglected to teach?
?
The passage shows a deliberate order: earlier works feed into the major work, and later works distill it. Yet you may have been content with scattered, unordered bits of knowledge. Is your Islamic learning a coherent, building structure, or a pile of disconnected facts? What will you do today to start building?

The Honesty Behind the Title 'Tabsirat al-Adilla'
Explains why the book is named Tabsirat al-Adilla and highlights its comprehensive value.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Nasafi named his work 'Tabsirat al-Adilla' and how the title reflects the book's inclusive and clarifying purpose.

Core Idea
A book's name should be truthful to its content, and 'Tabsirat al-Adilla' is exactly that.

Every scholarly work begins with its title, and the title of a book often reveals its author's intention. In this passage, we learn about the title 'Tabsirat al-Adilla' and why it was chosen. Al-Nasafi wanted to produce a work that not only presents Maturidi thought but also clarifies the proofs of all those who defended Islam. This lesson explores the meaning of the title and the honesty behind its selection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation to get a clear sense of the author's argument.
Notice how the author emphasizes the principle of honest naming.
2
Understand the principle of honest naming
The passage states that an author must choose a name that matches the content. Reflect on why this is important for scholarly works.
3
Examine the lexical meanings
The footnote provides the linguistic breakdown of 'tabsira' and 'adilla'. Understand how these meanings support the title's significance.
The root 'b-s-r' relates to insight and clarity.
4
Connect the title to the book's scope
The book is described as a huge kalam encyclopedia that goes beyond Maturidi thought. Consider how the title 'Clarification of Proofs' fits this inclusive approach.
This corrects a possible misunderstanding that the book is only about Maturidism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة الأدلة تسميته وقيمته معلوم أن مؤلف أي كتاب في أي علم من العلوم لكي يكون صادقا مع نفسه ومع من يكتب لهم، عليه أن يتخير لمؤلفه الاسم الذي يطابق المسمى. وبهذا المفهوم كان النسفي صادقا مع نفسه، ومع من كتب لهم حينما سمى تبصرة الأدلة - فالكتاب موسوعة كلامية ضخمة لا تمثل فكر الماتريدية فحسب، بل تقدم بأمانة وصدق الكثير من فكر وأدلة كل الذين شغلوا أنفسهم بالدفاع (۳) في القاموس بصر بمعنى علم، والتبصر بمعنى التأمل والتعرف، والتبصير والتبصرة بمعنى التعريف والإيضاح، والأدلة جمع دليل، والدليل هو الهادي والمرشد والكاشف راجع لسان العرب ومختار الصحاح والمصباح المنير.
Tabsirat al-Adilla: its naming and its value. It is known that the author of any book in any field of knowledge, in order to be honest with himself and with those for whom he writes, must choose for his book the name that corresponds to the named. With this concept, al-Nasafi was honest with himself and with those for whom he wrote when he named it Tabsirat al-Adilla. For the book is a huge kalam encyclopedia that does not represent the thought of the Maturidis only, but honestly and faithfully presents much of the thought and proofs of all those who occupied themselves with defense... (3) In al-Qamus: 'Basar' means knowledge, 'al-tabassur' means reflection and recognition, 'al-tabsir' and 'al-tabsira' mean definition and clarification, and 'al-adilla' is the plural of 'dalil', and 'al-dalil' is the guide, the indicator, and the revealer. See Lisan al-Arab, Mukhtar al-Sihah, and al-Misbah al-Munir.
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage discusses the significance of the book's title, 'Tabsirat al-Adilla.' It argues that an author should be honest by choosing a name that corresponds to the reality of the book. Nasafi was honest because his book is a huge encyclopedia covering proofs and ideas not only of the Maturidis but of all who defended the faith. The footnote provides the lexical meaning: 'tabsira' comes from 'basar' (knowledge) and means clarification, while 'adilla' is the plural of 'dalil' (proof, guide). So the title means 'Clarification of Proofs' or 'Illumination of Evidences,' reflecting the book's comprehensive, non-sectarian, and clear treatment of theological arguments.

Misconception: The title 'Tabsirat al-Adilla' suggests the book only covers Maturidi thought.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the book is a huge kalam encyclopedia that presents not only Maturidi thought but also the ideas and proofs of all those who occupied themselves with defending Islam.

B) It only represents Maturidi thought

Al-Nasafi's Method of Fair Refutation
Description of al-Nasafi's method of argument presentation and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's distinctive method of first elaborating his opponents' arguments in their own terms and then refuting them with reason and revelation.

Core Idea
Al-Nasafi refutes by first mastering his opponent's own argument.

This passage from al-Nasafi's Tabṣirat al-Adilla reveals his unique scholarly method. Unlike some polemicists, he does not dismiss opponents' views without engagement. Instead, he carefully presents their arguments as if he were one of them, then delivers a powerful refutation. This approach exemplifies the high standard of fair debate in classical Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core method
Read how al-Nasafi explains the opponent's argument using their own language and thought. He does not distort or simplify.
He is compared to al-Ghazali in this approach.
2
Identify the examples given
Notice the three groups: Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, and Khawarij. For each, he presents their view so convincingly that a reader might think he belongs to that group.
This builds trust with the reader and the opponent.
3
Spot the turning point
After extensive exposition, when the reader expects al-Nasafi to accept the opponent's position, he instead launches his refutation. Identify the phrase 'فإذا ما حسبته قد أسلم القياد لهم'.
This rhetorical surprise emphasizes the strength of his counter-argument.
4
Observe the tools of refutation
He uses both 'aql (reason) and naql (tradition/revelation). He also employs the full range of kalam methodologies.
He presents his own proofs and responds to all objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس هذا فحسب بل يشرح الدليل بفكر ولسان خصمه كما فعل معاصره الغزالي فتحسبه معتزليا في عرض أقوال المعتزلة، أشعريا كذلك مع الأشاعرة، خارجيا كذلك مع الخوارج، ويبحث ويوضح كل الاعتراضات التي يمكن أن توجه لأدلتهم، ويرد عليها بلسانهم، فإذا ما حسبته قد أسلم القياد لهم، انبري ليقدم صورة نيرة ومشرقة للفكر الإسلامي، هادما أدلة خصمه بالعقل والنقل، مستخدما جل مناهج المشتغلين بعلم الكلام، مبينا أدلته، شارحا لها، ومظهرا لكل الاعتراضات والنقوض التي توجه إليها ثم رادا بالعقل والنقل على أي اعتراض .
Not only this, but he explains the argument with the thought and tongue of his opponent, just as his contemporary al-Ghazali did, so that you would think he is a Mu'tazili when presenting the statements of the Mu'tazila, an Ash'ari likewise with the Ash'aris, a Khariji likewise with the Khawarij. He examines and clarifies all objections that can be directed against their arguments, and responds to them in their own tongue. Then, when you think he has surrendered to them, he leaps forward to present a bright and radiant picture of Islamic thought, demolishing his opponent's arguments by reason and tradition (al-'aql wa al-naql), using most of the methodologies of those engaged in the science of kalam, presenting his own arguments, explaining them, and showing all objections and refutations directed against them, then replying by reason and tradition to any objection.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage describes al-Nasafi's distinctive method. It begins by stating that he not only presents opponents' arguments fairly but explains them using the opponent's own intellectual framework and language, similar to al-Ghazali. For example, when citing Mu'tazila, one would think he is a Mu'tazili; with Ash'aris, an Ash'ari; with Khawarij, a Khariji. He then examines all possible objections to their arguments and responds using their own terms. This builds credibility. After this thorough exposition, when the reader might think al-Nasafi has accepted the opponent's position, he abruptly shifts: he presents a clear and bright picture of Islamic thought, demolishing the opponent's arguments using both reason ('aql) and transmitted revelation (naql). He employs the full range of kalam methodologies, laying out his own proofs, explaining them, and showing all objections and critiques that could be directed at them, and then he rebuts any objection using reason and revelation. This demonstrates his mastery and fairness.

Key Terms
الغزالي
العقل
النقل
علم الكلام
الاعتراضات
النقوض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think al-Nasafi merely repeats opponents' views without criticism, but he fully refutes them after explanation.
Correction: The passage shows that al-Nasafi first presents the opponent's argument in their own terms, then demolishes it using both reason and tradition.

Checkpoint
What does al-Nasafi do after presenting the opponent's argument according to this passage?
A) He immediately refutes it using only reason.
B) He refutes it after presenting it in the opponent's own terms, using both reason and tradition.
C) He accepts the opponent's view as correct.
D) He relies solely on the authority of earlier scholars.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's method is to present opponents' arguments so fairly that one thinks he agrees, then to refute them with both reason and tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken the time to truly understand an opposing viewpoint so well that you could articulate it better than its proponents? If not, what is stopping you from doing that for a belief you disagree with?
?
When you argue for your faith, do you rely on both reason and transmitted knowledge, or do you lean on one to the exclusion of the other? Which one are you neglecting, and how will you restore the balance starting today?
?
Are you willing to risk appearing as though you agree with falsehood in order to be fair to your opponent, or does your ego demand that you immediately assert your own position? Ask yourself: is my primary goal truth, or victory?

Nasafi's Methodology in Presenting and Refuting Opponents
Describes Nasafi's method of fully quoting opponents and then refuting them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Nasafi's distinctive method of presenting opponents' arguments fully and then refuting them with reason and tradition.

Core Idea
Nasafi defeats opponents by first mastering their own arguments.

This passage describes a key feature of Nasafi's approach in his work Tabsirat al-Adilla. Unlike some polemicists, he does not truncate or misrepresent his opponents' proofs. Instead, he presents them as they appear in their own books, sometimes verbatim, and even explains them from the opponent's perspective. This fair presentation sets the stage for a powerful refutation. As you read, notice how Nasafi's method combines intellectual honesty with rigorous critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own description of Nasafi's method come through.
Pay attention to the verbs describing Nasafi's actions.
2
Identify Nasafi's two-phase method
The passage describes two phases: first, full presentation of the opponent's proof; second, refutation. Find where each phase begins.
3
Notice the use of opponent's own language
Nasafi explains the proof 'with the thought and tongue of his opponent,' even responding to objections from their perspective. This shows his deep understanding.
Compare this to al-Ghazali's similar approach.
4
Reflect on the title 'Tabsirat al-Adilla'
The passage suggests the title means 'The Clarification of Proofs' – Nasafi tests proofs to reveal truth and expose weakness. Ask yourself how this method achieves that.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنسفي حينما يقدم أدلة من يسميهم خصوما لا يحاول أبدا أن يبتر الدليل بل يلزم نفسه في كثير من الأحايين بعرض دليل خصمه كما هو موجود في كتبهم ملتزما الحرفية أحيانا في النقل والمعنى أحيانا أخرى. ليس هذا فحسب بل يشرح الدليل بفكر ولسان خصمه كما فعل معاصره الغزالي فتحسبه معتزليا في عرض أقوال المعتزلة، أشعريا كذلك مع الأشاعرة، خارجيا كذلك مع الخوارج، ويبحث ويوضح كل الاعتراضات التي يمكن أن توجه لأدلتهم، ويرد عليها بلسانهم، فإذا ما حسبته قد أسلم القياد لهم ، انبري ليقدم صورة نيرة ومشرقة للفكر الإسلامي، هادما أدلة خصمه بالعقل والنقل، مستخدما جل مناهج المشتغلين بعلم الكلام، مبينا أدلته، شارحا لها، ومظهرا لكل الاعتراضات والنقوض التي توجه إليها ثم رادا بالعقل والنقل على أي اعتراض . النسفي إذا يقدم الأدلة التي ترشد وتكشف، ترشد إلى الحقيقة، وتكشف عن جوانب الوهاء في أدلة الخصوم، وهو في هذا الجانب لا ينطلق من فراغ، وإنما ينطلق من علم شامل وكامل بما يقوله ،خصمه معتمدا على عقيدة راسخة، وخلفية ثقافية متينة. ولعل هذا ما عناه النسفي بتسميته لكتابه – تبصرة الأدلة فهو عن تأمل وروية وعلم، يختبر الأدلة ليكشف عن الحقيقة، ويهدي إلى الصواب، وليوضح ما يرتكز عليه أهل السنة والجماعة، وخاصة الماتريدية التي ينتسب إليها.
And al-Nasafi, when he presents the proofs of those he calls opponents, never attempts to truncate the proof, but rather obliges himself in many cases to present his opponent's proof as it exists in their books, committing sometimes to literalness in transmission and sometimes to meaning. Not only that, but he explains the proof with the thought and tongue of his opponent, as did his contemporary al-Ghazali, so you would think him a Mu'tazili in presenting the statements of the Mu'tazila, an Ash'ari likewise with the Ash'aris, a Khariji likewise with the Khawarij, and he investigates and clarifies all the objections that can be directed against their proofs, and responds to them with their own tongue. Then, when you would think he has surrendered the reins to them, he springs forth to present a luminous and bright picture of Islamic thought, demolishing his opponent's proofs by reason and tradition, employing most of the methods of those engaged in the science of kalam, clarifying his own proofs, explaining them, and showing all the objections and refutations directed against them, then responding by reason and tradition to any objection. Al-Nasafi thus presents proofs that guide and reveal: they guide to truth and reveal aspects of weakness in the opponents' proofs. In this respect, he does not proceed from a vacuum, but proceeds from comprehensive and complete knowledge of what his opponent says, relying on a firm creed and a solid cultural background. Perhaps this is what al-Nasafi meant by naming his book 'Tabsirat al-Adilla' (The Clarification of Proofs): it is based on contemplation, deliberation, and knowledge; he tests proofs to reveal truth, guide to correctness, and clarify what the Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, especially the Maturidis to whom he belongs, rely upon.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a description of Imam al-Nasafi's methodology in his work 'Tabsirat al-Adilla'. The author of this introduction explains how al-Nasafi deals with the arguments of his opponents, such as the Ash'aris, Mu'tazilis, Khawarij, etc. First, he notes that al-Nasafi never truncates an opponent's proof; instead, he presents it exactly as found in their own books, sometimes verbatim and sometimes in meaning. Moreover, al-Nasafi explains the proof from the opponent's own perspective, using their own thought language, much like al-Ghazali did. He even investigates and clarifies all possible objections that could be raised against the opponent's proofs and responds to them in the opponent's own terms. After seeming to surrender to them, he then turns to demolish their arguments using both reason and transmitted evidence, employing the methods of kalam scholars. He then presents his own proofs and defends them against objections. The passage concludes that al-Nasafi's approach is not from ignorance but from comprehensive knowledge and a firm creed, and that the title 'Tabsirat al-Adilla' reflects his goal: through contemplation and knowledge, he tests proofs to reveal truth and guide to correctness, clarifying the foundations of Ahl al-Sunna and especially the Maturidi school to which he belongs.

Key Terms
تبصرة الأدلة
النسفي
خصوم
دليل
اعتراضات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nasafi distorts opponents' arguments to make them easier to refute.
Correction: Actually, he presents their proofs fully as they exist in their own books, often verbatim, and even explains them from their perspective before refuting.

Checkpoint

A) He truncates them to make them easier to refute.
B) He presents them fully as they exist in the opponents' books, sometimes verbatim.
C) He ignores them and only presents his own proofs.
D) He summarizes them briefly without detail.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's methodology combines thorough presentation of opponents' arguments with reasoned refutation, ensuring fairness and intellectual rigor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone whose beliefs differ sharply from yours, do you first seek to understand their argument as thoroughly as Nasafi did? Or do you rush to refute what you assume they believe? What would it cost you to adopt his method of full presentation before critique?
?
Nasafi risked appearing as if he had 'surrendered the reins' to his opponents by explaining their arguments from their own perspective. Is your commitment to truth strong enough that you would risk being misunderstood or misjudged in order to fully grasp an opposing view before responding?
?
The passage says Nasafi proceeds from 'comprehensive and complete knowledge of what his opponent says, relying on a firm creed and a solid cultural background.' How firm is your own creed, and how solid is your knowledge of what you believe and what others believe? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen either?

Editor's Manuscript Ranking and Symbols
Editor describes his ranking of four main manuscripts and the symbols assigned to each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's methodology for ranking manuscripts and the symbols he assigned to each.

Core Idea
Editorial choices in manuscript ranking shape the reliability of a critical edition.

This passage reveals the careful process behind a critical edition. The editor explains how he evaluated available manuscripts by age and accuracy, assigned symbols to the four primary copies, and determined which were secondary. Understanding this methodology helps you appreciate the textual foundation of the work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the editor's clear ranking order and the symbols he uses.
The passage is a methodological statement, not theological content.
2
Identify the four primary manuscripts
List the four primary manuscripts: Alexandria (B), Tal'at (T), Dar al-Kutub (D), and first Azhar (Z). Note the reasons for their ranking.
The editor emphasizes age and correctness as criteria.
3
Understand why some copies are secondary
Consider the two secondary copies (10m and 4208). Why did the editor treat them as secondary? Reasons include modern copying and strong similarity to primary copies.
This shows the editor's critical evaluation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما كانت المخطوطة الموجودة ببلدية الإسكندرية والتي قام المعهد بتصويرها أقدم المخطوطات التي وقعت تحت يدي تاريخيا، وأصحها، فقد اعتبرتها أصلا ورمزت لها بالحرف "ب"، يليها في الترتيب التاريخي والصحة نسخة طلعت وقد رمزت لها بالحرف "ط"، فنسخة دار الكتب المجهول تاريخ نسخها، والتي هي قريبة الشبه بالأصل وقد رمزت لها بالحرف "د"، فنسخة الأزهر الأولى، وقد رمزت لها بالحرف "ز".
أما النسختان ۱۰م بدار الكتب، ٤۲۰۸ بالمكتبة الأزهرية فقد كان رجوعي إليهما للتأكد والاستيضاح، ولهذا فقد اعتبرتهما نسختين ثانويتين، وقد دفعني لذلك حداثة نسخهما ،أولا ، وثانيا الشبه القوي بين النسخة ط و ۱۰م، ومن ناحية أخرى فقد نص ناسخ النسخة الثانية بالأزهر على نقلها من النسخة ٣٠١ بالأزهر، وعلى هذا فإن النسخ التي اعتمدت عليها اعتمادا كاملا هي ب، ط، د، ز.
Since the manuscript found in the Alexandria Municipality, which the institute photographed, was the oldest of the manuscripts that came into my hands historically and the most correct, I considered it the original and designated it with the letter "B". It is followed in historical order and correctness by the Tal'at copy, which I designated with the letter "T", then the Dar al-Kutub copy, whose date of copying is unknown, which is closely similar to the original, and I designated it with the letter "D", then the first Azhar copy, and I designated it with the letter "Z". As for the two copies: 10m in Dar al-Kutub and 4208 in the Azhar Library, I only referred to them for confirmation and clarification, and for this reason I considered them secondary copies. I was motivated to do so by the modernity of their copying, firstly, and secondly the strong similarity between copy T and 10m. On the other hand, the scribe of the second Azhar copy stated that he transmitted from copy 301 at al-Azhar. Based on this, the copies I fully relied upon are B, T, D, Z.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement by the editor explaining his ranking and codification of the manuscripts used for the critical edition. He begins by stating that the Alexandria manuscript, which the institute photographed, is the oldest and most correct among those available to him. Therefore, he made it the original (أصلاً) and designated it with the letter "B". He then ranks the others in order: the Tal'at copy ("T") next in age and correctness, followed by a Dar al-Kutub copy ("D") of unknown date but close to the original, and finally the first Azhar copy ("Z"). He mentions two additional copies (10m and 4208) which he treated as secondary due to their newer copying and the strong similarity between T and 10m. Additionally, the scribe of the second Azhar copy explicitly stated it was transmitted from another Azhar copy, so these were not primary sources. The editor concludes that the four copies he fully relied upon are B, T, D, and Z.

Key Terms
مخطوطة
أصل
نسخة
رمز
ترتيب تاريخي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The most recent manuscript is the most reliable for a critical edition.
Correction: Editors prioritize older manuscripts because they are closer to the original, as shown here where the oldest manuscript (Alexandria) is deemed most correct.

Checkpoint
Which manuscript did the editor consider the original and why?
A) The Tal'at copy because it was the oldest.
B) The Alexandria manuscript because it was the oldest and most correct.
C) The Dar al-Kutub copy because it was closely similar to the original.
D) The first Azhar copy because it was the most recent.

Key Takeaway
The editor ranked manuscripts by age and correctness, using symbols B, T, D, Z for primary sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The editor carefully chose his sources based on age and correctness. When you seek Islamic knowledge, do you evaluate the reliability of your sources as diligently? Name one source you commonly rely on and ask yourself: why do I trust it?
?
The editor used multiple manuscripts to cross-check. In your own learning, do you seek multiple perspectives or stick to a single teacher or book? What might you be missing by not consulting others?
?
The editor set aside modern copies as secondary. Are there modern interpretations or opinions you hold that might be less reliable than earlier ones? How can you verify that your understanding aligns with the earliest and most authentic sources?

The Editor's Approach to Manuscript Indexes
Editor explains his treatment of manuscript indexes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the editor chose to omit scribe-added indexes and begin the critical edition directly with Nasafi's original text.

Core Idea
Editorial decisions shape how we encounter classical texts.

In this brief but important passage, the editor of Tabsirat al-Adilla explains a key methodological choice. He decided to ignore the indexes added by scribes in later copies and adhere strictly to the format of the oldest manuscript. This lesson examines that decision and what it tells us about critical editing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice how the editor describes the different copies.
The passage is short, so take your time with each word.
2
Identify the two types of manuscripts
Look for the description of the original manuscript 'B' versus copies 'T' and 'Z'. What is the key difference?
Original manuscript B had no indexes; copies T and Z had indexes written by scribes.
3
Understand the editor's rationale
Why did the editor treat the indexes as outside the manuscript? Consider the principle of following the oldest and most authentic source.
This reflects a common editorial practice in textual criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لما كان الأصل "ب" عبارة عن مجموعة من اللوحات بدأت بكلام النسفي مباشرة ولم يوضع بها فهارس وكذلك كانت نهايتها بينما زودت النسختان ط، ز بفهارس من وضع الناسخين، فقد اعتبرت الفهارس خارجة عن المخطوط وابتدأت في الرمز بمثل ما ابتدأ النسفي في النسخة الأصلية، كما هو مبين في التحقيق.
Since the original "B" was a set of folios that began directly with Nasafi's words and no indexes were placed in it, and likewise its end, while the two copies T and Z were provided with indexes made by the scribes, I considered the indexes to be outside the manuscript and began the codification exactly as Nasafi began in the original copy, as is shown in the critical edition.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage explains the editor's approach to presenting the text. The original manuscript "B" consisted of folios that started immediately with Nasafi's words, without any tables of contents or indexes at the beginning or end. In contrast, copies T and Z had indexes added by their respective scribes. The editor decided to treat those indexes as extraneous to the manuscript itself and not part of the original work. Therefore, he chose to begin the printed edition in the same manner as the original manuscript, starting with Nasafi's own words, and he indicates that this is evident in the critical apparatus.

Scribal Practices and Issues in Manuscript B
Editor enumerates scribal habits and manuscript issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common scribal habits, errors, and physical damages in Manuscript B, and how the editor addressed them.

Core Idea
Manuscript B reveals much about scribal habits and textual corruption.

This passage from the critical edition introduces the specific characteristics of Manuscript B. The editor details the scribe's use of diacritical dots, simplification of hamzas, abbreviations, and error corrections. He also describes moisture damage that obliterated words and a puzzling disorganization of folios. Understanding these issues is crucial for appreciating the editorial decisions made in reconstructing the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview. Then read the translation to ensure comprehension.
The passage contains a garbled phrase that is noted in the commentary.
2
Identify the scribal habits
List the various scribal practices: dotting, hamza simplification, abbreviations, and marginal corrections.
Notice the examples given for each habit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثيرا ما كان الناسخ يتغاضى عن وضع النقط، أو يكتفي بنقطة واحدة لتدل على الباء أو الياء أو النون أو التاء أو الثاء، وكذا الفاء أو القاف على سبيل المثال - ثم نعول بكن وجود النكوبن - "لعل" ببطلانها نعول باطل، أقاويل، تائث، لسفيد الجامعوب السح - كذا كان يسهل الهمزات دائما فيكتب أسولة بدلا من أسئلة - وأشيا بدلا من أشياء، وكان يقوم باختصار بعض الكلمات مثل م أي ممنوع ، ح أي حينئذ ، رح أي رحمة الله، وممت أي ممتنع الظ أي الظاهرة، لا يخ أي لا يخلوه وغير ذلك.
مثلا
وقد كان يسهو أثناء النقل فيضع ما نسيه في الهامش مذيلة بكلمة "صح". وبالنسخة رطوبة في بعض اللوحات أثرت لدرجة طمس الكثير من الكلمات، وقد أثبتها بمعرفة السياق، وبقية النسخ الأخرى، أما بالنسبة للسقط فهو قليل، وقد أشرت إليه في موضعه واضعا الساقط في مكانه بين قوسين ومشيرا إليه بالهامش. هذا وأثناء المقارنة وجدت اضطرابا في ترتيب أقسام بعض اللوحات فمثلا اللوحات ۷۰، ۷۱، ۷۲ بعد أن ينتهي من وجه اللوحة ۷۰ ينتقل إلى ظهر ۷۱، وقد استمر الاضطراب في ترتيب وجهي كل لوحة من اللوحات الثلاث، وكذا انتقل من وجه اللوحة ۷۷ إلى ظهر اللوحة ١٠٦ ، ومن وجه اللوحة ٨٤ إلى ظهر اللوحة ٨٥ وبعد أن انتهى من وجه ٨٥ عاد إلى ظهر ٨٤ ، وبعد أن انتهى من وجه اللوحة ٩٦ عاد إلى ظهر اللوحة ۷۷ ، وبعد أن انتهى من وجه اللوحات ١٠٦ عاد إلى ظهر ٩٦. وقد تبين لي بمقارنة النسخ الأخرى، وبمعونة السياق أن هذا الاضطراب يرجع إلى التصوير وأن الأصل ببلدية الإسكندرية خال من هذه الاضطرابات وقد أبلغت المسئولين بالمعهد بذلك، وقمت أنا بترتيب اللوحات في المصور الذي تحت يدي، وقد أشرت إلى ذلك في موضعه من التحقيق.
The scribe often neglected to place dots, or sufficed with a single dot to indicate bā', yā', nūn, tā', or thā', and likewise fā' or qā', for example — then n'wl bkn wjwd al-nkwbn — "perhaps" by its falsity we rely on false, sayings, tā'th, lsfyd al-jām'wb al-sḥ — likewise he always simplified hamzas, writing 'aswlah' instead of 'as'ilah', and 'ashyā' instead of 'ashyā'', and he abbreviated some words, such as 'm' meaning 'mamnū', 'ḥ' meaning 'ḥīnādhā', 'rḥ' meaning 'raḥmat Allāh', 'mmt' meaning 'mumtani'', 'al-ẓ' meaning 'al-ẓāhirah', 'lā ykh' meaning 'lā yukhlū', and others. For example, he would make mistakes during copying and place what he forgot in the margin, ending with the word 'ṣaḥ'. The copy has moisture in some folios that affected it to the point of obliterating many words. I restored them by context and the other copies. As for lacunae, they are few, and I indicated them in their places, placing the missing text in brackets and indicating it in the margin. Also, during comparison, I found disorder in the arrangement of sections of some folios. For example, folios 70, 71, 72: after finishing the recto of folio 70, he goes to the verso of 71. The disorder continued in the arrangement of the recto and verso of each of the three folios. Likewise, he went from the recto of folio 77 to the verso of 106, and from the recto of folio 84 to the verso of 85. After finishing the recto of 85, he returned to the verso of 84. After finishing the recto of folio 96, he returned to the verso of 77. After finishing the recto of folios 106, he returned to the verso of 96. I learned, by comparing the other copies and with the help of context, that this disorder is due to photography and that the original in the Alexandria Municipality is free from these disorders. I informed the responsible officials at the institute of that, and I myself rearranged the folios in the photograph I have, and I indicated that in its place in the critical edition.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage covers the scribal habits and problems found in manuscript B, as observed by the editor. It begins with the scribe's inconsistent use of diacritical dots: he often omitted them or used a single dot for multiple letters (bā', yā', nūn, tā', thā', fā', qā'). The text then has a garbled phrase that likely describes further instances but is unclear. Next, the scribe consistently simplified hamzas, writing 'aswlah' instead of 'as'ilah' and 'ashyā' for 'ashyā'' (actually 'ashyā' already has a hamza, but likely he wrote it without hamza). He also used abbreviations: 'm' for 'mamnū'' (forbidden), 'ḥ' for 'ḥīnādhā' (then), 'rḥ' for 'raḥmat Allāh' (God's mercy), 'mmt' for 'mumtani'' (impossible), 'al-ẓ' for 'al-ẓāhirah' (apparent), 'lā ykh' for 'lā yukhlū' (not empty), and others. The scribe was prone to errors during transcription, writing his omissions in the margin and marking them with 'ṣaḥ' (correct). The manuscript also suffered from moisture damage that obliterated many words; the editor restored them using context and the other copies. Lacunae (squṭ) were few, and the editor indicated them by placing the missing text in brackets and noting it in the margin. Finally, the editor discovered a significant disorganization in the arrangement of folios in the photographic copy: for example, after the recto of folio 70, it jumps to the verso of 71, and similar irregularities occur throughout. Through comparison with other copies and context, he determined that the disorder was introduced when the manuscript was photographed, and that the original in Alexandria is free of these issues. He notified the institute and rearranged the folios in his copy, noting this in the critical edition.
