Refutation of Absolute Deniers of Reality
Nasafi refutes absolute deniers of reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes those who deny all objective reality and knowledge, using arguments from consensus, practical behavior, and self-defeating reasoning.

Core Idea
Denying reality is self-defeating and cannot be debated.

Imam al-Nasafi begins by affirming that rational people agree on the existence of knowledge and realities. However, a faction (the sophists) denies any reality and claims everything is mere opinion. Nasafi provides a multi-pronged refutation: they cannot be debated because they reject common ground; their own behavior shows they believe in reality; their argument about deceptive senses is self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first group
Read the Arabic passage about the consensus and the first sophist group. Notice how Nasafi sets up the consensus as a premise.
The passage begins with 'أجمع العقلاء'.
2
Understand why debate is impossible
Nasafi argues that debate requires a common foundation. Since the sophists reject any reality, there is no basis for discussion. Ask yourself: what would be the point of debating someone who denies the very grounds of argument?
3
Analyze the practical treatment strategy
Nasafi suggests inflicting severe punishment, then reminding the sophists that by their own doctrine, the pain is unreal. This is a reductio ad absurdum that exposes their inconsistency. Also note: their admission of not knowing their own doctrine is a self-contradiction.
This strategy is not literal torture but a dialectical tool.
4
Observe the self-defeating sensory argument
The sophists argue that senses are deceptive. Nasafi points out that they use the same senses to make that claim, so their argument is self-refuting. Reflect on how this mirrors other self-refuting claims in philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يتحدث عن إثبات الحقائق والعلوم، فيقول: أجمع العقلاء على ثبوت العلم والحقائق للأشياء إلا أن هناك فرقة تقول: لا حقيقة لشيء، ولا علم بشيء، وإنما ظنون وحسبانات، ويرى أن هؤلاء لا مجال لمناظرتهم، لأن أساس المناظرة تكون لإثبات رأي، ونفي آخر، وتنبني على أساس مشترك بين المتناظرين، وله رأي وجيه في علاج مثل هؤلاء، وهو إنزال العقاب الشديد بهم فإذا ما ضجروا قيل لهم: لا حقيقة لشيء وإنما هي حسبانات وظنون ويستمر العقاب حتى يرجعوا عن عنادهم، كذلك فإنهم عندما ينكرون الحقائق يقرون أنهم لا يعلمون صحة مذهبهم و من أقر ببطلان مذهبه، فقد كفى خصمه مؤنة مجادلته، كذلك فإن حرصهم على المنافع، وابتعادهم عن المضار يؤكد أنهم يعلمون حقائق الأشياء، وإلا لما عاشوا. ويقول: إن ما استدلوا به من أن الحواس خادعة دليل على بطلان أقوالهم، لأنهم اشتغلوا بالحواس في بيان الإبطال. وهناك طائفة اللاأدرية، ومناقشة هؤلاء تكون بطرح هذا السؤال: هل تدرون أنكم لا تدرون؟ فإن قالوا: نعم. أقروا بإبطال مذهبهم وإن قالوا: لا استمر الكلام معهم عن نفي الدراية، وفي النهاية يعاملون بنفس ما عومل به الفريق الأول. ثم هناك طائفة ثالثة تقول: إن حقائق الأشياء تابعة لاعتقادات المعتقدين. والرد على هؤلاء بأن نقول عكسهم، فإن وافقونا أقروا بإبطال مذهبهم، وإن خالفونا، فقد أقروا أن المعتقد ليس بتابع للاعتقاد ويعاملون بنفس الطريقة، فإذا تألموا يقال لهم: اعتقدوا بأن ما يفعل بكم خير، وهذا الفرقة المنكرة للحقائق والمعلوم هي فرقة السوفسطائية. والنسفي في حديثه عنهم يذكر شبههم، وأساس المناظرة السليمة والأسباب التي تجعل الحواس تخطئ.
Then he speaks about proving realities and sciences, saying: The rational people are agreed upon the affirmation of knowledge and realities for things, except that there is a faction that says: There is no reality to anything, and no knowledge of anything, but only opinions and conjectures. And he holds that there is no room for debating these people, because the basis of debate is to prove one opinion and negate another, and it is built upon a common foundation between the two disputants. And he has a sound opinion in treating such people: that is inflicting severe punishment on them. When they become annoyed, it is said to them: There is no reality to anything, but only conjectures and opinions; and the punishment continues until they give up their stubbornness. Likewise, when they deny realities, they admit that they do not know the soundness of their own doctrine, and whoever admits the invalidity of his doctrine has spared his opponent the trouble of arguing with him. Similarly, their eagerness for benefits and avoidance of harms confirms that they know the realities of things, otherwise they would not survive. And he says: What they have used as evidence, that the senses are deceptive, is a proof of the invalidity of their statements, because they occupied themselves with the senses in explaining invalidation. And there is the agnostic faction, and the discussion with these people is by posing this question: Do you know that you do not know? If they say: Yes, they admit the invalidity of their doctrine. If they say: No, the speech continues with them about the negation of knowledge, and in the end they are treated in the same way as the first group. Then there is a third faction that says: The realities of things are dependent on the beliefs of the believers. The refutation of these is by saying the opposite of them. If they agree with us, they admit the invalidity of their doctrine; and if they disagree, they have admitted that the belief is not dependent on belief, and they are treated in the same way. So if they feel pain, it is said to them: Believe that what is done to you is good. And this faction that denies realities and knowledge is the sophist faction. And al-Nasafi, in his discussion of them, mentions their doubts, the basis of sound debate, and the causes that make the senses err.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a report of Imam al-Nasafi's refutation of the sophists (al-sufistaiyya), those who deny objective reality and knowledge. It covers three distinct groups: 1) Those who outright deny any reality and claim everything is mere opinion. Nasafi says rational consensus affirms knowledge and reality, so these people cannot be debated because they reject the common basis of debate. Instead, Nasafi recommends a practical treatment: inflict severe punishment on them, and when they complain, remind them that by their own doctrine nothing is real, so their pain is also unreal. This is a form of reductio ad absurdum. Their stubbornness is broken by experience. Moreover, their admission that they do not know the soundness of their own doctrine is itself a confession that their doctrine is invalid. Also, their practical behavior—seeking benefit and avoiding harm—shows they implicitly believe in the reality of things. Their argument that the senses are deceptive is self-defeating because they use the same senses to claim deceptiveness. 2) The agnostics (al-la-adriyya) claim they do not know. Nasafi questions them: 'Do you know that you do not know?' If they say yes, they affirm knowledge, contradicting themselves. If they say no, they cannot even assert their position. Ultimately they are treated like the first group. 3) Those who say realities depend on individual beliefs. Nasafi refutes by saying the opposite: if reality depends on belief, then if someone believes the opposite, reality would change, leading to contradiction. If they agree, they undermine their own position; if they disagree, they admit that belief does not determine reality. Again punished similarly. The passage concludes by noting that Nasafi discusses their doubts, the correct basis for debate, and reasons for sensory error.

Key Terms
السوفسطائية (al-sufistaiyya, sophists)
حقائق (realities)
ظنون (opinions/conjectures)
مناظرة (debate)
عقاب (punishment)
حواس (senses)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Nasafi advocates physical torture as a standard punishment for all dissenters.
Correction: Nasafi's suggestion of punishment is a dialectical strategy to expose self-contradiction, not a literal prescription for all cases. It is applied to a group that rejects the very premises of rational discourse.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why is it impossible to debate the first sophist group?
A) Because they are too stubborn
B) Because they reject the common foundation required for debate
C) Because they are not rational
D) Because they use deceptive senses

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's refutation shows that the denial of all reality is self-refuting on multiple levels—logical, practical, and epistemological.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe in truth and reality, yet how often do you live as if your own desires or convenience override objective truth? Nasafi says even the sophist's behavior betrays his beliefs. What do your daily choices reveal about what you truly hold as real?
?
When you encounter an uncomfortable truth about yourself—a sin, a flaw, a duty you've neglected—do you ever push it aside, telling yourself 'it doesn't matter' or 'it's not real'? How does Nasafi's argument expose that denial as self-refuting? Will you confront that truth today?
?
Nasafi says that punishing the sophist and telling him his pain is unreal is a sound treatment. In what area of your life are you ignoring the consequences of your actions, treating them as unreal? What will you do to stop that denial and face reality?

Refutation of Agnostics and Subjectivists
Nasafi refutes agnostics and subjectivists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes those who claim ignorance (agnostics) and those who say reality depends on individual belief (subjectivists), using dialectical questions that expose self-contradiction.

Core Idea
Even the claim 'I do not know' is self-refuting.

After refuting absolute deniers, Nasafi tackles two other sophist factions: agnostics who claim they do not know, and subjectivists who hold that realities depend on individual beliefs. For each, a simple question reveals their position is incoherent. This lesson examines these arguments and the consistent treatment Nasafi applies to all such deniers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on agnostics and subjectivists
Read the Arabic passage that introduces the agnostic faction and the subjectivist faction. Note how Nasafi transitions from the first group to these.
The passage starts with 'وهناك طائفة اللاأدرية'.
2
Examine the refutation of agnostics
Nasafi asks: 'Do you know that you do not know?' If they say yes, they affirm knowledge, contradicting their agnosticism. If they say no, they cannot even assert their position. Consider how this question traps them.
3
Examine the refutation of subjectivists
Nasafi argues: say the opposite of their claim. If they agree, they admit their doctrine is wrong. If they disagree, they admit that belief does not determine reality. Also, the punishment test: if they feel pain, tell them to believe it is good—application of their own logic.
This mirrors the treatment of the first group.

D) By arguing that realities depend on beliefs

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's dialectical method shows that even the denial of knowledge or the claim that reality is subjective collapses when confronted with a simple self-refuting question.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
When you hold an opinion, do you ever act as if your belief changes reality? What concrete step can you take today to submit to objective truth—even when it is uncomfortable?

Three Objective Sources of Knowledge
Nasafi enumerates three sources and names the deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's three objective sources of knowledge and his rejection of subjective intuition and Sufism.

Core Idea
Knowledge comes from senses, true report, and intellect—not from subjective intuition.

Nasafi builds his epistemology on an objective foundation. He begins by identifying the three channels through which knowledge reaches humans: sound senses, true report (either mass-transmitted or prophetic with miracle), and intellect. He also sets the stage by noting which groups deny which sources.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once for rhythm, then read the translation to grasp the overall structure.
Notice that Nasafi explicitly rejects subjectivity and Sufism.
2
Identify the three sources
Find the three sources listed and note the brief definitions: sound senses, true report (mutawatir or prophetic with miracle), and intellect.
These are the foundation for all knowledge claims in his theology.
3
Examine the deniers
List which groups deny which sources: sophists deny all, Sumaniyya and Brahmins deny report, Sumaniyya also deny intellect.
Nasafi says he has refuted each, which will be detailed in later passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبني النسفي منهجه في المعرفة على أساس موضوعي، بعيدا عن الاستيطان أو الذاتية، ولهذا فإنه يرفض التصوف، ويبين أن المعارف تنتقل للإنسان من طرق ثلاثة وهي: أ- الحواس السليمة. ب الخبر الصادق، المنقول بالتواتر، أو خبر الرسول المؤيد بالمعجزة. ج العقل. ويقرر النسفي أن السوفسطائية تنكرها كلها، بينما ينكر السمنية، والبراهمة كون الخبر من أسباب المعارف، وتنكر السمنية كذلك كون العقل من أسباب المعارف، وقد رد على كل المنكرين. فبالنسبة لمنكري الحس فقد سبق الحديث عنهم. وبالنسبة لمنكري الخبر فإنه يبين أن الخبر الصادق هو المنقول بالتوات أو خبر الرسول المؤيد بالمعجزة، أما ما عدا ذلك فيدخل فيه كلام المنكرين، وأما منكرو العقل، فقد ذكر ،شبههم ورد عليها بإطناب ،شديد وبضرب الأمثلة، وقد بين أن العقل يخطئ لأسباب منها قصوره عن بلوغ درجة النظر الصحيح، وتقصيره في مراعاة شروط الفكر ، وهو هنا يقسم العلم الحادث إلى ضروري واكتسابي.
And al-Nasafi builds his methodology in knowledge on an objective basis, far from intuition or subjectivity, and for this he rejects Sufism, and explains that knowledge is transmitted to man through three ways: A) Sound senses. B) True report, transmitted by tawatur (mass transmission), or the report of the messenger supported by a miracle. C) Intellect. And al-Nasafi states that the sophists deny all of them, while al-Sumaniyya and al-Barahima deny that report is a cause of knowledge, and al-Sumaniyya also deny that intellect is a cause of knowledge, and he has refuted all the deniers. As for the deniers of the senses, their discussion has already preceded. As for the deniers of report, he explains that the true report is that which is transmitted by tawatur or the report of the messenger supported by a miracle. As for what is other than that, it falls into the speech of the deniers. As for the deniers of intellect, he mentioned their doubts and refuted them with lengthy elaboration and by giving examples, and he explained that the intellect errs for reasons including its insufficiency to reach the level of correct inquiry, and its negligence in observing the conditions of thought, and he here divides contingent knowledge into necessary and acquired.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents al-Nasafi's epistemology. He rejects intuition and subjectivity, and rejects Sufism as a source of knowledge. He affirms three objective sources: sound senses, true report (either widely transmitted or prophetic with miracle), and intellect. He then notes which groups deny which sources: the sophists deny all, the Sumaniyya and Brahmins deny report, and the Sumaniyya also deny intellect. He has individually refuted each denial. For senses, he previously dealt with it. For report, he defines true report as mutawatir or prophetic report with miracle, and all other reports are the claims of the deniers themselves. For intellect, he refutes their doubts at length, explaining that intellect errs due to incapacity for correct inquiry or negligence of thought conditions. Finally, he divides contingent knowledge into necessary (acquired without effort) and acquired (through inference).

Key Terms
الحواس السليمة
الخبر الصادق
التواتر
المعجزة
العقل
السوفسطائية
السمنية
البراهمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Nasafi accepts intuition or mystical experience as a source of knowledge.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly rejects subjectivity and Sufism, grounding knowledge in objective sources.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, which of the following is NOT one of the three sources of knowledge?
A) Sound senses
B) True report
C) Intuition
D) Intellect

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's epistemology is objective, based on three sources: senses, true report, and intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one belief you hold that you cannot trace back to any of these three objective sources? Will you abandon it or investigate it further after this lesson?
?
Nasafi rejects subjective intuition as a source of knowledge. In your daily life, how often do you rely on feelings or 'gut instinct' rather than senses, verified reports, or reason? Can you give a specific example where this has led you astray?
?
If you were to treat the Islamic reports you follow with the same rigor as Nasafi—requiring either mass transmission or prophetic miracle—would your practice change? Are you ready to examine the sources of what you consider 'certain' religious knowledge?

True Report as a Source of Knowledge
Nasafi defines true report and refutes its deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's definition of true report and his refutation of those who deny it as a source of knowledge.

Core Idea
True report is either mass-transmitted or prophetic with a miracle.

After listing the three sources, Nasafi turns specifically to the deniers of report. He defines what constitutes a true report—either mutawatir (mass-transmitted) or a prophetic report supported by a miracle—and argues that anything else falls into the claims of the deniers themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Notice how Nasafi first mentions the deniers of senses briefly, then focuses on report deniers.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Define true report
Identify the two types of true report: mutawatir and prophetic report with a miracle.
Mutawatir ensures certainty through multiple chains.
3
Understand the refutation
Nasafi says all other reports are the speech of the deniers themselves.
This is a rhetorical refutation—deniers rely on reports they reject.

?

?
The deniers of report unknowingly rely on reports to build their argument against reports. Are there any beliefs you hold that, if examined, rely on a source you claim to reject? Where might your own reasoning be self-refuting?
?
Nasafi's definition excludes any report that is not mutawatir or prophetic with miracle. In your daily life, how many of your religious beliefs meet this criterion? Does this make you uncomfortable, and if so, what should you do about it?

Intellect and Division of Knowledge
Nasafi refutes deniers of intellect and divides knowledge into necessary and acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's refutation of those who deny intellect, his explanation of why the intellect errs, and his division of contingent knowledge into necessary and acquired.

Core Idea
Intellect errs due to negligence or incapacity; knowledge is necessary or acquired.

After addressing the deniers of senses and report, Nasafi turns to those who deny intellect as a source of knowledge. He refutes their doubts at length, explaining that errors occur because of insufficiency or negligence. He then introduces a key distinction: contingent knowledge (al-ilm al-hadith) is either necessary (self-evident) or acquired (inferential).

Guided Path
1
Read about intellect deniers
Nasafi mentions he refuted their doubts with many examples and detail.
The actual refutation is not in this passage.
2
Understand why intellect errs
Two reasons: insufficiency to reach correct inquiry, and negligence in observing conditions of thought.
This shows intellect is reliable when used properly.
3
Learn the division of knowledge
Nasafi divides contingent knowledge into necessary (bedazzah) and acquired (muktasab).
Necessary knowledge is direct; acquired requires reasoning.

?

Degrees of Knowledge: Necessary and Inferential
Nasafi enumerates two degrees of knowledge and their relationship to certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's classification of knowledge into necessary and inferential, and how both can yield certainty.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either self-evident or derived, but both lead to certainty.

In this passage, al-Nasafi presents a foundational classification in Islamic epistemology. He reduces all knowledge to two types: necessary knowledge, which is immediate and certain, and inferential knowledge, which becomes certain when properly reasoned. This division is crucial for distinguishing between truths that require proof and those that are self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very concise style here.
2
Identify the two types of knowledge
Find the two categories: necessary knowledge (daruri) and inferential knowledge (istidlali). Note that necessary knowledge is immediately certain, while inferential knowledge requires conditions.
3
Understand the condition for inferential knowledge
Nasafi says inferential knowledge reaches certainty if all conditions are observed and the reasoning moves from particular to universal in a sound manner. Ask yourself what those conditions might be.
The passage does not list the conditions; that is for later study.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this classification matters: it helps in theological arguments to know which claims need proof and which are self-evident.
This connects to the broader methodology of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويحصر النسفي درجات المعرفة في درجتين: أ - المعرفة الضرورية، وهي يقينية. ب المعرفة الاستدلالية، وتنتهى إلى اليقين إذا روعيت كل الشروط، وانتقل فيها من الجزئي إلى الكلي بطريقة سليمة.
And al-Nasafi restricts the degrees of knowledge to two degrees: A) Necessary knowledge, and it is certain. B) Inferential knowledge, and it reaches certainty if all conditions are observed, and one moves in it from the particular to the universal in a sound manner.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defines the two degrees or types of knowledge according to al-Nasafi. First is necessary knowledge (daruri), which is immediately certain without need for proof. Second is inferential knowledge (istidlali), which is acquired through reasoning. If all proper conditions are met and the inference proceeds correctly from particulars to universals, it too yields certainty. This classification is important in kalam for distinguishing between self-evident truths and those requiring demonstration.

Key Terms
معرفة ضرورية
معرفة استدلالية
يقين
جزئي
كلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inferential knowledge is never certain.
Correction: Nasafi states that inferential knowledge reaches certainty if all conditions are observed and reasoning is sound.

Checkpoint
What condition must inferential knowledge meet to reach certainty?
A) It must be based on true report
B) All conditions must be observed and the inference must be sound
C) It must be accepted by the majority
D) It must be accompanied by inspiration

Key Takeaway
Nasafi classifies knowledge into two certainty-yielding types: necessary (immediate) and inferential (conditional on sound reasoning).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one core belief you hold about Allah. Is it based on necessary knowledge (something you 'just know') or inferential knowledge (something you reasoned through)? If inferential, have you personally verified that the reasoning moves soundly from particular to universal, or are you relying on someone else's inference?
?
Al-Nasafi teaches that inferential knowledge reaches certainty only when all conditions are observed. Which condition are you most likely to neglect in your own reasoning about faith? Be specific: when was the last time you checked the soundness of your movement from evidence to conclusion?
?
If you were to encounter a doubt about a belief you hold as certain, would you retreat to necessary knowledge or inspect your inference? This lesson forces you to examine: is your certainty grounded in genuine proof or just comfort? What would it take for you to truly 'know' rather than 'assume'?

Rejection of Inner Experiences and Inspiration
Nasafi rejects subjective inner knowledge and inspiration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Nasafi rejects subjective inner experiences and inspiration as sources of religious knowledge.

Core Idea
Subjective inner convictions lead to contradictory religions; only reason and revelation ensure unity.

Imam al-Nasafi continues his methodological critique by addressing two common claims: that what suddenly occurs to the heart (khawatir) and inspiration (ilham) can serve as sources of religious knowledge. He rejects both on the same ground: they produce a multiplicity of conflicting religions. This short but decisive passage shuts the door to subjectivism in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise structure: two rejections with a shared reason.
2
Identify the two rejected sources
Find 'ما يقع في القلب' (what falls into the heart) and 'الإلهام' (inspiration). Understand what each refers to in context.
The passage does not define these terms; assume they mean sudden intuitions and claimed divine inspiration.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Both rejections use the same argument: they lead to a multiplicity of religions. Ask yourself why that is a decisive theological problem.
In Islamic theology, truth is one; subjective sources would produce contradictory claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورأي النسفي فيها: أولا: يرفض النسفي ما يقع في القلب، ويقول : إن هذا يؤدي إلى كثرة الأديان. :ثانيا من يقول بأن الإلهام سبب من أسباب معرفة الأديان قول مرفوض؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى ما أدى إليه سابقه.
And al-Nasafi's view regarding them: First: al-Nasafi rejects what falls into the heart, and he says: This leads to a multiplicity of religions. Second: Whoever says that inspiration is a cause of knowledge of religions is a rejected saying; because it leads to what its predecessor led to.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage gives al-Nasafi's ruling on two related topics: what falls into the heart (i.e., sudden convictions or intuitions) and inspiration (ilham) as sources of religious knowledge. He rejects both. First, relying on what falls into the heart leads to multiple religions because different people get different thoughts. Second, inspiration as a source of religious knowledge is also rejected because it leads to the same problem: diversity and contradiction. This aligns with his earlier rejection of subjectivity and Sufi intuitive knowledge.

Key Terms
ما يقع في القلب
إلهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nasafi rejects the occurrence of inspiration itself.
Correction: Nasafi rejects inspiration as a valid epistemological source for religious knowledge, not necessarily its occurrence. He denies it as a basis for determining truth.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi reject 'what falls into the heart' as a source of knowledge?
A) Because it is not verifiable
B) Because it leads to a multiplicity of religions
C) Because it contradicts the senses
D) Because it is only for prophets

Key Takeaway
Nasafi rejects subjective inner experiences and inspiration because they undermine the unity of religious truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever acted on a sudden inner feeling or intuition in a religious matter without checking it against the Quran, Sunnah, or reasoned evidence? What was the outcome, and what did that teach you about the reliability of such feelings?
?
Think of a time when you were absolutely certain about something religious based on a personal experience. Could that certainty have been misplaced? How would you know the difference between true guidance and subjective imagination?
?
If someone tells you they received divine inspiration to adopt a new practice, what would be your responsibility as a believer to respond based on Nasafi’s principle? Are you willing to challenge such claims even if they feel sincere?

The Three Sources of Knowledge
Al-Nasafi enumerates the three sources of knowledge and the groups that deny them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's classification of the three sources of knowledge and the main groups that deny them.

Core Idea
Knowledge comes from senses, truthful report, and reason — and some groups deny each.

Al-Nasafi begins by laying out the fundamental sources from which humans acquire knowledge. These are sound senses, truthful report (either widely transmitted or prophetic), and reason. He then notes the positions of various skeptics: the Sophists deny all three, while the Sumaniyya and Brahmins deny specific sources. This lesson introduces these sources and the deniers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Nasafi presents the sources in a concise list.
Pay attention to the terms: الحواس السليمة, الخبر الصادق, العقل.
2
Identify the three sources
List the three sources: senses, truthful report, and reason. Note the conditions for report: it must be transmitted by tawātur or come from a miracle-backed messenger.
Truthful report is not any report; it has specific requirements.
3
Identify the deniers
Find the groups that deny each source: Sophists deny all, Sumaniyya deny report and reason, Brahmins deny only report.
Al-Nasafi says he has refuted all deniers, indicating his intention to defend these sources.
4
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself why al-Nasafi starts with these sources. They are the foundations for all theological knowledge.
Without established sources, no argument can proceed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ- الحواس السليمة. ب الخبر الصادق، المنقول بالتواتر، أو خبر الرسول المؤيد بالمعجزة. ج- العقل. ويقرر النسفي أن السوفسطائية تنكرها كلها، بينما ينكر السمنية، والبراهمة كون الخبر من أسباب المعارف، وتنكر السمنية كذلك كون العقل من أسباب المعارف، وقد رد على كل المنكرين. فبالنسبة لمنكري الحس فقد سبق الحديث عنهم. وبالنسبة لمنكري الخبر فإنه يبين أن الخبر الصادق هو المنقول بالتوات أو خبر الرسول المؤيد بالمعجزة، أما ما عدا ذلك فيدخل فيه كلام المنكرين، وأما منكرو العقل، فقد ذكر شبههم ورد عليها بإطناب شديد وبضرب الأمثلة، وقد بين أن العقل يخطئ لأسباب منها قصوره عن بلوغ درجة النظر الصحيح، وتقصيره في مراعاة شروط الفكر، وهو هنا يقسم العلم الحادث إلى ضروري واكتسابي. درجات المعرفة ويحصر النسفي درجات المعرفة في درجتين: أ - المعرفة الضرورية، وهي يقينية. ب المعرفة الاستدلالية، وتنتهى إلى اليقين إذا روعيت كل الشروط، وانتقل فيها من الجزئي إلى الكلي بطريقة سليمة.
A: Sound senses. B: Truthful report, transmitted by tawātur (widespread transmission), or the report of the messenger supported by a miracle. C: Reason. And al-Nasafi establishes that the Sophists deny all of them, while the Sumaniyya and the Brahmins deny that report is a cause of knowledge, and the Sumaniyya also deny that reason is a cause of knowledge, and he refuted every denier. As for the deniers of sense, their discussion has preceded. As for the deniers of report, he clarifies that the truthful report is the one transmitted by tawātur or the report of the messenger supported by a miracle; anything other than that includes the speech of the deniers. As for the deniers of reason, he mentioned their doubts and refuted them with great elaboration and by giving examples, and he explained that reason errs for reasons including its incapacity to reach the level of correct investigation and its deficiency in observing the conditions of thought. And here he divides acquired knowledge into necessary and acquired. Degrees of knowledge: And al-Nasafi limits the degrees of knowledge to two: A: necessary knowledge, which is certain; B: inferential knowledge, which ends in certainty if all conditions are observed and one moves from the particular to the universal in a sound manner.

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three sources of knowledge according to al-Nasafi: sound senses, truthful report (either mutawātir or prophetic report backed by a miracle), and reason. It then describes the positions of various deniers: the Sophists deny all three; the Sumaniyya and Brahmins deny report as a source; the Sumaniyya also deny reason. Al-Nasafi refutes each. For sense-deniers, the discussion is already passed. For report-deniers, he clarifies that only tawātur or prophetic miracle-backed report counts as valid; other reports are the speech of deniers. For reason-deniers, he elaborately refutes their doubts and notes that reason can err due to inability to reach correct investigation or failing to meet conditions of thought. The passage ends by dividing acquired knowledge into necessary and inferential, with the latter leading to certainty when conditions are met and moving from particular to universal correctly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Nasafi points out that truthful report must be from tawātur or a miracle-backed messenger. How much of what you believe about Islam comes from sources that lack that level of authentication? Are you willing to trace one of your unverified beliefs back to its original report and test its chain? Make a plan to investigate one piece of Islamic knowledge this week.
?
The Sophists denied all sources, essentially cutting themselves off from truth. In what area of your life have you become cynical or dismissive of clear evidence? This lesson confronts you with the danger of rejecting God-given means of knowledge. Identify one area of scepticism in your heart—whether toward a religious teaching, a scientific fact, or a personal relationship—and choose to submit to the truth through the proper source.

Refutation of Deniers of Report and Reason
Al-Nasafi clarifies the conditions for truthful report and refutes deniers of reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's refutation of those who deny report and reason as sources of knowledge, and the reasons for reason's errors.

Core Idea
Truthful report is limited to tawātur or miracle-backed reports; reason errs due to shortcomings in investigation.

After listing the sources, al-Nasafi addresses those who deny report and reason. He clarifies that only reports transmitted by tawātur or from a miracle-backed messenger are valid. He then refutes the deniers of reason by explaining that reason can err when it fails to conduct proper investigation or observe the conditions of thought. This lesson focuses on these refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the flow: first a brief mention of sense-deniers (already discussed), then report-deniers, then reason-deniers.
The passage summarises al-Nasafi's approach to each group.
2
Understand the criteria for truthful report
Note that only tawātur or miracle-backed reports count as truthful reports. Other reports are the speech of deniers.
This sets a high bar for accepting reports as knowledge.
3
Grasp the refutation of reason-deniers
Al-Nasafi refutes them at length, explaining that reason errs due to its incapacity to reach correct investigation or its failure to observe conditions of thought.
This shows that reason is reliable when used properly.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why al-Nasafi defends both report and reason: they are foundational for religious knowledge (revelation and rational proofs).

Degrees of Knowledge: Necessary and Inferential
Al-Nasafi divides acquired knowledge into necessary and inferential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's division of acquired knowledge into necessary (certain) and inferential (leading to certainty under conditions).

Core Idea
Knowledge is either necessary (immediate certainty) or inferential (conditional certainty).

After discussing the sources and refuting deniers, al-Nasafi turns to the kinds of knowledge that result from these sources. He divides acquired knowledge into two degrees: necessary knowledge, which is certain without effort, and inferential knowledge, which can reach certainty if all conditions are met and if one reason correctly from particulars to universals. This lesson examines this classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two types: necessary and inferential. Al-Nasafi also mentions how inferential knowledge becomes certain.
The passage is concise but important for understanding certainty.
2
Identify the two degrees
List them: a) necessary knowledge (ḍarūriyya) – certain; b) inferential knowledge (istidlāliyya) – ends in certainty if conditions are met and one moves from particular to universal correctly.
Inferential knowledge is not automatically certain.
3
Understand the conditions for certainty
Inferential knowledge requires observing all conditions and moving correctly from particular to universal. This shows that reasoning must be rigorous.
This ties back to the previous lesson on reason's error when conditions are not met.
4
Reflect on the application
Consider how this division applies to theological arguments: some truths are known necessarily (e.g., the existence of the world) while others require inference (e.g., the existence of the Creator).

Rejection of Ilham and Taqlid as Sources of Religious Knowledge
Al-Nasafi rejects ilham and taqlid as valid sources for religious knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nasafi rejects subjective inspiration and blind imitation as valid sources for religious knowledge, and what he requires instead.

Core Idea
Only proof (burhan) and inference (istidlal) are valid sources of religious knowledge, not personal inspiration or blind imitation.

Al-Nasafi presents a crisp argument against two common but flawed claims about how we know religion. He warns that accepting inner inspiration or uncritical imitation leads to a multiplication of contradictory beliefs. Instead, he insists that any claim must be backed by rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation.
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is structured as three clear points.
2
Identify the three claims al-Nasafi rejects.
The passage rejects: (1) what occurs in the heart, (2) the claim that ilham is a cause of knowledge, (3) taqlid as a cause unless proof is provided. Notice how the first two are linked.
3
Understand the reason for rejection.
For the first two, the reason is the same: they lead to multiplicity of religions. Ask yourself: why would that be a bad consequence?
This reveals al-Nasafi's commitment to unity of truth.
4
Grasp the exception for taqlid.
Al-Nasafi does not reject taqlid absolutely: if the muqallid proves his view with proof, then he is no longer a muqallid. This shows that the real criterion is proof, not the act of following.
This nuance is often misunderstood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهام والتقليد وما يقع في القلب ورأي النسفي فيها: أولا: يرفض النسفي ما يقع في القلب، ويقول : إن هذا يؤدي إلى كثرة الأديان. :ثانيا من يقول بأن الإلهام سبب من أسباب معرفة الأديان قول مرفوض؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى ما أدى إليه سابقه. :ثالثا: يرفض جعل التقليد سببا من أسباب معرفة الأديان، إلا لو أثبت المقلد صحة رأيه بالبرهان، وهنا لا يكون تقليدا، وإنما يكون استدلالا.
Inspiration (ilham), imitation (taqlid), and what occurs in the heart: al-Nasafi's view on them: First: al-Nasafi rejects what occurs in the heart, and says: this leads to multiplicity of religions. Second: whoever says that ilham is a cause of knowledge of religions, his statement is rejected, because it leads to what the previous leads to. Third: he rejects making taqlid a cause of knowledge of religions, unless the imitator proves the correctness of his opinion by proof, in which case it is not taqlid but rather inference (istidlal).
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents al-Nasafi's rejection of two supposed sources of religious knowledge: ilham (inspiration) and taqlid (imitation). First, he rejects what occurs in the heart (i.e., subjective inspirations or inner thoughts) because it leads to a proliferation of religions, as each person would claim his own experience as authority. Second, he explicitly rejects the claim that ilham is a legitimate cause for knowing religions, for the same reason: it leads to the same consequence. Third, he rejects taqlid as a source of religious knowledge unless the person who practices it (the muqallid) can prove his opinion with a proof (burhan). In that case, it is no longer taqlid but istidlal (inference). Thus, for al-Nasafi, only valid reasoning and properly transmitted revelation are acceptable.

Key Terms
الإلهام
التقليد
البرهان
الاستدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Nasafi rejects taqlid absolutely, without exception.
Correction: He allows taqlid if the muqallid can prove his opinion with proof, but then it ceases to be taqlid and becomes istidlal.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi reject 'what occurs in the heart' as a source of religious knowledge?
A) Because it is not based on reason
B) Because it leads to multiplicity of religions
C) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran
D) Because it was rejected by the early Muslims

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi rejects subjective inspiration and blind imitation as sources of religious knowledge, allowing only proof-based reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever trusted a 'feeling' or inner inspiration as a sign from Allah, without checking it against revelation and reason? How does al-Nasafi's warning about multiplicity of religions challenge you to verify that feeling?
?
Consider a belief you hold that you cannot defend with clear proof. Are you willing to seek that proof, even if it means your conviction might be tested? What is holding you back?

Rejecting Blind Following in Religious Knowledge
Al-Nasafi rejects taqlid as a cause for knowing religions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taqlid is rejected as a basis for religious knowledge and what the valid alternative is.

Core Idea
Blind following cannot ground religious truth; only proof transforms it into valid reasoning.

This lesson examines a key methodological principle from Imam al-Nasafi: taqlid (blind acceptance) is not a valid means of knowing the truth of religions. The passage clarifies that only when the follower provides proof does the act become valid rational inference. Let's unpack this important distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the clear rejection of taqlid and the conditional exception.
The passage is concise but contains a complete logical point.
2
Understand the rejection of taqlid
Taqlid means accepting a belief without evidence or personal investigation. Al-Nasafi rejects this as a cause for knowing religions.
This is a foundational principle in Sunni theology: faith must be based on knowledge, not imitation.
3
Identify the exception
The exception is when the muqallid (follower) provides proof (burhan) for his view. In that case, it is no longer taqlid but istidlal (inference).
The exception does not save taqlid; rather, it transforms the act into something else.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Contrast taqlid (blind acceptance) with istidlal (reasoned inference). Ask yourself: What makes the difference? The presence of proof.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the role of reason in creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثا: يرفض جعل التقليد سببا من أسباب معرفة الأديان، إلا لو أثبت المقلد صحة رأيه بالبرهان، وهنا لا يكون تقليدا، وإنما يكون استدلالا.
Thirdly: He rejects making taqlid a cause of knowing religions, unless the muqallid establishes the correctness of his view with proof (burhan), for then it is not taqlid but rather istidlal (inference).
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage states a methodological principle: taqlid (blind following) is not a valid means for acquiring knowledge of religions. The only exception is if the follower (muqallid) proves the correctness of his view with a proof (burhan). In that case, it is no longer taqlid but rational inference (istidlal). Thus, the author guards against blind acceptance of religious beliefs without intellectual investigation.

Key Terms
تقليد
برهان
استدلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that taqlid is always acceptable in matters of creed, even without evidence.
Correction: Al-Nasafi clarifies that taqlid is not a valid basis for religious knowledge; only proof-based inference (istidlal) is acceptable.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the exception to the rejection of taqlid?
A) If the muqallid is a scholar
B) If the muqallid proves his view with proof
C) If the muqallid follows a consensus
D) If the muqallid is sincere

Key Takeaway
Taqlid is not a valid basis for religious knowledge; only proof-based inference (istidlal) is acceptable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many of your core Islamic beliefs have you personally verified with proof? Not just from reading or hearing, but reasoned through? What would it take for you to truly own them?
?
Is there an aspect of your faith that you hold only because it was taught to you, without ever demanding proof? Are you willing to examine it today?
?
If your entire belief system were stripped of inherited acceptance, what would remain? How would you rebuild it using only proof (burhan)?

Two Classical Approaches to Ambiguous Verses
Nasafi presents tafwid and ta'wil as two methods for mutashabihat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main Sunni approaches to ambiguous Quranic verses: tafwid (affirmation without modality) and ta'wil (figurative interpretation), and how both preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Affirm the meaning, negate the how — two paths, one goal: protecting tawhid.

The Quran contains verses that seem to describe God in physical terms, like 'istiwa'' (establishing Himself). How should a believer understand these? Imam Nasafi presents two classical methods used by Sunni scholars. The first, championed by Ahmad ibn Hanbal and Malik, affirms the verses without asking 'how'. The second interprets them figuratively to avoid any contradiction with monotheism. Both approaches are valid and aim to protect God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Nasafi presents two groups.
2
Identify the first approach: tafwid
Examine what Ahmad and Malik say: they believe without asking 'how'. Malik's statement is key.
Tafwid means consigning the true meaning to God.
3
Understand the second approach: ta'wil
The second group 'diverted' the verses to interpretations that don't contradict tawhid.
Ta'wil is figurative interpretation based on Arabic usage.
4
Reflect on the common goal
Both approaches avoid anthropomorphism. Ask: how do they achieve the same end differently?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما عن الآيات المتشابهة فيقول : إن أحمد بن حنبل، ومالك، وغيرهما يقولون : نؤمن بها، ولا نقول كيف وكيف حتى قال مالك: الاستواء غير مجهول والكيف غير معقول والسؤال عنه بدعة، وهؤلاء لم يشتغلوا بالتأويل. وهناك قسم ثان صرف هذه الآيات إلى الوجوه التي لا تتناقض التوحيد.
As for the ambiguous verses, he says: Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Malik, and others say: We believe in them, and we do not say 'how' and 'how'—until Malik said: The 'istiwa'' is not unknown, the modality is not intelligible, and asking about it is an innovation; these did not occupy themselves with interpretation (ta'wil). And there is a second group that diverted these verses to aspects that do not contradict tawhid.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage describes two classical approaches to the mutashabihat (ambiguous verses) of the Quran, particularly those that seem to attribute physicality to God (e.g., the verse about istiwa'). The first approach, exemplified by Ahmad ibn Hanbal and Malik ibn Anas, is tafwid: they affirm the verses (nu'minu biha) without asking 'how' (kayf). Malik's famous saying is quoted: 'The istiwa' is not unknown (ghayr majhul), the modality is not intelligible (al-kayf ghayr ma'qul), and asking about it is an innovation (bid'ah).' These early scholars did not engage in figurative interpretation (ta'wil). The second group (qism thani) is said to have diverted (saraffa) these verses to interpretations (wujuh) that do not contradict tawhid (monotheism). This second approach is likely what later Ashʿaris and Maturidis adopted. The passage thus presents two valid Sunni methods of dealing with anthropomorphic verses, both avoiding anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
آيات متشابهة
تأويل
تفويض
استواء
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The early Salaf rejected any interpretation and just said 'we believe' without any meaning.
Correction: In fact, they affirmed the linguistic meaning (like Malik: 'istiwa' is not unknown') but negated knowledge of the modality. This is a form of interpretation (tafwid) and is distinct from mere belief without understanding.

Checkpoint
According to Malik's statement cited by Nasafi, what is the ruling on asking about the modality (kayf) of istiwa'?
A) It is obligatory
B) It is recommended
C) It is an innovation (bid'ah)
D) It is permissible

Key Takeaway
Sunni scholars agree on affirming ambiguous verses about Allah's attributes while avoiding anthropomorphism, either by consigning the modality to Allah (tafwid) or by figurative interpretation (ta'wil).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses describing Allah's attributes, do you feel a tension between affirming them and avoiding mental images? Which of the two approaches described here helps you personally, and why?
?
Malik warned that asking 'how' about istiwa' is an innovation. Is there any aspect of Allah's attributes that you have tried to 'figure out' or rationalize beyond what is revealed? What would it look like for you to truly consign the modality to Allah and rest content?
?
The second group diverted verses to meanings that 'do not contradict tawhid'. Reflect on your own heart: are you more inclined to accept a literal or figurative interpretation? Could your preference be influenced by a desire to control or fully understand Allah, rather than submitting to His transcendence? How can you practice humility when faced with the ambiguous verses?

Nasafi's Methodology for Mutashabih Verses: Ta'wil and Tafwid
Nasafi explains the dispute with Mu'tazila is verbal and outlines the proper approach to mutashabih verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's methodology for handling mutashabih verses, distinguishing between ta'wil and tafwid as valid approaches to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
When the text is ambiguous, safeguard tanzih through ta'wil or tafwid.

Imam Nasafi engages with the Mu'tazila on the issue of divine attributes, arguing that their dispute is largely verbal because both sides affirm God's transcendence. The real question is how to interpret verses that seem to imply anthropomorphism. In this passage, Nasafi outlines two permissible methods: figurative interpretation (ta'wil) or consigning the true meaning to Allah (tafwid) while affirming the apparent wording without implying createdness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow. Note that Nasafi begins by addressing the Mu'tazila directly.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms 'khilaf lafzi', 'ta'wil', and 'tafwid' in the passage. Understand how Nasafi presents them.
3
Analyze the two options
Pay attention to the end where Nasafi says either ta'wil or belief in the apparent wording with tafwid. Ask yourself: what is the common goal of both approaches?
4
Reflect on the application
Consider an example verse like 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arsh istawa' and apply either ta'wil or tafwid. How would each approach safeguard tanzih?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما في كلامه مع المعتزلة فيقول: إن خلافنا معهم خلاف لفظي، إلا أن عبارتهم يوهم ظاهرها الكفر ، ويقول لهم: أي ضرورة دعتكم إلى قولكم ، ولم يرد به نص لا في كتاب ولا في سنة، والواجب علينا عند انعدام النص صيانة هذا المعنى؟. ويبدأ بعد ذلك في الرد على قولهم في الآيات المتشابهة مثل الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى [طه: ٥] ومثل * وَجَاءَ رَبُّكَ وَالْمَلَكُ صَفًّا صَفًّا ﴾ [الفجر: ٢٢] إلى آخره، فيطيل في الرد ، ليثبت أن أي كلمة لها معنيان معنى يستحيل على الباري ومعنى لا يستحيل، والمطلوب منا أن نأخذ المعنى الثاني. أي أنه يقول بالتأويل، أو الإيمان بظاهر التنزيل مع صيانة العقيدة عما يوهم الحدوث، وتفويض الأمر في المراد إلى الله عزوجل.
As for his discourse with the Mu'tazila, he says: Our dispute with them is a verbal dispute, except that the apparent meaning of their expression suggests disbelief. And he says to them: What necessity drove you to your statement, and no text from the Book nor the Sunnah has indicated it? And our duty when the text is absent is to safeguard this meaning. Then he begins after that to refute their statement concerning the ambiguous verses such as 'The Most Merciful settled over the Throne' [Taha: 5] and such as 'And your Lord comes and the angels, rank upon rank' [Fajr: 22] and so on. He lengthens the refutation to prove that every word has two meanings: a meaning impossible for the Creator and a meaning not impossible. And what is required of us is to take the second meaning. That is, he affirms ta'wil (interpretation) or belief in the apparent wording of the revelation while safeguarding the creed from what implies temporality, and consignment (tafwid) of the matter regarding the intended meaning to Allah, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a methodological exposition. Nasafi first characterizes the dispute with the Mu'tazila on God's location as a verbal dispute, but he warns that the outward sense of their expression implies disbelief. He asks them: what necessity drove you to your statement when there is no textual proof for it? In the absence of text, our duty is to guard the meaning (i.e., not to say anything that contradicts transcendence). Then he proceeds to refute their approach to mutashabih verses like 'al-Rahman 'ala al-'arsh istawa' and 'Wa ja'a rabbuka'. He proves that every such word has two possible meanings: one that is impossible for God (e.g., physical settling, movement) and another that is not impossible (e.g., istawa meaning 'istawla' or 'dominated', or ja'a meaning 'His command came', etc.). The required action is to take the second meaning. Nasafi affirms two valid approaches: either ta'wil (figurative interpretation) or belief in the apparent wording while safeguarding the creed from implying temporality, and consigning (tafwid) of the intended meaning to Allah. This is the standard Ash'ari/Maturidi method for handling mutashabih attributes.

Key Terms
خلاف لفظي
آيات متشابهة
تأويل
تفويض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ta'wil and tafwid are contradictory methods.
Correction: Nasafi presents them as two valid options, both aiming to protect God's transcendence from anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what is the duty of Muslims when there is no textual proof for a particular interpretation of a divine attribute?
A) To remain silent
B) To accept the literal meaning
C) To safeguard the meaning of tanzih
D) To follow the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
Nasafi affirms that for ambiguous verses, Muslims must either interpret figuratively to avoid impossible meanings, or affirm the wording while consigning the true intent to Allah, always prioritizing God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى', what image arises in your mind? Are you willing to abandon any physical picture and say 'I affirm the wording but consign the meaning to Allah'? How does your ego react to not knowing the exact meaning?
?
Think of a time you were tempted to interpret a divine attribute in a way that made it seem more 'relatable' or 'understandable' to you. Did that interpretation risk implying a limitation on Allah? Will you now commit to checking your thoughts against Nasafi's principle: is this meaning possible for Allah or not?
?
Nasafi warns that the Mu'tazila's apparent expression 'suggests disbelief'. Reflect on your own words about Allah's attributes. Have you ever spoken carelessly, describing Allah in terms that could imply temporality? Will you choose to adopt ta'wil or tafwid to protect His transcendence, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Criticism of Abandoning Rational Proofs
Criticism of opponents rejecting rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's criticism of opponents who abandon rational evidence for coincidences and illusions, and why this is a methodological error.

Core Idea
Abandoning rational proofs opens the door to denying the Creator.

In this passage, the author sharply criticizes those who reject rational proofs in favor of coincidental signs and illusions. He argues that such a move is not only intellectually flawed but also dangerous, as it empowers those who deny the existence of the Creator. Understanding this criticism helps us appreciate the centrality of rational evidence in theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's criticism come through clearly.
Notice the strong language: 'turned away', 'clung', 'rejected', 'opened the widest door'.
2
Identify the two errors
The author lists two things opponents cling to instead of the absolute cause: coincidental indications and illusion. Find these in the text.
The 'absolute cause' refers to the rational proof for vision of Allah.
3
Understand the consequence
The author states that by rejecting rational proofs, opponents open the door for those who negate the Creator. Reflect on why this consequence follows.
Without rational evidence, any claim becomes possible, including denial of the Maker.
4
Reflect on submission to rational proofs
The passage ends by linking the error to lack of submission to rational proofs. Consider what 'submission' means in this context.
Submission here means accepting the authority of sound rational argument in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخصومنا أعرضوا عن العلة المطلقة الرؤية، وتمسكوا بما وجدوا من القرائن الاتفاقية، وتمسكوا بالوهم، ورفضوا الدلائل العقلية، وفتحوا على المعطلة أوسع باب لنفي الصانع، وذلك لأنهم لم ينقادوا للدلائل العقلية

Commentary
This passage is a criticism of opponents. The author states that these opponents abandoned the absolute cause (al-illa al-mutlaqa) related to vision (ar-ru'ya) and instead clung to coincidental indications (al-qara'in al-ittifaqiyya) and illusion (al-wahm). They rejected rational proofs (ad-dala'il al-aqliyya), thereby opening the widest door for those who negate the existence of the Creator (al-mu'attila). The reason for this error is their refusal to submit to rational proofs.

Nasafi's Refutation Announced
Nasafi announces his refutation plan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key elements of al-Nasafi's refutation strategy, including his critique of opponents' scriptural understanding and rational arguments about accidents and causality.

Core Idea
Nasafi refutes opponents by showing their scriptural weakness and rational contradictions.

Al-Nasafi now turns from exposition to refutation. In this passage he announces his method: he will show that the opponents' understanding of Quranic verses is deficient, that their rational arguments are self-contradictory, and that key theological concepts like the permanence of accidents and the relationship between capacity (istita'a) and action support his positions. This passage sets the agenda for the detailed arguments to follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall announcement.
Notice this is a single, dense sentence in Arabic.
2
Identify the five criticisms
The passage lists five ways Nasafi will attack opponents: (1) invalidating views, (2) showing deficient Quranic understanding, (3) exposing rational contradictions, (4) proving accidents cannot endure, (5) explaining the cause-effect relation of istita'a and act.
3
Reflect on the strategy
Consider how each point targets a different aspect: scriptural, rational, metaphysical. Ask yourself why Nasafi begins with this summary.
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Focus on causality point
Pay special attention to the claim that istita'a is like a cause and the act its effect. This is a key Maturidi doctrine that will be argued in detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يبدأ النسفي في مناقشة الخصوم مناقشة تبطل آراءهم، وتبين أن فهمهم للآيات قاصر، وأن شبههم العقلية تدل على تناقضهم، وأن الأعراض يستحيل بقاؤها ، وأن الاستطاعة تجري مع الفعل مجرى العلة من المعلول، وخلو العلة عن المعلول ممتنع.
Then al-Nasafī begins a discussion with the opponents, a discussion that invalidates their views and shows that their understanding of the verses is deficient, that their rational specious arguments indicate their contradiction, that accidents cannot possibly endure, and that istita‛a runs together with the act in the manner of the cause (‛illa) with the effect (ma‛lūl), and the absence of the cause from the effect is impossible.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a programmatic statement for the coming refutation. Al-Nasafī announces that he will now engage the opponents in a debate that: (1) invalidates their positions; (2) demonstrates their deficient understanding of Qur'anic verses; (3) shows the self-contradictory nature of their rational arguments; (4) establishes that accidents (a‛rāḍ) cannot endure (i.e., they are perishable); and (5) explains that istita‛a (the created capacity) is related to the act as the cause (‛illa) to its effect (ma‛lūl), meaning the cause cannot exist without the effect. This last point supports the view that the second type of istita‛a accompanies the act and cannot precede it, because the cause must be simultaneous with its effect.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: Nasafi also uses rational arguments about the nature of accidents and causal relationships.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi's refutation combines scriptural critique with rational arguments, establishing that accidents are perishable and istita‛a is inseparable from the act.

Two Aspects of Unrestricted Application in Theological Discussion
Nasafi states Abu Mansur's two aspects and links to the issue of creation of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two contextual rules Abu Mansur specifies for using the term 'إطلاق' in relation to will, and how this connects to the broader doctrine of the creation of actions.

Core Idea
Two contextual rules for using 'will' in theological discussion.

In this passage, Nasafi presents Abu Mansur's clarification on the use of the term 'إطلاق' (unrestricted application) concerning the concept of will. He distinguishes two situations: when the meaning is clear, the term can be used freely; when the questioner's intent is unclear, it must be avoided. This methodological precision then links to the established proof for the creation of human actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the full translation to grasp the author's meaning.
Pay attention to the terms 'إطلاق' and 'خلق أفعال العباد'.
2
Identify the two aspects
Find the two aspects Abu Mansur specifies. Notice the condition that distinguishes them: understanding the questioner's intent.
The first aspect permits unrestricted use when the meaning is understood; the second forbids it when intent is unclear.
3
Understand the link to the creation of actions
Notice how the author states that the same proof for the creation of actions applies here. This shows consistency in theological method.
This connects the methodological rule to a core doctrinal issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا خلاف بينهما، فأبو منصور يحدد في هذه المسألة وجهين: الأول: الإطلاق على المفهوم من الإرادة، والثاني : منع الإطلاق إذا لم يفهم مراد السائل. ثم يقول: إن مسألة خلق أفعال العباد، وقد بينا أن خالقها كلها هو الله تعالى، وما هو دليله هناك، فهو دليله هنا.

Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is an introductory definition and linkage. It begins by affirming that there is no disagreement between Abu Mansur and the author regarding the issue at hand. Abu Mansur then defines two distinct aspects relating to the term 'إطلاق' (unrestricted application) in the context of will. The first aspect permits unrestricted use when the meaning is understood from the word 'will'. The second prohibits that use if the questioner's specific intent is unclear. The author then connects this definition to the well-known argument concerning the creation of human actions (خلق أفعال العباد), insisting that the same proof used for that topic applies directly here, ensuring consistency in the theological method.

Nasafi's Call for Fair Comparison
Nasafi calls for comparing Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna on human acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's methodological call for comparing Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna positions on human actions to discern the truth.

Core Idea
Fair comparison reveals truth, not blind rejection.

In this concluding passage, Nasafi steps back from his polemical arguments and issues a methodological directive. He insists that a fair-minded person must directly compare the Mu'tazila's actual teachings on human acts with those of Ahl al-Sunna. This lesson unpacks that call and its significance in theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Nasafi frames his call for comparison after having already established proofs against the Mu'tazila.
2
Identify the key actors

3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Nasafi insists on comparison after his earlier strong polemics. What does this tell us about his approach to theological debate?
This shows that even when criticizing a sect, one must objectively examine their actual positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد أن يقيم النسفي أكثر من دليل على أن المعتزلة خصماء الله يقول: ليس على المنصف إلا أن يعود إلى كلامهم في أفعال العباد، ويقارنه بكلام أهل السنة ليقف على حقيقة مذهبم.
And after al-Nasafi established more than one proof that the Mu'tazila are enemies of God, he says: The fair-minded person must only return to their words concerning the actions of servants, and compare them with the words of Ahl al-Sunna in order to stand upon the reality of their school.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage serves as a methodological conclusion. Nasafi, after presenting multiple proofs that the Mu'tazila are, in his view, enemies of God (by the criteria of the hadith), emphasizes that a fair person should not stop at mere accusation. Instead, one should examine the Mu'tazila's actual statements on human actions and compare them directly with the position of Ahl al-Sunna. Only through this comparison can the truth about the Mu'tazila school be fully understood. This reflects Nasafi's commitment to scholarly fairness despite his strong critique.

أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nasafi is purely polemical and rejects Mu'tazila without consideration.
Correction: Nasafi calls for fair comparison between Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna to determine the truth.

B) Compare their views with Ahl al-Sunna
C) Consult other sects
D) Rely on hadith only

Nasafi's call for comparison shows that even after strong criticism, he insists on fair examination of the opponent's views.

?

Nasafi on the Qualities of the Imam: Ideals vs. Necessities
Nasafi lists additional qualities and states they are not all mandatory, citing historical precedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the qualities listed by Nasafi for an imam are ideal but not all mandatory, as shown by historical practice after the rightly-guided caliphs.

Core Idea
Leadership qualities are ideals, not absolute requirements.

Nasafi presents a list of desirable qualities for an imam, but immediately clarifies that not all are necessary. He provides historical evidence from the caliphate after the Rashidun. This passage shows the Maturidi emphasis on practicality in leadership qualifications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the qualities
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the seven qualities Nasafi mentions: ijtihad in fiqh, justice, piety, political acumen, knowledge of people's ranks, high intelligence, combination of firmness and gentleness, and bravery.
The list is not exhaustive but represents key virtues.
2
Identify the key condition statement
Focus on Nasafi's statement: 'Their presence all in him is not a condition.' This is the main doctrinal point. Understand that he is not dismissing these qualities but saying they are not all compulsory.
3
Understand the historical evidence
Nasafi cites the caliphate after the rightly-guided caliphs, where individuals who had not reached the level of ijtihad were appointed. This shows that perfect scholarship is not an absolute requirement for leadership.
This reflects the Maturidi principle of considering historical practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ب- يذكر النسفي الأوصاف التي لو توفرت في واحد صار أهلا للإمامة ومنها: أن يكون بلغ مبلغ المجتهدين في الفقه، وأن يكون عدلا ورعا، وأن يكون مهتديا إلى وجه السياسات والتدابير عارفا بمراتب الناس، في قمة الذكاء، جامعا بين الصرامة واللين، وأن يكون شجاعا. ثم يقول: إن وجودها كلها فيه ليس بشرط، والدليل على ذلك أن الخلافة انعقدت بعد الخلفاء الراشدين لمن لم يبلغوا في العلم مبلغ الاجتهاد.
b) Al-Nasafi mentions the attributes which if present in a person make him qualified for the Imamate, and among them: that he should have reached the level of the mujtahids in fiqh, that he be just and pious, that he be guided to the proper policies and measures, knowledgeable of the ranks of people, at the peak of intelligence, combining severity and gentleness, and that he be brave. Then he says: Their presence all in him is not a condition, and the evidence for that is that the caliphate was contracted after the rightly-guided caliphs for those who had not reached the level of ijtihad in knowledge.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage enumerates the desirable qualities for an Imam but then clarifies that they are not all mandatory. Nasafi lists: reaching the level of ijtihad in fiqh (independent reasoning), being just ('adl) and pious (wara'), being guided in politics and administration, knowing the ranks of people, being highly intelligent, combining firmness and gentleness, and being brave. After listing these, he states that having all of them in one person is not a condition (sharṭ). The evidence is historical: after the rightly-guided caliphs (al-khulafā' al-rāshidūn), the caliphate was given to people who were not of the level of ijtihad in knowledge. This shows that leadership does not require perfect scholarship.

Correction: Nasafi argues they are ideal but not all necessary, as shown by historical practice.

Nasafi shows that while an imam should ideally possess many virtues, the actual historical caliphate after the Rashidun proves that not all are necessary.

Commit to one specific change.

Is it possible that you have turned an ideal into an absolute necessity, causing you to dismiss God's appointed leaders unjustly?
