How Is the Imamate Established? Choice, Not Text
Nasafi argues Imamate is by choice, citing Companions' consensus and absence of claimed text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the Imamate is established by choice (ikhtiyar) rather than by explicit divine text (nass), and the evidence he provides from the Companions' actions and the absence of a textual claim.

Core Idea
The Imamate is a public matter decided by choice, not hidden text.

In this passage, Nasafi tackles a central point of Sunni-Shi'a debate: how is the leader of the Muslim community chosen? He argues forcefully that it is through the choice of the community, not through a divinely appointed text. His reasoning rests on the observable behavior of the Prophet's Companions and the logical implications of what would have happened if a text existed.

Guided Path
1
Read Nasafi's Claim and Proofs
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify Nasafi's main claim (Imamate is by choice) and the two main proofs he presents: the actions of the Companions and the conditional argument about 'Ali.
Notice the logical flow: claim → evidence from history → hypothetical reasoning.
2
Analyze the Role of the Companions
Focus on why Nasafi considers the Companions' consensus as strong evidence. Reflect on their high status, adherence to Sharia, and the fact that no one opposed their choice. Why would that be impossible if a text existed?
Nasafi uses the character of the Companions to strengthen his argument.
3
Examine the Conditional Argument
Read the part about 'Ali: if there were a text, 'Ali would have claimed it. Since he didn't, there is no text. Consider why Nasafi specifically mentions 'Ali's courage and truthfulness.
This counters the Shi'a position that 'Ali was the designated successor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسأل النسفي: بأي طريق تثبت؟ ويقول : تثبت بالاختيار لأنها من الأمور العامة، ولو كان فيها نص لاشتهر ولكن لم يحدث هذا بدليل أن الصحابة مع جلالة قدرهم، وتمسكهم بالشريعة اختاروا إمامهم، ولم يعارضهم أحد، وكيف يتصور أن يعرض الصحابة عن النص وهم القمة في الجلال والبعد عن الهوى، وقد أمروا بالمعروف ونهوا عن المنكر، وقد اصطفاهم الرسول عليه السلام لصحبته. ثم لو كان النص ثابتا على أحد لادعاه، وأقام الحجة على القوم، وخاصم من لم يتبعه، وحيث لم يحدث ذلك، إذن فلا نص. وعلى شجاع لا تأخذه في الله لومة لائم، ولا يمكن أن يترك الحق
And al-Nasafi asks: By which method is it established? He says: It is established by choice (ikhtiyār) because it is from the public affairs; and if there had been a text (naṣṣ) regarding it, it would have become well-known, but that did not happen. The evidence is that the Companions, despite their high status and adherence to the Sharia, chose their Imam, and no one opposed them. And how can it be imagined that the Companions would disregard a text while they are the peak of dignity and remoteness from whims, and they enjoined good and forbade evil, and the Messenger chose them for his companionship? Then if the text had been established on someone, he would have claimed it, presented proof to the people, and disputed with those who did not follow him. Since that did not happen, then there is no text. And 'Ali, courageous, did not fear in Allah the blame of the blamer, and he would not abandon the truth...
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage answers the question: by which method (bi-ayy ṭarīq) is the Imamate established? Nasafi states it is established by choice (ikhtiyār) because the Imamate is among the public affairs (al-umūr al-ʿāmma). If there had been a specific text (naṣṣ) designating a successor, it would have become widely known (ishtahara), but that did not happen. The proof (dalīl) is that the Companions (ṣaḥāba), despite their great status (jalāla qadrihim) and strong adherence to the Sharia, chose their Imam (after the Prophet) — referring to the election of Abu Bakr — and no one opposed them (lam yuʿāriḍhum aḥad). Nasafi argues that it is inconceivable that the Companions would disregard a clear text, since they were the highest in dignity (al-qimma fi al-jalāl) and farthest from whims (bu'd 'an al-hawā), and they ordained good and forbade evil, and the Prophet himself chose them for his companionship. He then gives a conditional argument: if there had been a text for someone (like 'Ali), that person would have claimed it, brought evidence against the people, and argued with whoever did not follow him. Since none of that happened, there is no text. The final statement about 'Ali being courageous and not fearing blame reinforces that 'Ali would have spoken up if there were a text.

Key Terms
اختيار
نص
صحابة
اشتهار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that 'Ali's silence during the election of Abu Bakr implies consent to the process, but Nasafi argues that if there were a text for 'Ali, he would have spoken up due to his courage and commitment to truth.
Correction: Nasafi uses 'Ali's silence as evidence that no text existed for him, because 'Ali would not have abandoned the truth.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence Nasafi presents for the Imamate being established by choice rather than by text?
A) The Qur'an explicitly commands election
B) The Companions chose an Imam without opposition, and no text was claimed
C) The Prophet said 'choose your Imam'
D) The Shi'a admit there is no text

Key Takeaway
Nasafi proves that the Imamate is established by choice because the Companions chose their leader without opposition and no designated person claimed a text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi says that if there were a text for a specific leader, that person would have claimed it. Reflect on your own life: Are there truths you believe in but remain silent about because you fear conflict or rejection? How can you be like 'Ali, who 'did not fear in Allah the blame of the blamer'? What would it look like for you to speak up for a truth you know, even if it costs you comfort?
?
The Companions chose their leader based on public consultation. Consider an area of your life where you have authority or influence (e.g., in your family, workplace, or community). Do you make decisions through genuine consultation with others, or do you tend to impose your own will? What would change if you truly adopted the principle of 'ikhtiyar' (choice through consultation) as a model?
?
Nasafi uses the logical test: 'If it had been true, it would have become well-known.' Think of a belief you hold that might not withstand this test. Is there something you accept without evidence simply because it is repeated? How can you apply Nasafi's demand for clear, widespread evidence to strengthen your own faith and understanding?

Reason for Writing and Methodology of the Book
Author explains reason for writing and methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the motivation behind Imam al-Nasafi's writing of this work and the methodological principles he followed, including his choice of a middle path in expression.

Core Idea
Clear proofs, balanced style, and humble reliance on Allah guide this work.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi describes the circumstances that led him to write his book. He responds to the request of friends for clear proofs in creed, drawing upon the methods of earlier Sunni masters. He also explains his deliberate stylistic choice to avoid both excessive detail and excessive brevity, and expresses his humility before Allah. This lesson explores that foundational passage.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the author's words directly. Notice the flow from request to motivation to methodology.
Pay attention to key terms like 'الدلائل' and 'التوسط'.
2
Identify the motive and method
The author states that friends asked him to write clear proofs. He then lists his sources (predecessors of Ahl al-Sunna) and his stylistic approach: a middle path between verbosity (الإطناب) and allusion (الإشارة).
3
Reflect on the humility
The author uses the phrase 'قلة البضاعة' (scantiness of goods) as a form of humility, not a literal claim of ignorance. This is a common scholarly trope.
This connects to the misconception field below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أصدقائي طلبوا مني أن أكتب لهم ما جل من الدلائل في المسائل الاعتقادية، وأبين ما كان يعتمد عليه من سلف من مشايخ أهل السنة والجماعة قدس الله أرواحهم لنصرة مذاهبهم وإبطال مذاهب خصومهم، من المعاني الجلية، والنكت القوية. معرضا عن الاشتغال بإيراد ما دق من الدلائل، ولطف من المسائل، سالكا طريقة التوسط في العبارة بين الإطناب، والإشارة، لتعم الفائدة، وتتوفر العائدة، ولا يتعذر على من أراد مطالعة ما فيه، الوقوف على ما تضمنت ألفاظه من معانيه، فحملني على الإقدام على المطلوب ما أنا مجبول عليه من إيثار الإسعاف، وإن كان يصدني عنه مر الإنصاف القلة البضاعة، بل لخروجي عن أهل الصناعة، والله كافي من استكفاه، ومعين من توكل عليه، في أمور دينه ودنياه، وهو حسبنا ونعم الوكيل.
Indeed, my friends asked me to write for them the clear proofs regarding creedal issues, and to explain what the predecessors among the masters of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a (may Allah sanctify their souls) relied upon for the support of their schools and the refutation of the schools of their opponents, from the clear meanings and strong points. While turning away from occupying myself with presenting the subtle proofs and delicate issues, following a middle path in expression between verbosity and allusion, so that the benefit may be general and the return may be abundant, and it may not be difficult for whoever wishes to study what is in it to grasp the meanings contained in its words. So what drove me to undertake what was requested was my inborn inclination to prefer fulfilling requests, even though the bitterness of justice (or fairness) due to lack of knowledge (or: the scantiness of my goods) would have deterred me, nay, because of my being outside the people of this craft. And Allah suffices those who seek sufficiency from Him, and He is the Helper of those who trust in Him, in the matters of their religion and their world, and He is our Sufficiency and the Best Disposer of affairs.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage explains the reason for writing the book: friends asked for clear proofs in creed. The author states his methodology: avoiding overly subtle proofs, using a middle style between verbosity and brevity, so as to be accessible. He then cites his natural inclination to help as his motive, despite feeling inadequacy. He ends with reliance on Allah.

Key Terms

المسائل الاعتقادية
مشايخ أهل السنة
التوسط
الإطناب
الإشارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'قلة البضاعة' is often taken literally to mean the author is ignorant or unqualified.
Correction: It is a conventional expression of humility (tawāḍuʿ) common among Islamic scholars, not a factual claim about knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Verbose
B) Allusive
C) Middle path between verbosity and allusion
D) Very brief

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Nasafi wrote this book in response to a request for clear proofs, adopting a balanced style and expressing humility, all while placing his trust in Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
His friends requested this book. Who around you is in need of clear guidance on creed? What are you doing to respond to that need, even if you feel inadequate, trusting that Allah is your Helper?

Nasafi's Balanced Methodology
Nasafi explains his balanced methodology and humble approach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's methodological approach in Tabsirat al-Adilla, specifically his balanced style between verbosity and allusion, and his humble disclaimer of his own competence.

Core Idea
Nasafi's 'middle path' makes theology accessible without sacrificing depth.

This passage serves as Nasafi's methodological preface to Tabsirat al-Adilla. He outlines his stylistic choices and intentions, emphasizing balance and accessibility. Understanding this orientation helps readers approach the work with the right expectations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with reverence
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the opening invocation and the author's tone of humility.
This is a conventional scholarly opening.
2
Identify the three pillars of his method
Find where he states his avoidance of subtle proofs, his middle path in expression, and the goal of widespread benefit.
These show his pedagogical intention.
3
Reflect on the humility statement
Consider why Nasafi admits 'scant knowledge' and how this is a conventional trope, not an actual confession of incompetence.
Humility is common in Islamic scholarly prefaces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدس الله أرواحهم لنصرة مذاهبهم وإبطال مذاهب خصومهم، من المعاني الجلية، والنكت القوية. معرضا عن الاشتغال بإيراد ما دق من الدلائل، ولطف من المسائل، سالكا طريقة التوسط في العبارة بين الإطناب، والإشارة، لتعم الفائدة، وتتوفر العائدة، ولا يتعذر على من أراد مطالعة ما فيه، الوقوف على ما تضمنت ألفاظه من معانيه، فحملني على الإقدام على المطلوب ما أنا مجبول عليه من إيثار الإسعاف، وإن كان يصدني عنه مر الإنصاف القلة البضاعة، بل لخروجي عن أهل الصناعة، والله كافي من استكفاه، ومعين من توكل عليه، في أمور دينه ودنياه، وهو حسبنا ونعم الوكيل.
May God sanctify their souls for supporting their doctrines and invalidating the doctrines of their opponents, from the clear meanings and strong points. Turning away from occupying oneself with presenting subtle proofs and delicate issues, following a middle path in expression between verbosity and allusion, so that benefit is general and advantage is abundant, and one who wishes to study what is in it is not prevented from grasping what its expressions contain of meanings. So what drove me to undertake the desired work is my innate preference for giving aid, even though the bitterness of fairness in my scant knowledge deters me, rather my lack of being among the people of this craft. But God suffices for whoever seeks His sufficiency, and is the helper of whoever relies on Him in the matters of his religion and his world. He is sufficient for us and the best Disposer of affairs.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a methodological preface in which Nasafi describes his approach in Tabsirat al-Adilla. Он opens with an invocation for the early masters (the 'souls' of preceding theologians). He then states his stylistic intention: he avoids delving into extremely abstruse proofs or overly subtle questions, instead adopting a middle path between excessive elaboration and mere hints. The goal is to make the work widely beneficial and accessible, so that readers can grasp the intended meanings without difficulty. He admits his own shortcomings—the 'scantiness of his goods' and his being 'outside the people of this craft'—as a humble disclaimer, but reaffirms his reliance on God's sufficiency. This humility is consistent with traditional scholarly modesty. The passage serves as an orienting statement for the reader, setting expectations for the work's style and purpose.

Key Terms
توسط
عبارة
إطناب
إشارة
فائدة
عائدة
بضاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Nasafi's admission of 'scant knowledge' means he was not qualified to write this work.
Correction: This is a conventional expression of humility common among classical scholars, not an actual confession of incompetence.

Checkpoint
What kind of style does Nasafi adopt in Tabsirat al-Adilla according to this passage?
A) He focuses only on the most difficult proofs and questions.
B) He follows a middle path between verbosity and allusion.
C) He writes in an extremely concise and allusive manner.
D) He provides lengthy, exhaustive explanations for every point.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi deliberately adopts a balanced, accessible style in Tabsirat al-Adilla, avoiding extremes of verbosity or conciseness, and humbly relies on God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi deliberately avoids subtle proofs to make his work widely beneficial. In your interactions with others about Islam, do you sometimes prioritize impressing them with deep knowledge over making the message clear and accessible? How can you adopt his 'middle path' to better serve those around you?
?
He admits his 'scant knowledge' and being 'outside the people of this craft,' yet he proceeds, relying on God. When you feel inadequate in your understanding of faith or in teaching others, do you let that prevent you from acting, or do you entrust the matter to God and move forward humbly? What concrete step can you take this week to rely on God while stepping out in service?
?
The goal of his work is that 'one who wishes to study is not prevented from grasping meanings.' Do you make an effort to present Islamic knowledge in a way that removes obstacles for learners, or do you inadvertently make it harder? What is one change you can make to your approach to ensure others can grasp what you share?

Critique of Dividing Knowledge: Conditions of a Proper Definition
Critique of dividing knowledge; conditions of a proper definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a definition of knowledge cannot be given by simply dividing it into types, and you will learn the logical conditions of a sound definition in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
A definition must cover all instances; dividing knowledge is not defining it.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi critiques a common approach to defining knowledge that merely divides it into necessary and acquired. He argues that this is not a true definition because it fails to include all individuals of the defined. Instead, he lays out the rigorous conditions a definition must satisfy: universality and convertibility, which ensure comprehensiveness and exclusiveness. Understanding these conditions is essential for any student of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very dense argumentative style.
2
Identify the author's main claim
The author states that the so-called definition is actually a division (taqsīm) of knowledge, not a proper definition. Find this statement.
The phrase 'وهذا أيضا فاسد في التحديد إذ هو تقسيم العلم المحدث دون تحديده' introduces the critique.
3
List the conditions of a valid definition
The author mentions two conditions: universality (iṭṭirād) and convertibility (inʿikās). Understand that these ensure comprehensiveness (jamʿ) and exclusiveness (manʿ).
These are logical terms used in definition theory.
4
Understand the test applied to the divided definition
The author gives an example: if knowledge is defined as 'belief about something as it is by necessity', then inferential knowledge would be excluded. See how this invalidates the definition.
The same test applies to defining knowledge by inference.
5
Conclude why the divided definition fails
The definition fails because it does not cover all instances of knowledge; it either excludes some (so it's not universal) or includes non-knowledge (if it's not exclusive).
This shows the importance of proper definition in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا أيضا فاسد في التحديد إذ هو تقسيم العلم المحدث دون تحديده، لأن من شرط صحة التحديد أن يوجد جميع صفات الحد في كل فرد من أفراد المحدود إذ من شرطه الاطراد والانعكاس؛ ليحصل بهما الجمع والمنع إذ الحد ما يجمع جميع محدوده ويمنع غيره عن مشاركته، ولن يحصل هذا إلا باشتمال الحد أفراد المحدود، وفيما قسم لا يوجد هذا المعنى فإن علما ما لا يكون على جميع عن الضرورة والاستدلال لا يكون ضروريا، فلو كان اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به عن ضرورة عملا لأنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به عن ضرورة لخرج الاستدلالي عن كونه علما، لخروجه عن الحد، ولو بقي علما مع خروجه عن الحد لبطل الحد، لخروجه عن أن يكون جامعا. وكذا هذا الاعتبار في العلم الاستدلالي
This too is invalid in definition because it is a division of temporal knowledge without defining it. For from the conditions of the validity of definition is that all the attributes of the definition exist in every individual of the defined, since from its conditions are universality (iṭṭirād) and convertibility (inʿikās); so that by them comprehensiveness (jamʿ) and exclusiveness (manʿ) are achieved. For the definition is that which includes all of its defined and prevents others from sharing in it. And this cannot be obtained except by the definition including the individuals of the defined. And in that which is divided, this meaning does not exist. For knowledge that is not based on both necessity and inference cannot be necessary. So if it were belief about something as it is by necessity – because it is belief about something as it is by necessity – then inferential knowledge would be excluded from being knowledge, due to its exiting from the definition. And if it remained knowledge while exiting from the definition, the definition would be invalidated, due to its failure to be comprehensive. And the same consideration applies to inferential knowledge.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a definition of knowledge that divides it into necessary and acquired. The author first states that such a 'definition' is actually a division (taqsīm) of knowledge, not a proper definition. He then explains the conditions of a sound definition: it must be universal (iṭṭirād) and convertible (inʿikās), meaning that every individual of the defined must be covered and nothing else enters. This achieves comprehensiveness (jamʿ) and exclusiveness (manʿ). The author argues that in a divided definition, these conditions are not met. He gives an example: if knowledge is defined as 'belief about something as it is by necessity', then acquired (inferential) knowledge would not be included, so either it is not knowledge (contradicting common consensus) or the definition fails to be comprehensive. Thus the divided definition is invalid.

التحديد

الاطراد
الانعكاس
الجمع
المنع
المحدود
الضرورة
الاستدلال
الاعتقاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can define knowledge by listing its types (necessary and acquired).
Correction: A proper definition must be universal and convertible, covering all instances uniformly; a division fails to meet these conditions.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the primary flaw in defining knowledge as divided into necessary and acquired?
A) It is based on weak textual evidence.
B) It is a division, not a definition, and fails to include all instances.
C) It conflates knowledge with belief.
D) It was rejected by the early scholars.

Key Takeaway
A proper definition must be universal and convertible, encompassing all instances and excluding others; dividing knowledge into types does not satisfy this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How can you apply the condition of universality from this lesson to ensure your definition of religious knowledge covers all legitimate sources?
?
The author insists a proper definition must be both comprehensive and exclusive. Is there an area of your life—like your definition of 'righteousness' or 'success'—that is too narrow, excluding aspects you know are important? What concrete step can you take today to broaden that definition to include all its necessary parts?
?
Think of a time you dismissed someone's good deed because it didn't fit your personal 'definition' of charity. How does this lesson's emphasis on universality challenge you to reconsider your standards? What would it mean for you to apply this condition to your judgments of others?

Induction vs. Demonstration: The Logical Distinction
Sanusi explains the difference between induction and demonstration and warns against mixing them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between induction (istiqrāʾ) and demonstration (burhān) and why confusing them is a methodological error.

Core Idea
Induction and demonstration move in opposite directions; mixing them shows ignorance.

In this passage, the author critiques a common logical mistake: treating division and definition as if they belong to the same category. He explains that division (taqsīm) moves from particulars to universals—called induction—while definition (taḥdīd) moves from universals to particulars—called demonstration. Confusing these two reveals a lack of understanding of fundamental realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Notice the terms for division and definition and how they are described.
Pay attention to the contrasting phrases: 'من الكليات بواسطة الجزئيات' and 'من الجزئيات بوساطة الكليات'.
2
Identify the two methods
Identify the two logical methods: division (for universals via particulars) and definition (for particulars via universals). Note their technical names: istiqrāʾ and burhān.
3
Reflect on the warning
Consider why the author says that whoever lumps them together has little knowledge of realities. How would this error affect theological reasoning?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلالي، والذي يدل على بطلان الحدود المقسمة، أن التقسيم وضع لمعرفة الكليات بواسطة الجزئيات، ويسمى هذا عند أهل المنطق استقراء، والتحديد وضع لمعرفة الجزئيات بوساطة الكليات، ويسمى هذا برهانا، فمن جعل الأمرين بابا واحدا فهو قليل الحظ من العلم بالحقائق. ثم الذي يبطل الحدود الثلاثة أنها علقت بالشيء ولن يستقيم هذا الأمر إلا بأحد أمرين: إما أن يجعل المعدوم شيئا لكونه معلوما كما هو مذهب المعتزلة، وإما أن يكون المعدوم غير معلوم كما هو مذهب هشام بن عمرو وقيام الدلالة على بطلان المذهبين جميعا، يبطل هذا التحديد ونقيم الدلالة بعد هذا على بطلانهما - إن شاء الله تعالى
The [statement] that indicates the invalidity of the divided definitions is that division (al-taqsīm) is established for knowing universals by means of particulars, and this is called by the people of logic induction (istiqrāʾ); and definition (al-taḥdīd) is established for knowing particulars by means of universals, and this is called demonstration (burhān). Whoever makes the two matters one door has little share of knowledge of realities. Then what invalidates the three definitions is that they are attached to the thing, and this matter will not be correct except by one of two matters: either that the non-existent (al-maʿdūm) is made a thing because it is known, as is the doctrine of the Muʿtazila, or that the non-existent is not known, as is the doctrine of Hishām ibn ʿAmr. And the evidence for the invalidity of both doctrines [being established], this definition is invalidated, and we will establish the evidence after this for their invalidity—if God wills.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the method of defining knowledge through division. The author distinguishes between two logical methods: division (تقسيم), which moves from particulars to universals and is called induction (استقراء), and definition (تحديد), which moves from universals to particulars and is called demonstration (برهان). Mixing these two methods into one category indicates ignorance of true realities. Then the author points out that the three definitions (presumably of knowledge) are attached to the thing (الشيء), but this can only be correct if one resolves the issue of whether the non-existent (المعدوم) is a thing. He presents two opposing views: the Muʿtazila hold that the non-existent is a thing because it is known, while Hishām ibn ʿAmr holds that the non-existent is not known. The author declares that both views are invalid and that evidence will be provided later, thus invalidating the definitions based on this dichotomy.

Key Terms
تقسيم
تحديد
استقراء
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That division and definition are the same logical process.
Correction: They are opposite: division induces universals from particulars, while definition deduces particulars from universals.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the purpose of division (taqsīm)?
A) To know particulars through universals
B) To know universals through particulars
C) To demonstrate the existence of God
D) To combine induction and demonstration

Key Takeaway
Division and definition are distinct logical methods with opposite directions; mixing them indicates ignorance of realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about a difficult topic in your faith, do you tend to start from general principles (like 'God is just') and then apply them to specific cases, or do you first gather examples and then generalize? Which approach does this lesson suggest is correct for each task, and how might using the wrong one lead you to error?
?
The author warns that mixing induction and demonstration shows 'little share of knowledge of realities.' In your own study of Islamic knowledge, have you ever confused the method of defining terms with the method of categorizing them? How can you intentionally practice using the right method for the right purpose starting today?
?
If someone tells you that they 'proved' something about God using only examples from nature, which method are they likely using? Is that sufficient? What does this lesson demand you check in your own reasoning to avoid being deceived by flawed logic?

The Dilemma of the Non-Existent: Critique of Mu'tazila and Hisham
Sanusi raises the dilemma of the non-existent and rejects both opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author rejects both the Mu'tazilite view that the non-existent is a thing and Hisham ibn Amr's view that it is not known, and how this dilemma invalidates certain definitions.

Core Idea
The non-existent cannot be both a thing and unknown; both positions are invalid.

The author now turns to a major theological and logical problem: if definitions rely on the concept of 'thing', they must account for the non-existent. Two opposing views exist: the Mu'tazila say the non-existent is a thing because it is known, while Hishām ibn ʿAmr says it is not known. The author declares both false and uses this to refute the definitions based on this dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Identify the two conflicting positions and note how they are linked to the definitions.
The phrase 'بأحد أمرين' introduces the two possibilities.
2
Analyze the two views
Compare the Mu'tazilite view (non-existent is a thing because known) with Hisham's view (non-existent is not known). Consider the implications for defining knowledge.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author says the invalidity of both views is established, so the definition itself fails. Why does this dilemma invalidate the definition?
Think about how the definition's reliance on 'thing' becomes unstable.

The Foundation of Sound Debate: Consensus on Knowledge
Establishes the consensus of rational people on the reality of knowledge, refuting ancient skeptics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the consensus of rational people on the reality of knowledge is the necessary foundation for any meaningful theological debate.

Core Idea
Without shared belief in truth, no debate is possible.

This passage lays the groundwork for all future theological discussion. Imam al-Nasafi asserts that rational people universally agree that knowledge and truth exist. He then identifies the one group that rejected this—the ancient skeptics—and explains why no rational debate can occur with them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The Arabic text is slightly truncated at the end; the argument is still clear.
2
Identify the two consensus claims
Notice that the passage contains two distinct consensuses: first, on the existence of knowledge and truths; second, on the impossibility of debating those who deny this.
The second consensus is a direct consequence of the first.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does denying the reality of knowledge make debate impossible? The commentary will help you answer.
This connects to the core idea of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إثبات الحقائق والعلوم: أسس المناظرة السليمة] وإذا عرف حد العلم وحقيقته نقول : أجمع العقلاء على ثبوت العلم والحقائق للأشياء، سوى طائفة من الأوائل تجاهلت ورضيت لنفسها رتبة تستنكف البهائم عنها، فزعمت أن لا حقيقة لشيء ولا علم بشيء، وإنما هي ظنون.. وحسبانات. وأجمع العقلاء على أن لا مناظرة بيننا وبين من هذا قوله لأن بيننا
Speech on establishing truths and sciences: The foundations of sound debate. And when the definition and reality of knowledge are known, we say: The rational people are unanimously agreed on the existence of knowledge and truths for things, except a group of the ancients who feigned ignorance and chose for themselves a rank from which even beasts would disdain. They claimed that there is no reality for anything and no knowledge of anything, but only opinions and conjectures. And the rational people are unanimously agreed that there is no debate between us and one who says this, because between us
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage discusses the foundations of sound debate (al-munāẓara al-salīma). Once the definition and reality of knowledge are known, the author asserts a consensus (ijmā') of all rational people ('uqalā') on the existence (thubūt) of knowledge and truths (ḥaqā'iq) for things. The only exception is a group of ancient skeptics (ṭā'ifa min al-awā'il) who feigned ignorance and chose a rank that even beasts would disdain. They claimed that there is no reality to anything and no knowledge, only opinions and conjectures (ẓunūn wa ḥusbānāt). The author then states that the rational people are unanimously agreed that no debate is possible between us and such a person, because (the sentence cuts off, but the implied reason is that they deny the very basis of knowledge and truth). The passage establishes the methodological principle that meaningful debate requires a shared acceptance of the reality of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ask yourself today: am I pretending not to know something that Allah has made evident? Decide to stop hiding behind false ignorance and embrace the truth, even if it demands change.
?
This lesson establishes that sound debate requires a shared commitment to reality. Consider a disagreement you currently have with a family member or friend about religion. Does that person accept that objective truth exists? If not, does the passage give you permission to disengage from endless argument? Reflect on whether your efforts are productive or whether you are violating the principle of no debate with those who deny truth. Make a concrete plan to either redirect the conversation toward foundational beliefs or to peacefully withdraw.

The Benefit and Conditions of Valid Debate
Nasafi defines debate's purpose and outlines its prerequisites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's definition of the benefit of debate and the necessary conditions for a debate to be valid, including the role of agreed principles and causal attributes.

Core Idea
Debate requires shared principles; it cannot convince the willfully stubborn.

Imam Nasafi opens his discussion of debate by clarifying its purpose: to establish truth over falsehood through proof. But he immediately notes that not everyone is capable of being reasoned with. In this passage, he explains both the aim of debate and the essential conditions that must be met for it to be meaningful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm, then read the literal translation. Note that Nasafi uses technical terms like 'attribute of the cause' (وصف العلة).
2
Identify the two parts of the passage
The passage has two main moves: first, defining the benefit of debate and the problem of stubborn opponents; second, explaining the structure of a valid debate with agreed principles.
3
Reflect on the conditions for debate
Ask yourself: why does Nasafi insist on agreed-upon principles? What happens if the opponent denies all axioms? This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة المناظرة أن يثبت بالدلائل صحة قول وبطلان قول آخر، والعلم الحاصل عن النظر في الدلائل وإن كان يبلغ النهاية في القوة فطريقه أخفى من طريق علم الحواس والبداهة، ومن بلغ في الوقاحة والعناد مبلغاً لا يبالي من إنكار ما يثبت من العلوم والحقائق بالحواس والبداهة في العقول، لا يرجى منه قبول العلم الثابت بالاستدلال، ولأن المناظرة تكون بين اثنين بينهما أصول مسلمة حكمها الإثبات، وأصول آخر مسلمة حكمها النفي، ووجد فرع له شبه بكلام النوعين في الأصول بوجه من الوجوه، فيختلف اثنان أن إلحاقه بأي الأصلين أولى، وشبهه بأيهما كان بوصف العلة، وبأيهما كان بوصف الوجود، أعني ما وجد اتفاقا من غير أن يكون علة الحكم الثابت في العلم فيناظران ليظهر وصف العلة من وصف الوجود الواقع اتفاقا فيلحق بالأصل الذي يشاركه في وصف علة الحكم الثابت فيه، وإذا لم يكن لهؤلاء المتجاهلة أصل مجمع عليه لا يتصور مناظرتهم.
The benefit of debate is that it establishes, by proofs, the correctness of one statement and the falsity of another. And the knowledge obtained from reflection upon proofs, even if it reaches the ultimate in strength, its path is more hidden than the path of sensory and intuitive knowledge. And one who has reached such a level of insolence and stubbornness that he does not care to deny what is established of sciences and realities by the senses and intuition in intellects, there is no hope of his accepting knowledge established by inference. And because debate takes place between two persons who have between them agreed principles whose ruling is affirmation, and other agreed principles whose ruling is negation, and there is found a branch that has a resemblance to both types of principles in some respect, then two disagree about which of the two principles it is more appropriate to attach it to, and its resemblance to either of them is by the attribute of the cause, and by either of them is by the attribute of existence, i.e., what occurs by chance without being the cause of the ruling established in knowledge. So they debate to make clear the attribute of the cause from the attribute of existence that occurs by chance, so that it is attached to the principle that shares with it the attribute of the cause of the ruling established therein. And if these ignorant ones have no agreed-upon principle, their debate is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines the benefit of debate and outlines the conditions under which debate is valid. It begins by stating that the benefit of debate is to establish, by proofs, the truth of one statement and the falsehood of another. Then it notes that even if the knowledge from reflection is very strong, its path is more hidden than sensory or intuitive knowledge, and that those who are extremely stubborn and deny even sensory truths cannot be expected to accept rational proofs. The author then explains the structure of a proper debate: two parties must have agreed principles that serve as premises, some affirming and some negating. When a particular case (branch) resembles both sets of principles, the debaters disagree about which principle it should be attached to. The attachment is based on whether the resemblance is due to the attribute of the cause or the attribute of accidental existence. The purpose of the debate is to distinguish the true cause from accidental attributes, so that the branch is attached to the principle that shares the relevant causal attribute. Finally, the author concludes that if the opponents have no agreed-upon principle, debate with them is impossible.

Key Terms
فائدة المناظرة
أصول مسلمة
وصف العلة
وصف الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Debate can convince anyone if you argue well enough.
Correction: Nasafi states that a person who denies even sensory truths cannot be expected to accept rational proofs; debate requires shared agreed principles.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what must the two parties in a debate have in order for the debate to be valid?
A) They must have agreed-upon principles (أصول مسلمة).
B) They must have equal knowledge.
C) They must use only sensory evidence.
D) They must have no prior commitments.

Key Takeaway
A valid debate requires both parties to share agreed-upon principles; without them, reasoned discussion is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent disagreement you had. Did you and the other person share any agreed-upon principles? If not, what does Nasafi's condition say about the possibility of that debate? What principle will you seek to establish before your next discussion?
?
When you debate, do you genuinely aim to identify the true cause of the disagreement, or are you more focused on winning? Recall a specific argument: were you more concerned with the attribute of the cause or with incidental points? How can you shift your intention?
?
Nasafi says that someone who denies even sensory facts cannot be convinced by inference. Is there an area where you have been stubborn to the point of denying clear evidence? What would it take for you to accept truth from someone you disagree with?

Refuting the Skeptical Argument from Sensory Contradiction
Nasafi presents and begins refuting the sensory contradiction objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the skeptics' argument that sensory contradictions undermine knowledge and Nasafi's initial refutation that such contradictions presuppose knowledge of realities.

Core Idea
Skeptics rely on the very knowledge they deny.

We now encounter a classic skeptical challenge: if the senses give contradictory reports, how can they be a reliable source of knowledge? Nasafi presents this objection through examples – the jaundiced person tasting honey as bitter, the cross-eyed person seeing one thing as two – and then begins to expose the flaw: the skeptics must already know what the senses are and that they contradict, which itself is knowledge. This lesson unpacks that first move in the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, paying attention to the two examples given (taste and sight) that illustrate the alleged contradiction.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence; we only have the beginning of the refutation.
2
Identify the skeptics' claim
The skeptics argue that because the senses give contradictory judgments, they cannot be a valid cause of knowledge. Notice the strength of their claim: they say the senses are not even suitable evidence, let alone a cause.
Skeptics often push for certainty but deny it from senses.
3
Analyze the beginning of Nasafi's response
Nasafi points out that the very articulation of the doubt shows the skeptics know realities. They must know what senses are, that their judgments contradict, and that contradiction disqualifies them as evidence. This self-refutation is the key.
The refutation is incomplete in the text, but the direction is clear.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: can the skeptics consistently deny sensory knowledge while using it to construct their argument? This lesson highlights the problem of self-defeating skepticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبهتهم أن أعلا أسباب العلم عندكم الحواس الخمس، وهي لا تصلح سببا له، لأن قضاياها متناقضة، فإن الممرور يجد العسل مرا، وغيره يجده حلوا، والأحول يرى الشيء شيئين، وغيره يراه واحدا وذلك كله عمل الحس، وما تناقضت قضاياه، كل هذا التناقض لا يصلح دليلا لشيء، فضلا عن أن يكون سببا مثبتا. وهذه الشبهة تدل أنهم يعلمون الحقائق ويثبتونها، غير أنهم يعاندون فإنهم لو لم يعرفوا الحواس أنها ما هي وأن قضاياها متناقضة وأن ما يتناقض قضاياه لا

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from skeptics against the reliability of the senses as a source of knowledge, and begins a refutation. The objection is that the senses are supposed to be the highest source of knowledge, but they are unreliable because their judgments are contradictory. Examples include the jaundiced person tasting honey as bitter while others taste it as sweet, and the cross-eyed person seeing one object as two. The author then starts to refute by noting that this very argument shows that the skeptics do know realities and are merely being obstinate, because they must have knowledge of what the senses are and that their judgments are contradictory. The text cuts off at this point, so the full refutation is not provided.

The First Cause of Knowledge: The Sound Senses
Al-Nasafi enumerates the sound senses as the first cause of knowledge.

Core Idea
The sound senses are the first gateway to all knowledge.

Al-Nasafi begins his discussion on the causes of knowledge by establishing that knowledge has specific means. After affirming the reality of knowledge, he declares that the first of these means is the sound senses. The passage then lists the senses, setting the stage for the other causes to follow.

The Three Causes of Knowledge
Nasafi enumerates the three causes of knowledge with a logical rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three causes of knowledge according to Imam Nasafi and the logical rationale for why they are exhaustive.

Core Idea
All knowledge comes from three sources: the senses, truthful report, and intellect.

Imam Nasafi begins by affirming that knowledge and realities are established, then presents a foundational epistemology for kalam. He enumerates three causes of knowledge and provides a logical principle that divides all possible sources into these three categories. This classification is crucial for understanding how theologians argue and what counts as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the three causes listed: sound senses, truthful report, and intellect.
The senses are explicitly listed as five.
2
Understand the rationale
The passage then gives a logical reason for why these three are exhaustive. It uses a tripartite division based on whether the cause is external, an instrument, or the mind itself.
3
Reflect on completeness
Consider why this classification covers all possible ways of knowing. This is a key epistemological claim in kalam.
This connects to later discussions about knowledge of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت الحقائق والعلوم فنقول : إن أسباب العلم وطرقه ثلاثة، أحدها: الحواس السليمة وهي: حاسة السمع، وحاسة البصر، وحاسة الشم، وحاسة الذوق، وحاسة اللمس. والثاني: خبر الصادق. والثالث: العقل. ووجه الحصر أن السبب إن كان من خارج فالخبر الصادق، وإلا فإن كان آلة غير المدرك فالحواس وإلا فالعقل.
And when realities and sciences are established, we say: Indeed the causes of knowledge and its ways are three: one of them is the sound senses, which are: the sense of hearing, the sense of sight, the sense of smell, the sense of taste, and the sense of touch. The second is the truthful report. The third is the intellect. And the reason for the enumeration is that if the cause is external, then it is the truthful report; otherwise, if it is an instrument other than the perceiver, then it is the senses; otherwise, it is the intellect.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the causes of knowledge. The author first affirms the reality of things and knowledge, then enumerates three sources: (1) the five sound senses, (2) truthful report (i.e., reliable testimony or revelation), and (3) intellect. The author then provides a rationale for why these three are exhaustive: if the cause is external to the knower, it is report; if it is an instrument of perception distinct from the mind, it is the senses; otherwise, it is the intellect itself. This division covers all possible sources of knowledge.

Key Terms
أسباب العلم
الحواس السليمة
خبر الصادق
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three causes are separate and unrelated ways of knowing that conflict with each other.
Correction: They are complementary and cover all knowledge: senses perceive particulars, reports transmit knowledge from others, and intellect abstracts universals.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which cause of knowledge is classified as external?
A) The senses
B) The intellect
C) The truthful report
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The three causes of knowledge—senses, truthful report, and intellect—exhaust all possible sources of knowledge according to a logical division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you hear a 'truthful report'—whether from a scholar or a news source—do you critically evaluate it using your intellect and senses, or do you accept it passively? How can you better integrate all three causes?
?
Your intellect can perceive truths, but have you ever rejected a clear rational truth because it conflicted with your desires? Will you commit to following your intellect when it points to the truth, even if it is uncomfortable?

Objection Against Report and Its Self-Refutation
Nasafi presents a skeptic's objection and refutes it via self-refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the skeptics' denial of report as a source of knowledge is refuted through a self-refutation argument.

Core Idea
The skeptic's claim that report is unreliable is itself a report.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi engages with a fundamental epistemological challenge: if reports can be true or false, how can we trust them? The skeptics conclude that report cannot yield knowledge. But Nasafi shows that this very conclusion is a report, making the skeptics' position self-defeating. Let us follow the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the objection, then the refutation.
2
Identify the skeptics' claim
What is the skeptics' argument against report? They say it can be true or false, so we cannot know which is which, so knowledge is not established.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation points out that the skeptics' own statement is a report. By making that claim, they rely on the very thing they deny, making their argument self-refuting.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما الكلام في الخبر فهم يقولون : إنه قد يكون صدقا، وقد يكون كذبا. فكان في نفسه مختلفا، ولا ندري الصدق من الكذب، فلا يثبت به العلم . فيقال لهم: قولكم إن الخبر ليس من أسباب المعارف خبر منكم ، وقد أقررتهم ببطلان الخبر، فكان هذا إقرارا ببطلان مقالتكم.
As for the discussion concerning report, they say: It may be true, and it may be false. Thus it is in itself variable, and we do not know the true from the false, so knowledge is not established by it. So it is said to them: Your statement that report is not among the causes of knowledge is a report from you, and you have admitted the falsehood of report, so this is an admission of the falsehood of your own statement.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the validity of report as a source of knowledge and its refutation. The skeptics argue that since reports can be either true or false, we cannot distinguish them, so report cannot yield certainty. The refutation is a classic self-refutation argument: the skeptics' own claim that 'report is not a source of knowledge' is itself a report. By asserting it, they rely on the very thing they deny. Thus, their statement is self-defeating and constitutes an admission that their position is false.

Key Terms
الخبر
الصدق
الكذب
العلم
إقرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This refutation dismisses all forms of skepticism about knowledge.
Correction: The refutation only shows that the specific argument—that report cannot yield knowledge because it may be false—is self-refuting. It does not address other forms of skepticism.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the skeptics' denial of report as a source of knowledge?
A) By citing a revealed text
B) By showing their statement is itself a report
C) By appealing to sense experience
D) By using an analogy

Key Takeaway
The skeptics' denial of report as knowledge is self-refuting because their own statement is a report.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a claim that 'you can't trust anything,' have you ever stopped to ask whether that claim itself should be trusted? How does this passage force you to examine the consistency of your own doubts?
?
Consider a report you rely on daily—like news or someone's testimony. What would it mean for your life if you treated all such reports as unreliable? How does this lesson challenge you to critically assess your sources without falling into wholesale skepticism?
?
Have you ever dismissed an entire category of knowledge because of occasional falsehoods? What is your personal responsibility to seek truth in reports, even when errors exist?

The Three Sources of Knowledge and Groups That Deny Them
Classification of the three sources of knowledge and survey of opposing groups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three sources of knowledge according to Islamic theology and the positions of various groups regarding each source.

Core Idea
Knowledge comes from senses, truthful report, and reason – but not all groups affirm all three.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla enumerates the three fundamental sources of knowledge: the senses, truthful report, and reason. It then surveys various theological and philosophical groups, from the Sophists to the Barāhima, showing which sources they accept or reject. Understanding this classification helps us appreciate the diversity of epistemological views in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice how the author first lists the three sources and then sequentially discusses groups that deny each source.
The passage is dense with group names; don't worry about memorizing all at first.
2
Identify the three sources of knowledge
The passage explicitly states three sources: the sense of touch (as a representative of the senses), truthful report, and reason. Note that the author has already discussed the senses earlier ('قد مر الكلام في الحواس').
3
Map the groups' positions
Track which groups deny which sources: Sophists deny all; Sumaniyya deny report and reason; Barāhima deny report but affirm reason; Mulhida, Rāfiḍa, and some Mushabbiha deny reason.
The Barāhima are specifically noted as affirming reason, distinguishing them from the Sumaniyya.
4
Consider the significance
Ask yourself why the author presents this classification. It establishes a baseline for later arguments about the validity of theological reasoning (naẓar) and the role of revelation.
This connects to the broader debate about the use of reason in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاسة اللمس والثاني: خبر الصادق والثالث : العقل . والسوفسطائية أنكروها كلها، وقد مر الكلام في الحواس. وقد أقر بكون الحواس من أسباب المعارف العقلاء سوى هؤلاء المتجاهلة، ووافقهم في نفي كون الخبر من أسباب جميع المعارف فريقان (من الأوائل) ، أحدهما : السمنية والآخر : البراهمة . ووافقهم في نفي كون العقل من أسباب المعارف السمنية لا غير، وإلى القول ببطلان النظر وخروج العقل من أن يكون من أسباب المعارف ذهبت الملحدة والرافضة وجماعة من المشبهة فأما البراهمة فإنهم يقرون بكون العقل من أسباب المعارف.
And the sense of touch; the second: the truthful report; and the third: reason. The Sophists denied all of them, and the discussion of the senses has already passed. The rational ones have affirmed that the senses are among the causes of knowledge except for these ignorant ones. Two groups (from the ancients) agreed with them in denying that report is among the causes of all knowledge: one of them is the Sumaniyya and the other is the Barāhima. The Sumaniyya alone agreed with them in denying that reason is among the causes of knowledge. As for the opinion of the invalidity of reflection and the exclusion of reason from being among the causes of knowledge, the Mulhida, the Rāfiḍa, and a group of the Mushabbiha adopted it. As for the Barāhima, they affirm that reason is among the causes of knowledge.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage classifies the sources of knowledge (maʿārif) into three: senses (ḥawāss), specifically the sense of touch; truthful report (khabar ṣādiq); and reason (ʿaql). The author then surveys various groups that deny one or more of these sources. The Sophists (al-Sūfisṭāʾiyya) deny all three. Most rational people affirm the senses except for a group the author calls 'ignorant'. Two groups, the Sumaniyya and the Barāhima, deny that report is a source of knowledge. The Sumaniyya alone deny reason, while the Mulhida (atheists), Rāfiḍa (a sect rejecting the first three caliphs), and some Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) hold that reflection (naẓar) is invalid and reason is not a source. The Barāhima, however, affirm reason. Thus the author maps out the various stances on epistemological sources.

Key Terms
المعارف
الحواس
خبر الصادق
العقل
السوفسطائية
السمنية
البراهمة
الملحدة
الرافضة
المشبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All groups deny the same sources of knowledge.
Correction: Different groups deny different sources. For example, the Barāhima deny report but affirm reason, while the Sumaniyya deny both report and reason.

Checkpoint
Which group denies both report and reason as sources of knowledge according to this passage?
A) The Sophists
B) The Sumaniyya
C) The Barāhima
D) The Mulhida

Key Takeaway
The three sources of knowledge – senses, truthful report, and reason – are affirmed by most rational people, but various groups reject one or more, reflecting key epistemological debates in Islamic theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive knowledge from the Quran or authentic hadith, do you ever find yourself subordinating it to your own reasoning or desires? In what specific area of your life do you need to submit your reason to the truthful report and trust Allah's word over your own logic?
?
The Barāhima accept reason but reject revelation. Do you ever act practically as if your reasoning is sufficient without constant reference to divine guidance? Identify one decision you are facing where you must consciously prefer the truthful report over your personal reasoning.
?
The Sophists deny all sources of knowledge and thus deny truth itself. Is there any part of your daily life where you avoid acknowledging the truth because it would demand a change? Ask Allah to give you the courage to accept the truth from all three sources: senses, truthful report, and reason, and to act accordingly.

The Self-Refuting Denial of Report
First part of the refutation, focusing on self-refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the denial of report as a source of knowledge is self-defeating because the denial itself is a report.

Core Idea
Denying report is like sawing off the branch you're sitting on.

The opponents argue that since reports can be false, they cannot be relied upon for knowledge. But their very statement is a report. In this passage, the author exposes the logical flaw: if reports are invalid, so is their denial. This lesson demonstrates the power of self-refutation arguments in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's objection
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the opponents claim report is unreliable because it can be true or false.
2
Identify the refutation
Notice how the author turns the argument back on the opponents: their own statement is a report.
This is a classic self-refutation argument.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: If the denial of report is itself a report, what does that do to the opponent's claim?
The opponent's position becomes untenable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حجتهم في رد الخبر والرد عليهم فأما الكلام في الخبر فهم يقولون : إنه قد يكون صدقا، وقد يكون كذبا. فكان في نفسه مختلفا، ولا ندري الصدق من الكذب، فلا يثبت به العلم . فيقال لهم: قولكم إن الخبر ليس من أسباب المعارف خبر منكم ، وقد أقررتهم ببطلان الخبر، فكان هذا إقرارا ببطلان مقالتكم. ويقال للسمنية منهم : بم عرفتهم أن ما وراء الحواس ليس بحجة، ولا من أسباب المعارف؟ أبالحس عرفتم أم بغير الحس؟ فإن قالوا: عرفنا بالحس، قيل لهم بأي حس عرفتم؟ ويذكر كل حس على حدة ليظهر بطلان دعواهم، ويقال لهم : ما بالنا لا نعرف ذلك بالحس ونحن أرباب الحواس السليمة، ولا يجري الاختلاف في المحسوسات بين أرباب الحواس السليمة؟ وإن قالوا: عرفنا ذلك بغير الحس، فقد أقروا أن شيئا سوى الحواس من أسباب المعارف - ثم نفس الكلام منهم دليل على أنهم عرفوا بالخبر شيئا، لأنهم تكلموا بلغة من اللغات، ومعرفة اللسان واللغة ليست بالحس ولا بالعقل، إذ أوفر خليقة الله عقلا، وأذكاهم حسا، لو سمع لغة لم يتعلمها، لا يعرف معناها، وإنما يعرف ذلك بإخبار الملقن ، ثم بإنكار الخبر تعطيل السمع واللسان وكفران لنعمة الله تعالى بهما، وإلحاق نفسه بالبهائم إذ بالبيان بان الإنسان من الحيوان، ولا يرضى بهذه الرتبة لنفسه مجنون، ولا يقال : إن الخبر إن بطل فسائر أقسام الكلام باق، وهي الاستخبار والأمر والنهي ، لأن هذه الأقسام لا يعرف صيغها إلا بإخبار الملقن، ولأن كل قسم من هذه الأقسام يبطل ببطلان الخبر ، أما الاستخبار فلأن الخبر إذا لم يوجب العلم خرج الاستخبار عن حد الفائدة، والتحق بالعبث، ولأن كل استخبار فيه معنى الخبر لولا هو لبطل الاستخبار، فإنك إذا قلت: هل في الدار زيد؟ تقديره أريد أن تخبرني بكون زيد في الدار إن كان ، وبنفي كونه فيها إن لم يكن، وإذا قلت اسقني تقديره أطلب منك أن تسقيني، وكذا فيه إخبار عن حسن المأمور به، فلو لم يكن هذا الإخبار ثابتا، لخرج الأمر عن أن يكون صادرا عن حكيم، وإذا قال لا تضربني فكأنه قال : أطلب منك أن لا تضربني، وأكره أن تضربني.
Their argument in rejecting report and the refutation of them. As for the discussion of report, they say: It may be true and it may be false. Thus it is in itself variable, and we do not know the truth from the falsehood, so knowledge is not established by it. So it is said to them: Your statement that report is not among the causes of knowledge is a report from you, and you have acknowledged the invalidity of report, so this is an acknowledgment of the invalidity of your statement. And it is said to the Sumaniyya among them: By what did you know that what is beyond the senses is not a proof and is not among the causes of knowledge? Did you know it by sense or by other than sense? If they say: We knew it by sense, it is said to them: By which sense did you know it? Each sense is mentioned individually to show the invalidity of their claim, and it is said to them: Why do we not know that by sense, we who possess sound senses, and there occurs no disagreement about sense-perceptibles among possessors of sound senses? And if they say: We knew it by other than sense, then they have affirmed that something other than the senses is among the causes of knowledge. Then their very speech is evidence that they knew something by report, because they spoke in one of the languages, and knowledge of a tongue and language is not by sense nor by reason; for even the most abundant in intellect and the sharpest in sense among God's creatures, if he hears a language he has not learned, does not know its meaning; rather he knows that only by the report of a teacher. Then by denying report there is the disablement of hearing and speech and ingratitude for God's blessing through them, and bringing oneself down to the level of beasts; for by expression man is distinguished from animal, and no madman would be content with this rank for himself. And it should not be said: If report is invalidated, the other types of speech remain, namely interrogation, command, and prohibition, because these types are not known except by the report of a teacher, and because each of these types is invalidated by the invalidation of report. As for interrogation, because if report does not produce knowledge, interrogation leaves the scope of benefit and becomes absurd; and because every interrogation contains the meaning of report; if it were not for that, interrogation would be invalid. For if you say, 'Is Zayd in the house?', its implied meaning is, 'I want you to inform me of Zayd being in the house if he is, and to inform me of his non-existence if he is not.' And if you say, 'Give me to drink', its implied meaning is, 'I ask you to give me to drink'; similarly it contains a report about the goodness of what is commanded. So if this report were not established, the command would not issue from a wise person. And if one says, 'Do not strike me', it is as if he says, 'I ask you not to strike me, and I dislike that you strike me.'
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage presents the objection raised by those who deny report (khabar) as a source of knowledge and then gives a detailed refutation. The opponents argue that since a report can be true or false, it is variable and we cannot distinguish truth from falsehood, so knowledge cannot be based on it. The refutation first points out that the opponents' own statement ('report is not a source of knowledge') is itself a report; by their own standard, it is unreliable, so their argument is self-refuting. Next, a specific argument is directed to the Sumaniyya, who deny anything beyond the senses. They are asked how they know that what is beyond the senses is not a proof. If they say by sense, they must specify which sense, and a check of each sense shows that none can perceive 'beyond the senses'. If they say by other than sense, they admit a source beyond the senses. Then a further argument is made: the very fact that the opponents speak a language shows they have acquired language through report (from a teacher), not through sense or reason alone. Denying report would thus disable hearing and speech, ingratitude for God's gifts, and reduce humans to animals. Finally, the author refutes the objection that if report is invalidated, other speech acts (interrogation, command, prohibition) remain. He shows that these speech acts also depend on report: interrogation seeks a report, command implies a report about the goodness of the commanded act, etc. Therefore, all speech acts are invalid without report.

Key Terms
الخبر
الصدق
الكذب
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument against report is that it can be false, which seems plausible.
Correction: The refutation shows that the denial of report is itself a report, so the argument is self-refuting.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what is the flaw in the argument that report cannot be a source of knowledge?
A) It assumes all reports are false
B) It is a report itself
C) It ignores sense perception
D) It relies on reason

Key Takeaway
Denying report as a source of knowledge is self-refuting, because the denial itself is a report.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on reports every day—from what you hear in the news to what people tell you. Yet you might doubt a report because it could be false. How does this lesson challenge you to examine the consistency of your own skepticism? What would it mean for your daily life if you truly denied all reports?
?
Think of a time you rejected an idea or claim because you thought it was unreliable. Did you notice that your rejection itself was a claim about the world? How does the self-refutation argument force you to question whether your own standards are applied consistently to yourself?
?
If you believe that some reports are trustworthy (e.g., from teachers or scripture), what evidence do you have for that belief? Does the lesson's argument reveal that the act of believing any report already assumes the validity of report in general? How does that change your approach to seeking knowledge?

Method of Learning

4 min
Senses Beyond Senses and Language Acquisition
Refutation of the Sumaniyya and the argument for report through language.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the Sumaniyya (who deny knowledge beyond the senses) by showing that language itself is acquired through report, not sense or reason alone.

Core Idea
Language proves we rely on report, because we learn it from teachers.

The Sumaniyya argue that only sense perception yields knowledge, rejecting report. The author challenges them: how do you know that what is beyond the senses is invalid? If by sense, which sense? If by other than sense, you admit a non-sensory source. Then he shows that language, which they use, is learned through report, not sense or reason. This lesson explores this crucial argument for the necessity of report.

Guided Path
1
Read the challenge to the Sumaniyya
Read the part where the author asks how they know that beyond-senses is not a proof.
Focus on the logical dilemma.
2
Examine the language argument
Notice the claim that language is known only through report from a teacher, not by sense or reason.
Even the smartest person cannot understand an unknown language without a teacher.
3
Consider the implication
If the Sumaniyya speak a language, they have used report, contradicting their position.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible to know language through reason or sense alone.
Correction: Language is learned through report from others; even the most intelligent cannot understand an unfamiliar language without a teacher.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the Sumaniyya's own speech refute them?
A) They speak a language learned through report
B) They use sense perception to speak
C) They rely on reason alone
D) They admit they are animals

Key Takeaway
Language itself proves the necessity of report, because it is learned only through the report of a teacher, not through sense or reason alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on language every day, yet you never chose it nor invented it. Who taught you your mother tongue? And if you needed a teacher for that, how much more do you need a teacher from Allah for the words of guidance? How grateful are you for the chain of teachers that brought you the Quran?
?
This lesson forces you to admit that your entire worldview rests on testimony. Think of one deeply held belief you thought you arrived at independently. Trace it back—did it come without someone's report? How does this humble you before the need for prophetic revelation?
?
You speak, you argue, you deny—all using language you received from others. If you deny the necessity of report, you deny the very tool you use to deny. Will you now accept that your tongue itself is evidence of your dependence on others? And will that dependence drive you to submit to the ultimate Report—the Quran?

5 min
Why Interrogation, Command, and Prohibition Rely on Report
Refutation of the claim that other speech acts survive the denial of report.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that if report is invalidated, other types of speech (interrogation, command, prohibition) remain valid.

Core Idea
All speech acts ultimately depend on report, so denying report collapses all meaningful communication.

The opponents might concede that report is invalid but argue that other speech acts like asking questions, giving commands, and making prohibitions are still valid. The author demonstrates that these also depend on report, whether explicitly or implicitly. This lesson explores the logical interconnection of speech acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection says: Even if report is invalid, other types like interrogation remain.
2
Understand the refutation for interrogation
The author shows that interrogation seeks a report, and if report is invalid, interrogation becomes pointless.
3
Understand for command and prohibition
Commands and prohibitions contain implicit reports about the goodness or badness of actions.
4
Reflect on the overall point
Without report, no speech act can be meaningful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حجتهم في رد الخبر والرد عليهم فأما الكلام في الخبر فهم يقولون : إنه قد يكون صدقا، وقد يكون كذبا. فكان في نفسه مختلفا، ولا ندري الصدق من الكذب، فلا يثبت به العلم . فيقال لهم: قولكم إن الخبر ليس من أسباب المعارف خبر منكم ، وقد أقررتهم ببطلان الخبر، فكان هذا إقرارا ببطلان مقالتكم. ويقال للسمنية منهم : بم عرفتهم أن ما وراء الحواس ليس بحجة، ولا من أسباب المعارف؟ أبالحس عرفتم أم بغير الحس؟ فإن قالوا: عرفنا بالحس، قيل لهم بأي حس عرفتم؟ ويذكر كل حس على حدة ليظهر بطلان دعواهم، ويقال لهم : ما بالنا لا نعرف ذلك بالحس ونحن أرباب الحواس السليمة، ولا يجري الاختلاف في المحسوسات بين أرباب الحواس السليمة؟ وإن قالوا: عرفنا ذلك بغير الحس، فقد أقروا أن شيئا سوى الحواس من أسباب المعارف - ثم نفس الكلام منهم دليل على أنهم عرفوا بالخبر شيئا، لأنهم تكلموا بلغة من اللغات، ومعرفة اللسان واللغة ليست بالحس ولا بالعقل، إذ أوفر خليقة الله عقلا، وأذكاهم حسا، لو سمع لغة لم يتعلمها، لا يعرف معناها، وإنما يعرف ذلك بإخبار الملقن ، ثم بإنكار الخبر تعطيل السمع واللسان وكفران لنعمة الله تعالى بهما، وإلحاق نفسه بالبهائم إذ بالبيان بان الإنسان من الحيوان، ولا يرضى بهذه الرتبة لنفسه مجنون، ولا يقال : إن الخبر إن بطل فسائر أقسام الكلام باق، وهي الاستخبار والأمر والنهي ، لأن هذه الأقسام لا يعرف صيغها إلا بإخبار الملقن، ولأن كل قسم من هذه الأقسام يبطل ببطلان الخبر ، أما الاستخبار فلأن الخبر إذا لم يوجب العلم خرج الاستخبار عن حد الفائدة، والتحق بالعبث، ولأن كل استخبار فيه معنى الخبر لولا هو لبطل الاستخبار، فإنك إذا قلت: هل في الدار زيد؟ تقديره أريد أن تخبرني بكون زيد في الدار إن كان ، وبنفي كونه فيها إن لم يكن، وإذا قلت اسقني تقديره أطلب منك أن تسقيني، وكذا فيه إخبار عن حسن المأمور به، فلو لم يكن هذا الإخبار ثابتا، لخرج الأمر عن أن يكون صادرا عن حكيم، وإذا قال لا تضربني فكأنه قال : أطلب منك أن لا تضربني، وأكره أن تضربني.

If they say by sense, they must specify which sense, and a check of each sense shows that none can perceive 'beyond the senses'. If they say by other than sense, they admit a source beyond the senses. Then a further argument is made: the very fact that the opponents speak a language shows they have acquired language through report (from a teacher), not through sense or reason alone. Denying report would thus disable hearing and speech, ingratitude for God's gifts, and reduce humans to animals. Finally, the author refutes the objection that if report is invalidated, other speech acts (interrogation, command, prohibition) remain. He shows that these speech acts also depend on report: interrogation seeks a report, command implies a report about the goodness of the commanded act, etc. Therefore, all speech acts are invalid without report.

Key Terms
الاستخبار
الأمر
النهي
الخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Commands are learned through sense
B) Commands contain an implicit report about the goodness of the commanded act
C) Commands are not speech acts
D) Commands rely on reason alone

Key Takeaway
All types of speech — questioning, commanding, prohibiting — implicitly rely on report, so denying report destroys all meaningful communication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you give a command or ask a question, do you recognize that you are implicitly relying on the truthfulness of report? How does this awareness affect your responsibility to speak truthfully?
?
Think of a time you commanded someone to do something—did you consider that your command presupposes a report about the goodness of the action? How should that awareness change how you issue commands?
