The Dialectical Proof that Report is a Source of Knowledge
Dialectical opening proving report is a source of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial dialectical argument establishing that report (khabar) is a source of knowledge alongside or beyond the senses.

Core Idea
Report is a source of knowledge: the senses alone cannot account for all knowledge.

The author opens with a dialectical challenge to those who deny the senses as a proof. He forces them to admit that knowledge comes from something other than the senses, setting the stage for the argument that report is that source. This lesson covers the initial exchange and its conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialectical exchange
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the question-and-answer structure between the author and an imaginary opponent.
The author uses a common kalam method: forcing the opponent into a contradiction.
2
Identify the two possible answers
The opponent is given two choices: knowledge by sense or by other than sense. See how each is refuted or leads to an admission.
3
Understand the conclusion
The opponent's admission that knowledge comes from other than the senses paves the way for the next lesson's argument that report is that source.
This passage ends with the admission that something besides the senses is a cause of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحواس ليس بحجة، ولا من أسباب المعارف؟ أبالحس عرفتم أم بغير الحس؟ فإن قالوا: عرفنا بالحس، قيل لهم بأي حس عرفتم؟ ويذكر كل حس على حدة ليظهر بطلان دعواهم، ويقال لهم : ما بالنا لا نعرف ذلك بالحس ونحن أرباب الحواس السليمة، ولا يجري الاختلاف في المحسوسات بين أرباب الحواس السليمة؟ وإن قالوا: عرفنا ذلك بغير الحس، فقد أقروا أن شيئا سوى الحواس من أسباب المعارف - ثم نفس الكلام منهم دليل على أنهم عرفوا بالخبر شيئا، لأنهم تكلموا بلغة من اللغات، ومعرفة اللسان واللغة ليست بالحس ولا بالعقل، إذ أوفر خليقة الله عقلا، وأذكاهم حسا، لو سمع لغة لم يتعلمها، لا يعرف معناها، وإنما يعرف ذلك بإخبار الملقن، ثم بإنكار الخبر تعطيل السمع واللسان وكفران لنعمة الله تعالى بهما، وإلحاق نفسه بالبهائم إذ بالبيان بان الإنسان من الحيوان، ولا يرضى بهذه الرتبة لنفسه مجنون، ولا يقال : إن الخبر إن بطل فسائر أقسام الكلام باق، وهي الاستخبار والأمر والنهي، لأن هذه الأقسام لا يعرف صيغها إلا بإخبار الملقن، ولأن كل قسم من هذه الأقسام يبطل ببطلان الخبر، أما الاستخبار فلأن الخبر إذا لم يوجب العلم خرج الاستخبار عن حد الفائدة، والتحق بالعبث، ولأن كل استخبار فيه معنى الخبر لولا هو لبطل الاستخبار، فإنك إذا قلت: هل في الدار زيد؟ تقديره أريد أن تخبرني بكون زيد في الدار إن كان، وبنفي كونه فيها إن لم يكن، وإذا قلت اسقني تقديره أطلب منك أن تسقيني، وكذا فيه إخبار عن حسن المأمور به، فلو لم يكن هذا الإخبار ثابتا، لخرج الأمر عن أن يكون صادرا عن حكيم، وإذا قال لا تضربني فكأنه قال : أطلب منك أن لا تضربني، وأكره أن تضربني وكذا فيه إخبار عن قبح المنهي عنه، ولولا هو لخرج النهي من أن يكون صادرا عن حكيم وإذا قال: يا زيد فكأنه قال : أدعوا زيدا، وإذا قال: ليت زيدا عندنا فكأنه قال : أتمنى كون زيد عندنا، وإذا كان كذلك علم أن الكلام كله يبطل ببطلان الخبر وتتعطل فائدة السمع واللسان، ويلتحق الإنسان بالبهائم. وما يقولون: إن الخبر يتنوع إلى صدق وكذب، نقول : ما يحتمل الكذب لا يوجب العلم. الخبر الموجب للعلم وإنما يوجب العلم ما لا يتصور كونه كذبا، وهو ما تواتر من الأخبار، إذ كون مثله كذبا مستحيل وكذا ما تأيد بالبرهان المعجزي، وهو قول الرسول عليه السلام، وقط لم يتمكن كذب في هذين الخبرين، والله الموفق.
Are the senses not a proof, and not among the causes of knowledge? Did you know by sense or by other than sense? If they say: We knew by sense, it is said to them: By which sense did you know? And each sense is mentioned individually to show the falsehood of their claim, and it is said to them: Why do we not know that by sense while we are possessors of sound senses, and there is no disagreement in sensibles among possessors of sound senses? And if they say: We knew that by other than sense, then they have admitted that something other than the senses is among the causes of knowledge — then their very speech is evidence that they knew something by report, because they spoke in one of the languages, and knowledge of the tongue and language is not by sense nor by intellect, for the most abundant in intellect of God's creation and the sharpest in sense, if he heard a language he had not learned, he would not know its meaning; rather, he knows that only by the informing of the teacher. Then by denying report, hearing and tongue are rendered useless, and it is ingratitude for the blessing of God Most High by them, and joining oneself to the beasts, for by expression man is distinguished from animal, and no madman would be pleased with this rank for himself. And it is not said: If report is invalidated, then the remaining divisions of speech remain, namely interrogation, command, and prohibition, because these divisions are not known except by the informing of the teacher, and because every division of these divisions is invalidated by the invalidation of report. As for interrogation, because if report does not necessitate knowledge, interrogation leaves the limit of benefit and joins futility; and because every interrogation contains the meaning of report; were it not for that, interrogation would be invalid. For if you say: 'Is Zayd in the house?' its purport is: I want you to inform me of Zayd's being in the house if he is, and of negating his being in it if he is not. And if you say: 'Give me water' its purport is: I request from you that you give me water; and likewise in it there is informing about the goodness of the commanded thing. So if this informing were not established, the command would cease to be issued from a wise one. And if he says: 'Do not hit me,' it is as if he said: I request from you that you not hit me, and I dislike that you hit me; and likewise in it there is informing about the ugliness of the prohibited thing; were it not for that, the prohibition would cease to be issued from a wise one. And if he says: 'O Zayd,' it is as if he said: I call Zayd; and if he says: 'Would that Zayd were with us,' it is as if he said: I wish for Zayd to be with us. And if that is so, it is known that all speech is invalidated by the invalidation of report, and the benefit of hearing and tongue becomes idle, and man joins the beasts. And what they say: report is divided into truth and falsehood, we say: what admits falsehood does not necessitate knowledge. The report that necessitates knowledge: only that it be inconceivable to be false necessitates knowledge, and that is what is mutawatir (mass-transmitted) among reports, for its being false is impossible; likewise what is supported by miraculous proof, and that is the statement of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and falsehood has never been possible in these two reports. And God is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part proof that report (khabar) is a necessary source of knowledge. The author begins by posing a dialectical question to those who deny the senses as a proof: 'Did you know by sense or by other than sense?' If they claim sense, they are asked which specific sense, and it is argued that there is no universal agreement among all people with sound senses on these matters. If they admit other than sense, they concede that something besides sense gives knowledge. Then the author points out that their very speech in a language proves they rely on report, since language is learned through instruction, not through sense or intellect alone. Denying report would render speech and hearing useless and reduce humans to beasts. Next, the author anticipates the objection that other speech acts (interrogation, command, prohibition) could remain even if report is invalidated. He refutes this by showing each depends on the concept of informing. Interrogation seeks a report; command implies an informing about the goodness of the act; prohibition implies informing about its ugliness; even vocatives and wishes contain an implicit report. Thus all speech collapses without report. Finally, the author addresses the objection that report can be false, so it cannot yield certainty. He responds by distinguishing between reports that admit falsehood and those that do not. Certainty comes only from reports that are inconceivably false: the mutawatir (mass-transmitted) report, and the statement of a prophet supported by a miraculous proof. These two types of report are immune to falsehood.

الحس
الخبر
أسباب المعارف

C) Report is the only source.
D) They do not know anything.

Key Takeaway
By forcing the opponent to admit a source of knowledge beyond the senses, the author opens the door for report (khabar) to be that source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a matter of faith that cannot be proven by your senses—like the reality of angels or the unseen—do you find yourself secretly doubting because you haven't 'seen' it yourself? How does this passage's argument challenge that doubt, and what will you do the next time that doubt arises?
?
Think of a time when you demanded a sensory 'proof' for a religious teaching before accepting it. What was that teaching, and did that demand reveal a reliance on the senses that this passage says is insufficient? Commit to one specific step you will take to strengthen your trust in the report (khabar) of the Quran and Sunnah over your own sensory judgment.
?
The passage forces the opponent to admit that there is knowledge beyond the senses. In your daily life, how much of your certainty about your religion comes from sensory experience (seeing miracles, feeling spiritual states) versus from the testimony of the prophets and scholars? What is one habit you can change to rely more on authentic report than on your fluctuating feelings?

The Language Argument and the Necessity of Report
Proof from language and analysis of speech acts showing report is essential and certain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how language learning proves the necessity of report, that all speech acts depend on report, and that only certain types of report give certainty.

Core Idea
All speech, and thus all human communication, depends on report (khabar).

Having established that knowledge comes from beyond the senses, the author now shows that report is that source. He uses the example of language learning: we know a language only through instruction, not through sense or intellect. He then defends report against the objection that other speech acts could stand without it, and finally distinguishes the reports that yield certainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the language argument
Read how the author proves that language is learned through report, not through sense or reason. Notice the example of the most intelligent person not understanding an unfamiliar language.
2
Trace the dependency of other speech acts
The author lists interrogation, command, prohibition, vocative, and wish, showing that each contains an implicit report. See how the refutation works.
This is a detailed argument showing that all speech collapses without report.
3
Understand the two types of certain report
The author concludes by explaining that only mutawatir and prophetic reports (supported by miracles) are immune to falsehood and thus give certainty.
This answers the objection that report can be false.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some hold that report (khabar) cannot be a source of certainty because it can be false.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which two types of report give certainty?
A) Any report that is true.
B) Mutawatir reports and prophetic reports supported by miraculous proof.
C) Reports that are widely accepted.
D) Only prophetic reports.

Key Takeaway
All speech acts implicitly rely on report, and only reports that cannot be false (mutawatir and prophetic) provide certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on reports every day—from news to what friends tell you. If someone told you that report can never give certainty, you would not live consistently. Yet you claim to base your religion on reports from the Prophet ﷺ. Is your trust in those reports as firm as your trust in everyday reports, or do you treat them with suspicion? If you doubt, what does that say about your faith?
?
The author argues that denying report reduces humans to beasts. Consider your own life: when you hear a verse from the Quran or a hadith, do you receive it as a certain report that demands action, or do you mentally qualify it with 'but that might not be authentic' or 'that might not apply today'? Where is the line between healthy skepticism and the beastly rejection of report?
?
You have learned that mutawatir reports are certain because collusion on a lie is impossible. Think of the most well-known mutawatir report about the Prophet ﷺ: the Quran itself, or the prayer, or the hajj. Do you feel the certainty of that report in your heart? If not, what is stopping you from internalizing that certainty, and what would it take for you to live as if it were as certain as the sun rising?

Method of Learning
beginner

The Valid Sources of Religious Knowledge: Senses, Reports, and Reason
Nasafi lists three rejected sources and three accepted sources for knowing religious truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi restricts valid sources of religious knowledge to senses, reports, and intellect, excluding inner feelings, inspiration, and blind following.

Core Idea
Religious truth is known through sense, report, and reason — not inner feelings or authority alone.

Nasafi has already established that senses, reports, and intellects are causes of knowledge. Now he goes further: he explicitly denies that any other source — such as inner feelings, inspiration, or blind following — can determine the truth of religions. This is a crucial methodological principle in Sunni kalam: religious claims must be judged by objective evidence, not subjective experience or uncritical acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rejected sources
The passage names three things that some might consider valid: what occurs in the heart (inner feelings), inspiration (ilham), and taqlid (blind following). Note each.
The phrase 'ما يقع في القلب حسنه' refers to a subjective sense of goodness.
2
Understand the three valid sources
Nasafi says it is already established that senses, reports, and intellects are causes of knowledge. He then asserts there is no cause beyond these.
This is a negative statement: only these three, nothing else.
3
Grasp the implication
The passage draws a sharp boundary: religious truth or falsehood can only be known through these three. Any method outside them is invalid.
This contrasts with Sufi or traditionalist views that might prioritize inspiration or precedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إبطال كون ما يقع في القلب حسنه والإلهام والتقليد من أسباب معرفة صحة الأديان: فإذا ثبت بما ذكرنا كون الحواس والأخبار والعقول من أسباب المعارف فنقول : ليس وراء هذه الأشياء سبب يعرف به صحة الأديان وفسادها.
The discussion on invalidating that what occurs in the heart of its apparent goodness, inspiration (ilham), and taqlid (blind following) are among the causes of knowing the truth of religions. Since it has been established by what we have mentioned that senses (hawass), reports (akhbar), and intellects ('uqul) are among the causes of knowledge, we say: There is no cause beyond these things by which the truth or falsehood of religions is known.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage begins a new section. The author states that only three sources are valid for knowing the truth of religions: senses, reports, and intellects. He explicitly rejects the idea that inner feelings of goodness, inspiration (ilham), or blind following (taqlid) can serve as sources. This is a foundational claim in Sunni kalam: religious truth must be based on empirical evidence and rational proof, not subjective experience or authority alone.

Key Terms

تقليد
حواس
أخبار
عقول
أسباب المعارف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Inner feelings or inspiration are valid sources of religious truth independent of reason and sense evidence.
Correction: Nasafi explicitly denies that inner feelings, inspiration, or taqlid are causes of knowledge; only senses, reports, and intellect are valid.

Checkpoint

A) Senses
B) Reports
C) Intellect
D) Inner feelings of goodness

Key Takeaway
Nasafi restricts the valid means of knowing religious truth to senses, reports, and intellect, excluding subjective feelings, inspiration, and blind following.

Subjective Feeling Cannot Validate Religion
Refutes subjective feeling as a criterion for religious truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why subjective feeling cannot be a valid criterion for religious truth and how it leads to logical contradictions.

Core Idea
Subjective feeling cannot distinguish truth from falsehood in religion.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes a dangerous idea: that whatever feels good in one's heart must be true religion. He shows that if everyone followed their heart, contradictory religions would all be true, which is absurd. This argument teaches us the importance of objective criteria for religious knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how al-Nasafi begins with the claim and immediately calls it impossible.
2
Identify the claim and its refutation
Find the initial claim about subjective feeling and the reason al-Nasafi gives for its impossibility.
The reason is the multiplicity and contradiction of religions.
3
Examine the absurd consequences
List the specific contradictory pairs that would become simultaneously true if subjective feeling were accepted.
These pairs show the logical absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم : من وقع في قلبه حسن شيء لزمه التمسك به، وهذا محال، لأن كثرة الأديان ظاهرة، وتضادها أمر بين، وكل يدعي وقوع حسن ما يدين به، وقبح ما يدين به غيره في قلبه فيؤدي إلى أن يكون كل من يدين بشيء كان دينا صحيحا، لوقوع حسنه في قلبه المتدين به فيكون القول بحدوث العالم وقدمه، وثبوت الصانع وتعطيله، وتوحيده وتثنيته، وثبوت الصفات له ونفيها صحيحا، وهذا محال وكذا ينبغي أن يكون كل دين حقا، ولوقوع حسنه في قلب من اعتقد، باطلا، لوقوع قبحه في قلب من لم يعتقده واعتقد ضده، وفساد هذا مما لا يخفى على المجانين، والله الموفق.
And some people said: Whoever finds in his heart that something is good, it is incumbent upon him to adhere to it. But this is impossible, because the multiplicity of religions is apparent, and their contradiction is clear, and each one claims that what he practices seems good to him and what others practice seems evil in his heart. This would lead to the conclusion that every religion that someone practices is correct because of the goodness he feels in his heart. Then the statements that the world is originated or eternal, that the Creator exists or does not exist, that He is one or two, that attributes are established for Him or negated, would all be correct. This is impossible. Similarly, every religion would have to be true because of the goodness felt in the heart of the believer, and false because of the evil felt in the heart of one who does not believe and believes the opposite. The corruption of this is not hidden even from the insane. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage refutes the notion that subjective inner feeling ('what falls in the heart') can be a valid criterion for determining the truth of a religion. The author (al-Nasafi) begins by stating that some people claim that if a person feels that something is good in his heart, he must adhere to it. He then argues that this is impossible because there are many contradictory religions, and each adherent claims that his own religion feels good and the others feel evil in his heart. If subjective feeling were a valid criterion, then all contradictory religions would be considered true simultaneously. This would lead to absurd consequences, such as affirming both that the world is created and eternal, that a Creator exists and does not exist, that God is one and two, and that attributes are affirmed and negated. All of these contradictory positions would be deemed correct. Therefore, subjective feeling cannot be a reliable basis for religious truth. The author concludes that the corruption of this view is obvious even to the insane.

Correction: Subjective feeling cannot be a valid criterion because it leads to accepting contradictory beliefs as equally true.

Why Inspiration Cannot Be a Criterion for Truth
Refutes the claim that inspiration (ilham) can determine the truth of religions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why inspiration (ilham) cannot serve as a valid criterion for determining the truth of religions, and how the argument forces reliance on rational proof.

Core Idea
Contradictory inspirations cancel each other out; only proof can judge.

This passage tackles a common claim: that direct inspiration from God can tell us which religion is true. But if everyone claims inspiration for their own beliefs, how do we decide? The author presents a clever logical challenge that shows inspiration needs an external standard.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author compares this argument to a previous one about subjective feeling.
2
Identify the claim and its consequences
Find the claim that inspiration is a tool for truth. Notice the author's first move: comparing it to the earlier argument and showing it leads to contradictory religions being true.
3
Understand the dialectical challenge
The author imagines someone saying 'I have been inspired that inspiration is corrupt.' This forces the opponent to admit that some inspiration is false.
This is a powerful reductio ad absurdum.
4
See the conclusion
If some inspiration can be false, then we need a way to distinguish true from false. That way is rational proof, not inspiration itself.
5
Reflect on the method
Ask yourself: Could this argument be applied to other claims of direct knowledge? How does it safeguard the need for objective criteria?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم بأن الإلهام سبب معرفة صحة الأديان والمذاهب، وهذا أيضاً مثل الأول، لأن كلا يدعي أنه ألهم صحة قول نفسه، وفساد مذهب خصمه، فيؤدي إلى القول بصحة الأديان المتناقضة على ما قررنا، وإلى القول بأن كل فاسد، ويقال لهؤلاء، إنى ألهمت أن القول بأن الإلهام آلة صحة الأديان فاسد أصحيح إلهامي هذا أم فاسد؟ فإن قال هو أقر أن القول بأن الإلهام آلة معرفة صحة الأديان والمذاهب قول فاسد، فقد أقر بكون شيء من الإلهام فاسد، وإذا كان الإلهام بعضه صحيحا، وبعضه فاسدا لم يمكن الحكم بصحة كل الإلهام على الإطلاق، ما لم يقم دليل صحته فصار المرجع إلى الدليل دون الإلهام، والله الموفق.
And some people said that inspiration (al-ilham) is a cause for knowing the correctness of religions and schools of thought. This too is like the first [argument], because each one claims that he has been inspired with the correctness of his own view and the corruption of his opponent's doctrine. This leads to affirming the correctness of contradictory religions, as we have explained, and to the conclusion that everything is corrupt. And it is said to these people: 'I have been inspired that the statement that inspiration is a tool for the correctness of religions is corrupt. Is this inspiration of mine correct or corrupt?' If he says 'It is correct,' then he affirms that the statement that inspiration is a tool for knowing the correctness of religions and schools of thought is a corrupt statement, thus he affirms that some inspiration is corrupt. And if some inspiration is correct and some corrupt, it is not possible to judge all inspiration as absolutely correct without a proof of its correctness. So the reference becomes to proof, not inspiration. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that inspiration (ilham) can serve as a valid criterion for determining the truth of religions. The author compares it to the previous argument about subjective feeling. He notes that each person claims to be inspired with the truth of his own view and the falsehood of his opponent's, which again leads to accepting contradictory religions. Then he presents a powerful dialectical challenge: suppose someone claims to be inspired that inspiration itself is not a valid tool. Is that inspiration correct or corrupt? If the opponent admits it is correct, then he admits that some inspiration is false (the one that claims inspiration is a tool). If he says it is corrupt, then he admits that his own inspiration (that it is a tool) could be corrupt. Therefore, not all inspiration can be accepted without a way to distinguish true from false. This returns the matter to rational proof (dalil). Thus inspiration cannot be an ultimate criterion.

Key Terms
الإلهام
الدليل
آلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Personal inspiration is sufficient proof for the truth of a religion.
Correction: The argument shows that because different people claim contradictory inspirations, inspiration itself cannot be the proof; we need an external criterion (rational proof) to judge which inspiration is true.

Checkpoint
What is the main problem with using inspiration (ilham) to determine true religion?
A) Inspiration is rare.
B) Different people claim contradictory inspirations, so a criterion to judge them is needed.
C) Inspiration only comes to prophets.
D) The heart is more reliable.

Key Takeaway
Inspiration cannot be the ultimate criterion for truth; rational proof (dalil) is necessary to distinguish true inspiration from false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel a strong inner conviction about a belief, do you ever test it against objective proof, or do you assume its intensity proves it true? How does this lesson challenge that habit?
?
Think of a time you disagreed with someone who claimed divine guidance. Did you dismiss them simply because their claim contradicted yours, or did you have a criterion beyond feelings? What does this passage say about the validity of your approach?
?
If inspiration alone cannot certify truth, what does that mean for your most deeply held religious convictions? Are you willing to subject them to rational proof, even if it unsettles you? What would it cost you to do so?

Why Blind Following Cannot Determine True Religion
Refutes taqlid because it leads to infinite regress without proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why blind following (taqlid) cannot serve as a valid basis for determining true religion, and how the author's arguments expose its logical flaws.

Core Idea
Blind following fails because it cannot resolve contradictions and leads to an infinite regress of authorities.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi refutes the notion that taqlid (blind following) can lead to knowledge of the true religion. He presents a series of logical arguments showing that taqlid collapses under scrutiny. As you read, pay attention to how he uses the demand for proof to dismantle the position of the blind follower.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the overall structure: a general statement followed by conditional scenarios and an infinite regress argument.
The Arabic uses 'إن' for conditionals.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The author considers two possibilities: following every religious person, or following only one religion. Underline the phrases that describe each. Note the problem with each.
The first scenario is impossible due to contradictions; the second requires proof.
3
Understand the key condition
In the second scenario, the follower is asked for proof. If he gives no proof, his choice is invalid; if he gives proof, he has left taqlid and relies on evidence.
4
Grasp the infinite regress argument
The author shows that asking for justification leads to an endless chain unless proof is given. This proof itself would invalidate taqlid.
This is a classic argument in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا التقليد ليس مما يعرف به صحة الأديان، لأنه إن قلد كل متدين فذلك مستحيل لما مر من التناقض في الأديان، وإن قلد أهل دين من الأديان سئل عن اختياره ذلك وترجحه على غيره من الأديان، فإن لم يقم الدليل بطل اختياره، وإن أقام الدليل بطل التقليد، وصار المرجع هو الدليل، ويقال له: هلا أبطلت التقليد تقليد لمن أبطله فبأي شيء أجاب بطل التقليد، ولأن التقليد ليس فيه إلا كثرة الدعوى، فإنه إذا قيل : لم قلت: إن دينك صحيح ؟ قال : لأن فلانا يعتقده، فيقال له: ولم قلت: إن دين فلان كان صحيحا ؟ وكذلك لو ذكر ثالثا ورابعا إلى ما لا يتناهى
And likewise, taqlid (blind following) is not a means by which the correctness of religions is known. Because if one blindly follows every religious person, that is impossible due to the contradiction in religions as previously mentioned. And if one blindly follows the people of a particular religion, he will be asked about his choice and preference over other religions. If he does not provide proof, his choice is invalid; and if he does provide proof, then taqlid is invalidated and the reference becomes proof. It is also said to him: 'Why did you invalidate taqlid by following someone who invalidated it? By what did he answer?' Thus taqlid becomes invalid. And because taqlid contains nothing but a multiplicity of claims. For if it is said: 'Why do you say your religion is true?' he says: 'Because so-and-so believed it.' Then it is said to him: 'And why do you say that so-and-so's religion was true?' And likewise if he mentions a third and a fourth, ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage refutes taqlid (blind following) as a valid method for ascertaining the truth of a religion. The author presents several arguments. First, if one blindly follows every religious person, then he would accept all contradictory religions, which is impossible. Second, if one chooses to follow only a specific religion, he must have a reason for that choice. If he cannot provide a proof (dalil), his choice is baseless. If he does provide a proof, then he has moved beyond mere taqlid to reasoned evidence, thus taqlid is abandoned. Third, a dialectical argument: the taqlid practitioner likely follows someone who also followed predecessors. Asking why he follows a particular authority leads to an infinite regress (ad infinitum) unless a proof is eventually given. Therefore, taqlid is not a valid epistemological basis for religious truth.

Key Terms
التقليد
الدليل
اختيار
التناقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Blindly following a trusted scholar or tradition is sufficient to know the true religion.
Correction: The argument shows that taqlid fails to discriminate between true and false religions because it cannot resolve contradictions and leads to an infinite regress unless proof is provided, which abandons taqlid itself.

Checkpoint
What happens if a person practicing taqlid (blind following) is asked why he chose a particular religion?
A) He can provide a proof from his authority.
B) He either has no proof (invalidating his choice) or gives a proof (invalidating taqlid).
C) He can quote scripture.
D) He can claim consensus.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid cannot establish religious truth because it either leads to accepting contradictions or requires proof, which abandons taqlid itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
What would it cost you to abandon taqlid and seek proof for every major belief you hold? Are you prepared to face the discomfort of that journey, knowing it is the only way to avoid infinite regress?

The Invalidity of Taqlid for Religious Truth
Nasafi presents the dilemma and regress argument against taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why taqlid (blind acceptance) cannot serve as a valid basis for determining religious truth, due to the regress problem.

Core Idea
Taqlid leads to an infinite chain of claims, not to truth.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi explains why merely following an authority (taqlid) is insufficient for establishing the truth of a religion. He presents a dilemma: if you imitate everyone, you face contradictions; if you imitate one group, you must justify your choice. This justification either requires proof—which abandons taqlid—or leads to an endless regress of appeals to authority. The passage highlights a fundamental principle of Islamic theology: truth must be known through evidence (dalil), not through imitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Familiarize yourself with Nasafi's argument by reading the Arabic and its translation.
2
Identify the two options
Notice the two possibilities for the imitator (imitating all or imitating one) and their consequences.
3
Understand the regress problem
See how each appeal to authority creates a new claim, requiring further evidence.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that claims are established by proof, not by other claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا التقليد ليس مما يعرف به صحة الأديان، لأنه إن قلد كل متدين فذلك مستحيل لما مر من التناقض في الأديان، وإن قلد أهل دين من الأديان سئل عن اختياره ذلك وترجحه على غيره من الأديان، فإن لم يقم الدليل بطل اختياره، وإن أقام الدليل بطل التقليد، وصار المرجع هو الدليل، ويقال له: هلا أبطلت التقليد تقليد لمن أبطله فبأي شيء أجاب بطل التقليد، ولأن التقليد ليس فيه إلا كثرة الدعوى، فإنه إذا قيل : لم قلت: إن دينك صحيح ؟ قال : لأن فلانا يعتقده، فيقال له: ولم قلت: إن دين فلان كان صحيحا ؟ وكذلك لو ذكر ثالثا ورابعا إلى ما لا يتناهى
فيصبر كلما كثر ذكر من قلده كثرت الدعوى، والدعوى تصح بالبرهان لا بدعوى أخرى، وبهذا يبطل قول من يجعل مجرد الطرد دليل كون الوصف وصف العلة، لما في كل محل يرى أن الحكم فيه ثبت وهذا الوصف فيه موجود، قيل له: لم قلت: إنه ثبت بهذه العلة؟ ، فكلما كان محال الحكم والوصف أكثر كان الدعوى أكثر إلا إذا أقام الدليل أن من قلده كان دينه صحيحا، فإن كان ذلك الدليل موجودا في حقه فقد صح قوله، غير أنه لا حاجة به إلى بيان أنه أخذ المذهب من فلان كما لو سئل موحد عن صحة دينه، فيبين أنه أخذ ذلك من أستاذه، فإذا قيل له: لم قلت: إن دين أستاذك كان حقا ؟ أقام دلائل وحدانية الصانع فثبت له صحة دين أستاذه، وثبت أيضا صحة دينه، لوجود ذلك الدليل فيهما جميعا، ولو كان ذلك الدليل غير موجود في حقه، ثبت به صحة دين من قلده، ولا يثبت به صحة دينه، ما لم يتبين أيضا أن هذا الدين عين ذلك الدين الذي كان يعتقده من قلده، مثاله أن واحدا من المسلمين، لو سئل الدليل على صحة دينه، فقال : دليل صحته أن محمدا كان يعتقد هذا الدين، فإذا قيل له ولم قلت إنه كان محقا في دينه؟ فقال: لأنه ظهرت على صحة رسالته المعجزات وأثبت حصول المعجزات له، حكم بصحة دين الرسول محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، لقيام الدليل ولكن الدليل غير موجود في حق المقلد إذ لم يظهر على يده المعجزة، فلو سلم الخصم أن ما يعتقده هذا هو عين ما كان يدين به محمد و ثبت صحة دين هذا ، ولو أنكر الخصم وقال: نعم دين محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم كان دينا صحيحا بدليل قيام المعجزة، ولكن لم قلت: إن ما يقوله من القول
Likewise, taqlid is not a means by which the truth of religions is known. Because if he imitates every religious person, that is impossible due to the contradiction in religions. And if he imitates the people of one religion among religions, he is asked about his choice of that and its preference over other religions. If he does not provide proof, his choice is invalid; and if he provides proof, then taqlid is invalidated and the reference becomes the proof. And it is said to him: 'Why did you not invalidate taqlid by imitating someone who invalidated it? With what did he answer? Then taqlid is invalidated.' And because in taqlid there is nothing but an abundance of claims. For when it is said: 'Why do you say your religion is true?' he says: 'Because so-and-so believed it.' So it is said to him: 'And why do you say that so-and-so's religion was true?' And likewise if he mentions a third and a fourth, ad infinitum. 
Thus it becomes that whenever the mention of those he imitates increases, the claims increase. And claims are established by proof, not by another claim. And by this is invalidated the statement of those who make mere correlation a proof that a property is the property of the cause, because in every case where one sees that the ruling is established and this property is present, it is said to him: 'Why do you say it is established by this cause?' So whenever the cases of ruling and property are more numerous, the claim is more numerous, unless he establishes proof that the one he imitated had his religion true. And if that proof exists in his own case, then his statement is sound, but he has no need to mention that he took the doctrine from so-and-so. Just as if a monotheist is asked about the truth of his religion, he explains that he took that from his teacher. And when it is said to him: 'Why do you say your teacher's religion was true?' he produces proofs of the oneness of the Creator, and so the truth of his teacher's religion is established for him, and also the truth of his own religion is established, due to the existence of that proof in both of them. And if that proof does not exist in his own case, then the truth of the religion of the one he imitated is established by it, but his own religion's truth is not established, unless it is also made clear that this religion is the very same religion that the one he imitated believed. An example: a Muslim, if asked for the proof of the truth of his religion, says: 'The proof of its truth is that Muhammad believed this religion.' Then when it is said to him: 'And why do you say that he was correct in his religion?' he says: 'Because miracles appeared confirming the truth of his message and he established the occurrence of miracles for him.' Then the truth of the religion of the Messenger Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is judged by the existence of the proof, but the proof does not exist in the case of the imitator since a miracle did not appear at his hand. So if the opponent concedes that what this person believes is the very same as what Muhammad believed, then the truth of this person's religion is established. But if the opponent denies and says: 'Yes, the religion of Muhammad was a true religion by the evidence of the occurrence of miracles, but why do you say that what he says...'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage argues that taqlid (uncritical acceptance of authority) cannot establish the truth of any religion. The author begins by stating that taqlid is not a valid means to know religious truth. Two possibilities are considered: if one imitates every religion, it is impossible due to contradictions; if one imitates a particular religion, he must explain his choice. If he gives no proof, his choice is baseless; if he gives proof, he has abandoned taqlid in favor of independent evidence. The author then highlights the problem of infinite regress: when asked why his religion is true, the imitator appeals to an authority, but is then asked why that authority's religion is true, leading to an endless chain of claims. The author emphasizes that claims must be established by proof (burhān), not by other claims. He also applies this principle to refute those who use mere correlation (al-ṭard) as evidence for a cause's property. The author then clarifies that if the imitator has independent proof (e.g., proofs of monotheism for the teacher), then both his teacher's and his own religion are verified. However, if the proof is only present in the teacher (e.g., miracles for the Prophet), the imitator's religion is not automatically true unless he demonstrates that his religion is identical to that of the teacher. The example of a Muslim citing Muhammad's miracles shows that the miracle establishes the Prophet's truth, but the imitator himself lacks such a miracle, so his own claim relies on identity of doctrine. The final part is cut off, but the argument continues with an objection.

Key Terms
تقليد
دليل
دعوى
برهان
مقلد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that following a scholar who had proof is sufficient for truth without verifying it.
Correction: The passage shows that even if the authority had proof, the imitator must either have that proof himself or establish identity of doctrine, otherwise his claim is baseless.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of giving proof when asked about one's choice of religion?
A) It confirms taqlid.
B) It invalidates taqlid.
C) It leads to contradiction.
D) It ends the discussion.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid cannot establish religious truth because it inevitably leads to an infinite regress of unproven claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a religious belief you hold that you have never questioned. Is it possible that you accepted it simply because someone you trusted believed it? What would it cost you to seek proof for that belief today?
?
Nasafi says claims are established by proof, not by another claim. In your journey of faith, where have you substituted the authority of a scholar or teacher for your own certainty? Will you now take one tangible step to move from imitation to verification?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being asked: 'Why did you follow this religion?' If your answer is 'Because my parents or a scholar believed it,' is that sufficient? What must change in your heart and mind before that day?

The Failure of Mere Correlation and the Need for Shared Proof
Nasafi refutes correlation as proof and distinguishes between shared and exclusive proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mere correlation (al-ṭard) is not valid evidence for a cause's property, and how the imitator's claim depends on whether the proof exists in his own right or only in the authority.

Core Idea
Proof must exist in the imitator's own case, or the religion must be exactly the same.

In this continuation, Imam Nasafi extends his critique of taqlid to a related principle in legal theory: the use of mere correlation (al-ṭard) to establish that a property is the 'illa (cause) of a ruling. He then addresses a crucial scenario: what if the imitator has independent proof (like proofs of monotheism) versus relying on a proof unique to the authority (like miracles). This part of the passage clarifies the conditions under which the imitator's religion can be considered true.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 'tard' argument
See how some scholars used mere correlation to claim a property is the cause.
2
Notice the refutation
Nasafi applies the same regress critique to this method.
3
Examine the example of the monotheist
Understand how shared proof works: the monotheist uses proofs of God's oneness that apply to both him and his teacher.
4
Examine the example of the Muslim citing Muhammad's miracles
See why the imitator needs identity of doctrine when the proof is unique to the authority.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Truth requires either personal proof or demonstrable identity with the proven authority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا التقليد ليس مما يعرف به صحة الأديان، لأنه إن قلد كل متدين فذلك مستحيل لما مر من التناقض في الأديان، وإن قلد أهل دين من الأديان سئل عن اختياره ذلك وترجحه على غيره من الأديان، فإن لم يقم الدليل بطل اختياره، وإن أقام الدليل بطل التقليد، وصار المرجع هو الدليل، ويقال له: هلا أبطلت التقليد تقليد لمن أبطله فبأي شيء أجاب بطل التقليد، ولأن التقليد ليس فيه إلا كثرة الدعوى، فإنه إذا قيل : لم قلت: إن دينك صحيح ؟ قال : لأن فلانا يعتقده، فيقال له: ولم قلت: إن دين فلان كان صحيحا ؟ وكذلك لو ذكر ثالثا ورابعا إلى ما لا يتناهى
فيصبر كلما كثر ذكر من قلده كثرت الدعوى، والدعوى تصح بالبرهان لا بدعوى أخرى، وبهذا يبطل قول من يجعل مجرد الطرد دليل كون الوصف وصف العلة، لما في كل محل يرى أن الحكم فيه ثبت وهذا الوصف فيه موجود، قيل له: لم قلت: إنه ثبت بهذه العلة؟ ، فكلما كان محال الحكم والوصف أكثر كان الدعوى أكثر إلا إذا أقام الدليل أن من قلده كان دينه صحيحا، فإن كان ذلك الدليل موجودا في حقه فقد صح قوله، غير أنه لا حاجة به إلى بيان أنه أخذ المذهب من فلان كما لو سئل موحد عن صحة دينه، فيبين أنه أخذ ذلك من أستاذه، فإذا قيل له: لم قلت: إن دين أستاذك كان حقا ؟ أقام دلائل وحدانية الصانع فثبت له صحة دين أستاذه، وثبت أيضا صحة دينه، لوجود ذلك الدليل فيهما جميعا، ولو كان ذلك الدليل غير موجود في حقه، ثبت به صحة دين من قلده، ولا يثبت به صحة دينه، ما لم يتبين أيضا أن هذا الدين عين ذلك الدين الذي كان يعتقده من قلده، مثاله أن واحدا من المسلمين، لو سئل الدليل على صحة دينه، فقال : دليل صحته أن محمدا كان يعتقد هذا الدين، فإذا قيل له ولم قلت إنه كان محقا في دينه؟ فقال: لأنه ظهرت على صحة رسالته المعجزات وأثبت حصول المعجزات له، حكم بصحة دين الرسول محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، لقيام الدليل ولكن الدليل غير موجود في حق المقلد إذ لم يظهر على يده المعجزة، فلو سلم الخصم أن ما يعتقده هذا هو عين ما كان يدين به محمد و ثبت صحة دين هذا ، ولو أنكر الخصم وقال: نعم دين محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم كان دينا صحيحا بدليل قيام المعجزة، ولكن لم قلت: إن ما يقوله من القول
Likewise, taqlid is not a means by which the truth of religions is known. Because if he imitates every religious person, that is impossible due to the contradiction in religions. And if he imitates the people of one religion among religions, he is asked about his choice of that and its preference over other religions. If he does not provide proof, his choice is invalid; and if he provides proof, then taqlid is invalidated and the reference becomes the proof. And it is said to him: 'Why did you not invalidate taqlid by imitating someone who invalidated it? With what did he answer? Then taqlid is invalidated.' And because in taqlid there is nothing but an abundance of claims. For when it is said: 'Why do you say your religion is true?' he says: 'Because so-and-so believed it.' So it is said to him: 'And why do you say that so-and-so's religion was true?' And likewise if he mentions a third and a fourth, ad infinitum. 
Thus it becomes that whenever the mention of those he imitates increases, the claims increase. And claims are established by proof, not by another claim. And by this is invalidated the statement of those who make mere correlation a proof that a property is the property of the cause, because in every case where one sees that the ruling is established and this property is present, it is said to him: 'Why do you say it is established by this cause?' So whenever the cases of ruling and property are more numerous, the claim is more numerous, unless he establishes proof that the one he imitated had his religion true. And if that proof exists in his own case, then his statement is sound, but he has no need to mention that he took the doctrine from so-and-so. Just as if a monotheist is asked about the truth of his religion, he explains that he took that from his teacher. And when it is said to him: 'Why do you say your teacher's religion was true?' he produces proofs of the oneness of the Creator, and so the truth of his teacher's religion is established for him, and also the truth of his own religion is established, due to the existence of that proof in both of them. And if that proof does not exist in his own case, then the truth of the religion of the one he imitated is established by it, but his own religion's truth is not established, unless it is also made clear that this religion is the very same religion that the one he imitated believed. An example: a Muslim, if asked for the proof of the truth of his religion, says: 'The proof of its truth is that Muhammad believed this religion.' Then when it is said to him: 'And why do you say that he was correct in his religion?' he says: 'Because miracles appeared confirming the truth of his message and he established the occurrence of miracles for him.' Then the truth of the religion of the Messenger Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace, is judged by the existence of the proof, but the proof does not exist in the case of the imitator since a miracle did not appear at his hand. So if the opponent concedes that what this person believes is the very same as what Muhammad believed, then the truth of this person's religion is established. But if the opponent denies and says: 'Yes, the religion of Muhammad was a true religion by the evidence of the occurrence of miracles, but why do you say that what he says...'
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage argues that taqlid (uncritical acceptance of authority) cannot establish the truth of any religion. The author begins by stating that taqlid is not a valid means to know religious truth. Two possibilities are considered: if one imitates every religion, it is impossible due to contradictions; if one imitates a particular religion, he must explain his choice. If he gives no proof, his choice is baseless; if he gives proof, he has abandoned taqlid in favor of independent evidence. The author then highlights the problem of infinite regress: when asked why his religion is true, the imitator appeals to an authority, but is then asked why that authority's religion is true, leading to an endless chain of claims. The author emphasizes that claims must be established by proof (burhān), not by other claims. He also applies this principle to refute those who use mere correlation (al-ṭard) as evidence for a cause's property. The author then clarifies that if the imitator has independent proof (e.g., proofs of monotheism for the teacher), then both his teacher's and his own religion are verified. However, if the proof is only present in the teacher (e.g., miracles for the Prophet), the imitator's religion is not automatically true unless he demonstrates that his religion is identical to that of the teacher. The example of a Muslim citing Muhammad's miracles shows that the miracle establishes the Prophet's truth, but the imitator himself lacks such a miracle, so his own claim relies on identity of doctrine. The final part is cut off, but the argument continues with an objection.

طرد
علة
معجزة
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if an authority had a miracle, then simply following that authority suffices for truth.
Correction: Nasafi argues that the miracle proves the authority's truth, but the follower's truth requires either personal proof or exact identity of doctrine.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the monotheist do when asked about his teacher's religion?
A) He cites the teacher's miracles.
B) He produces proofs of the oneness of the Creator.
C) He says 'because I follow him.'
D) He continues the chain of authorities.

Key Takeaway
For taqlid to be valid, the proof that establishes the authority's truth must also be present in the imitator's own belief, or the beliefs must be demonstrably identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'I am Muslim because my parents are Muslim,' what proof exists in your own life that the religion of Muhammad is true? If you have no such proof, what step will you take today to find it?
?
Imagine someone asks you: 'Why do you believe your religion is the truth?' If your answer relies on the authority of a scholar or parent, does this lesson require you to verify that their belief is exactly the same as the Prophet's? How would you do that?
?
Your faith may feel secure because you trust a teacher or a community. But if the proof that made that teacher's religion true is not also true for you, what must you change? Be honest: Are you satisfied with borrowed certainty, or will you seek your own share of proof?

Every Claim Requires Proof
Nasafi states that claims require proof and refutes mere coincidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a claim (daʿwā) requires proof (burhān) rather than another claim, and why mere coincidence (ṭard) is insufficient to establish causation.

Core Idea
Every claim needs proof, not just another claim.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi tackles a crucial issue in theological methodology: the danger of building arguments on unsubstantiated claims. He begins by stating a fundamental principle that any assertion must be supported by evidence, not merely by citing another assertion. This principle immediately invalidates the simplistic reasoning of those who rely on mere coincidence between a description and a ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's concise style.
The passage starts with 'فيصبر كلما كثر ذكر من قلده...'.
2
Identify the two key claims
Find the statement that 'a claim is validated by proof, not by another claim' and the refutation of those who use mere coincidence (ṭard) as proof of causation.
The author uses a logical argument to show the insufficiency of ṭard.
3
Understand the consequence
Reflect on why the only way to stop an infinite regress is to bring proof that the followed authority's religion is correct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيصبر كلما كثر ذكر من قلده كثرت الدعوى، والدعوى تصح بالبرهان لا بدعوى أخرى، وبهذا يبطل قول من يجعل مجرد الطرد دليل كون الوصف وصف العلة، لما في كل محل يرى أن الحكم فيه ثبت وهذا الوصف فيه موجود، قيل له: لم قلت: إنه ثبت بهذه العلة؟ فكلما كان محال الحكم والوصف أكثر كان الدعوى أكثر إلا إذا أقام الدليل أن من قلده كان دينه صحيحا، فإن كان ذلك الدليل موجودا في حقه فقد صح قوله، غير أنه لا حاجة به إلى بيان أنه أخذ المذهب من فلان كما لو سئل موحد عن صحة دينه، فيبين أنه أخذ ذلك من أستاذه، فإذا قيل له: لم قلت: إن دين أستاذك كان حقا؟ أقام دلائل وحدانية الصانع فثبت له صحة دين أستاذه، وثبت أيضا صحة دينه، لوجود ذلك الدليل فيهما جميعا، ولو كان ذلك الدليل غير موجود في حقه، ثبت به صحة دين من قلده، ولا يثبت به صحة دينه، ما لم يتبين أيضا أن هذا الدين عين ذلك الدين الذي كان يعتقده من قلده، مثاله أن واحدا من المسلمين، لو سئل الدليل على صحة دينه، فقال: دليل صحته أن محمدا كان يعتقد هذا الدين، فإذا قيل له: ولم قلت إنه كان محقا في دينه؟ فقال: لأنه ظهرت على صحة رسالته المعجزات وأثبت حصول المعجزات له، حكم بصحة دين الرسول محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، لقيام الدليل ولكن الدليل غير موجود في حق المقلد إذ لم يظهر على يده المعجزة، فلو سلم الخصم أن ما يعتقده هذا هو عين ما كان يدين به محمد وثبت صحة دين هذا، ولو أنكر الخصم وقال: نعم دين محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم كان دينا صحيحا بدليل قيام المعجزة، ولكن لم قلت: إن ما يقوله من القول هو قول محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فحينئذ يحتاج إلى الإثبات بالدليل إن هذا عين ذلك الدين، ولو أقام الدليل ثبت صحة ما يدينه وإلا فلا، والله الموفق.
So it becomes that the more one mentions the one he follows, the more claims increase. And a claim is validated by proof, not by another claim. And by this is invalidated the statement of those who make mere coincidence (ṭard) a proof that a description is the description of the cause (ʿilla). For in every case where it is seen that the ruling is established and this description is present, it is said to him: 'Why do you say it is established by this cause?' For whenever the cases of ruling and description are more, the claim is greater, unless he brings proof that the one he follows had a correct religion. If that proof exists in his case, then his statement is correct. However, he does not need to state that he took the school from so-and-so, just as if a monotheist (muwaḥḥid) is asked about the correctness of his religion; he states that he took it from his teacher. If it is said to him: 'Why do you say your teacher's religion was true?' He brings proofs of the oneness of the Creator, so the correctness of his teacher's religion is established for him, and also the correctness of his own religion is established, because that proof exists in both. And if that proof does not exist in his case, the correctness of the religion of the one he follows is established by it, but the correctness of his own religion is not established, unless it is also clarified that this religion is the same as that religion which the followed one believed. For example, if a Muslim is asked for proof of the correctness of his religion and says: 'The proof of its correctness is that Muhammad believed in this religion,' and it is said to him: 'Why do you say he was correct in his religion?' He says: 'Because miracles appeared proving the correctness of his message, and the occurrence of miracles for him is established.' The correctness of the religion of the Messenger Muhammad (peace be upon him) is judged, because proof exists. But the proof does not exist in the case of the follower (muqallid), since no miracle appeared at his hand. If the opponent concedes that what this person believes is exactly what Muhammad believed, then the correctness of this person's religion is established. If the opponent denies and says: 'Yes, the religion of Muhammad (peace be upon him) was a correct religion by the proof of the occurrence of a miracle, but why do you say that what he says is the statement of Muhammad (peace be upon him)?' Then he needs to prove by evidence that this is the same religion. If he brings proof, the correctness of what he believes is established; otherwise not. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument against those who claim that mere imitation (taqlid) suffices without proof. The author begins by stating that the more one relies on an authority, the more claims are involved, but a claim is validated by proof, not by another claim. He then refutes the argument that mere coincidence (ṭard) of a description with a ruling is sufficient to show causation. Instead, every assertion of a ruling being caused by a description requires justification. The only way to avoid infinite regress is to ultimately prove that the authority followed had a correct religion. The author illustrates with the example of a monotheist who follows a teacher: when asked about the correctness of his religion, he can prove the teacher's truth through arguments for the oneness of the Creator, which apply to both teacher and student. However, if the proof does not apply directly to the follower, he must show that his religion is identical to that of the authority whose correctness is proven. The author then gives a concrete example concerning Muslims following Prophet Muhammad. If a Muslim says his religion is correct because Muhammad believed it, he must prove that Muhammad was correct, which is done by citing miracles. But since miracles do not occur for the follower, the follower's own religion is not automatically established unless he can prove that his belief is exactly the same as Muhammad's. If the opponent accepts that Muhammad's religion was true but denies that the follower's belief is identical, the follower must prove identity. Thus, the overall point is that taqlid alone is insufficient; one must be able to provide evidence for the truth of the authority and the identity of one's own belief with that authority.

Key Terms
دعوى
برهان
طرد
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can prove a claim by simply repeating another claim or pointing to mere coincidence.
Correction: Every claim requires its own independent proof; mere repetition or coincidence does not constitute proof.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what validates a claim?
A) Another claim
B) Proof (burhān)
C) Mere coincidence
D) Following an authority

Key Takeaway
A claim must be supported by proof, not by another claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you accepted a belief or claim simply because it was repeated often by others, without verifying it with proof? How does this lesson challenge that habit in your daily life?
?
Are there any religious positions you hold solely because you trust an authority, without having personally examined the evidence? What would it take for you to prove that authority's correctness, as this passage requires?
?
Think of a time you assumed a pattern (like a coincidence) proved a cause, only to be wrong. How can this lesson’s principle—that mere frequency does not equal proof—change how you evaluate claims in your faith and daily decisions?

Proving Identity of Belief
Nasafi illustrates the need to prove identity when the proof does not apply to the follower.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a muqallid must provide proof for his own belief by establishing both the authority's correctness and the identity of his belief with that authority.

Core Idea
Following an authority requires proving both the authority's truth and that you share the same belief.

After establishing the principle that claims need proof, Imam al-Nasafi now illustrates with concrete examples. He shows that a muqallid who relies on an imam must be able to prove the imam's truth through evidence that applies to the imam. However, this does not automatically make the follower's own belief correct unless he can also show that his belief is identical to the imam's. The examples of a monotheist and a Muslim follower of Prophet Muhammad clarify this requirement.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the entire passage for lesson 2, paying attention to the two examples.
The passage begins with 'فإن كان ذلك الدليل موجودا في حقه فقد صح قوله...'
2
Analyze the first example (monotheist)
Notice how the monotheist's proof of his teacher's truth (proof of oneness) also applies to himself, so his belief is automatically correct.
The proof exists in both teacher and student.
3
Analyze the second example (Muslim follower)
See how the follower's proof (miracles) applies only to the Prophet, not to himself. He must then prove that his belief is exactly the same as the Prophet's.
If the opponent denies identity, the follower must bring evidence.
4
Understand the general rule
Reflect on the condition: the follower's belief is only valid if either the proof applies to him or he proves identity of belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

So it becomes that the more one mentions the one he follows, the more claims increase. And a claim is validated by proof, not by another claim. And by this is invalidated the statement of those who make mere coincidence (ṭard) a proof that a description is the description of the cause (ʿilla). For in every case where it is seen that the ruling is established and this description is present, it is said to him: 'Why do you say it is established by this cause?' For whenever the cases of ruling and description are more, the claim is greater, unless he brings proof that the one he follows had a correct religion. If that proof exists in his case, then his statement is correct. However, he does not need to state that he took the school from so-and-so, just as if a monotheist (muwaḥḥid) is asked about the correctness of his religion; he states that he took it from his teacher. If it is said to him: 'Why do you say your teacher's religion was true?' He brings proofs of the oneness of the Creator, so the correctness of his teacher's religion is established for him, and also the correctness of his own religion is established, because that proof exists in both. And if that proof does not exist in his case, the correctness of the religion of the one he follows is established by it, but the correctness of his own religion is not established, unless it is also clarified that this religion is the same as that religion which the followed one believed. For example, if a Muslim is asked for proof of the correctness of his religion and says: 'The proof of its correctness is that Muhammad believed in this religion,' and it is said to him: 'Why do you say he was correct in his religion?' He says: 'Because miracles appeared proving the correctness of his message, and the occurrence of miracles for him is established.' The correctness of the religion of the Messenger Muhammad (peace be upon him) is judged, because proof exists. But the proof does not exist in the case of the follower (muqallid), since no miracle appeared at his hand. If the opponent concedes that what this person believes is exactly what Muhammad believed, then the correctness of this person's religion is established. If the opponent denies and says: 'Yes, the religion of Muhammad (peace be upon him) was a correct religion by the proof of the occurrence of a miracle, but why do you say that what he says is the statement of Muhammad (peace be upon him)?' Then he needs to prove by evidence that this is the same religion. If he brings proof, the correctness of what he believes is established; otherwise not. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument against those who claim that mere imitation (taqlid) suffices without proof. The author begins by stating that the more one relies on an authority, the more claims are involved, but a claim is validated by proof, not by another claim. He then refutes the argument that mere coincidence (ṭard) of a description with a ruling is sufficient to show causation. Instead, every assertion of a ruling being caused by a description requires justification. The only way to avoid infinite regress is to ultimately prove that the authority followed had a correct religion. The author illustrates with the example of a monotheist who follows a teacher: when asked about the correctness of his religion, he can prove the teacher's truth through arguments for the oneness of the Creator, which apply to both teacher and student. However, if the proof does not apply directly to the follower, he must show that his religion is identical to that of the authority whose correctness is proven. The author then gives a concrete example concerning Muslims following Prophet Muhammad. If a Muslim says his religion is correct because Muhammad believed it, he must prove that Muhammad was correct, which is done by citing miracles. But since miracles do not occur for the follower, the follower's own religion is not automatically established unless he can prove that his belief is exactly the same as Muhammad's. If the opponent accepts that Muhammad's religion was true but denies that the follower's belief is identical, the follower must prove identity. Thus, the overall point is that taqlid alone is insufficient; one must be able to provide evidence for the truth of the authority and the identity of one's own belief with that authority.

Key Terms
مقلد
دليل
معجزة
عين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simply claiming to follow a true imam makes one's own belief true without additional evidence.
Correction: One must prove that one's belief is identical to the imam's, especially if the proof for the imam does not apply to oneself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what additional step is required for a follower if the proof for the imam's truth does not apply to the follower?
A) He must perform a miracle.
B) He must prove his religion is identical to the imam's.
C) He must find a new imam.
D) He does not need any further proof.

Key Takeaway
A muqallid's belief is only valid if he can prove both the imam's truth and the identity of their beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If someone asked you to prove that your Islam is exactly the religion of Muhammad (peace be upon him), what would you say? Are you prepared to give evidence, or do you simply assume it?
?
When you follow a scholar or teacher, do you ever check whether their teachings truly match the Prophet's? Or do you trust them blindly? What would you do if you found a contradiction?
?
Could you honestly say that every belief you hold is backed by proof, or are there things you believe just because you were brought up to believe them? Are you willing to examine them?

The Lenient View: Faith Without Rational Proof in Every Detail
Al-Rustughfani's lenient view on rational proof for faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position that rational proof is not required for every detail of faith, as long as prophethood is established.

Core Idea
Faith can rest on prophetic authority without separate rational proof for each belief.

This passage presents the view of most theologians that faith requires proof, but then details a lenient stance from the Maturidi school. Imam al-Rustughfani, a follower of al-Maturidi, argues that once a person recognizes the Prophet through miracles, accepting his statements about creation and God’s oneness is sufficient. This lesson explores that lenient view and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the majority and Rustughfani view
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and English translation carefully. The passage presents the majority view first, then the exception from al-Rustughfani.
Note the key phrase: 'لا يشترط أن يبني اعتقاده على الاستدلال العقلي في كل مسألة'.
2
Identify the key condition for accepting the Prophet's statements
Al-Rustughfani says that if a person bases belief on the Prophet's words after recognizing his prophethood through miracles, it is sufficient. Identify what the person accepts from the Prophet without separate rational proof.
The passage mentions specific beliefs: the temporal origination of the world, the existence of the Maker, and His oneness.
3
Consider the extension by Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub to texts and sunna
Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub expands this view: any belief based on a text (nass) or sunna is sufficient because all of that is proof (dalil). Reflect on the implications of this broadening.
4
Reflect on the role of rational proof in faith
Think about what this lenient view means for the average believer. Does it lower the bar for faith? How does it relate to the concept of taqlid?
This connects to broader debates in Islamic theology about faith and rationality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أهل وذهب أكثر المتكلمين إلى أنه لابد لثبوت الإيمان، أو لكونه نافعا من دليل بني عليه اعتقاده، غير أن الشيخ أبا الحسن الرستغفني صاحب الإمام أبي منصور الماتريدي - رحمه الله - يقول: لا يشترط أن يبني اعتقاده على الاستدلال العقلي في كل مسألة ، بل إذا بنى اعتقاده على قول الرسول، وعرف أنه رسول الله وأنه ظهرت على يديه المعجزات ثُمَّ قبل منه القول في حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع، ووحدانيته من غير أن عرف صحة كل من ذلك بدليل عقلي كان كافيا – وكذا أبو عبد الله الحليمي من متأخري أهل الحديث في ديارنا، مال إلى هذا، وبعض الحديث اكتفوا بذلك الدليل المقترن بالتصديق أن كون إجماعا. وفرع على هذا أبو منصور بن أيوب من متكلمي أهل الحديث، وقال على قياس هذا ينبغي أن كون لو بني اعتقاده على نص أو سنة كان كافيا لأن كل ذلك دليل. والمشهور من مذهب أبي الحسن الأشعري أنه لا يكون مؤمنا ما لم يعتقد كل مسألة من مسائل الأصول عن دليل عقلي غير أن الشرط أن يعرف ذلك بقلبه ولا يشترط أن يعبر عن ذلك بلسانه وأن يكون بصيرا بمجادلة الخصوم قادرا على دفع ما يورد عليه من الإشكال وإبطال ما توجه عليه من الشبه، قال عبد القاهر البغدادي): إن
Ahl… And most theologians (mutakallimun) held that for the confirmation of faith, or for it to be beneficial, a proof must be built upon which one's belief is based. However, the Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani, the companion of Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi—may Allah have mercy on him—says: It is not required that he builds his belief upon rational reasoning (al-istidlal al-‘aqli) in every matter. Rather, if he builds his belief upon the saying of the Messenger, and he knows that he is the Messenger of Allah and that miracles appeared at his hands, then he accepts from him the statement about the temporal origination of the world, the existence of the Maker, and His oneness, without knowing the correctness of each of these by a rational proof, it is sufficient. Likewise, Abu ‘Abdullah al-Halimi among the later scholars of Hadith in our lands inclined to this. And some of the Hadith folk sufficed with that proof combined with affirmation (tasdiq), that it be unanimous. And upon this Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub, from the theologians of the Hadith folk, derived a branch, saying: By analogy with this, it should be that if he builds his belief upon a text or a sunna, it is sufficient, because all of that is proof. And the well-known view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash‘ari is that he is not a believer unless he believes every matter of the fundamentals (usul) from a rational proof, except that the condition is that he knows that in his heart and it is not required that he express it with his tongue, and that he is perceptive in debating opponents, able to repel what is presented to him of difficulties and to invalidate what is directed at him of doubts. ‘Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi said: Verily...
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage addresses the epistemological requirement for valid faith (iman). It begins by stating the majority of theologians hold that faith requires a proof (dalil) built upon rational reasoning (istidlal ‘aqli) for faith to be valid or beneficial. Then it presents the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani, a key follower of Imam al-Maturidi, who does not require rational proof in every detail: if a person bases his belief on the Prophet’s statement after recognizing his prophethood through miracles, then accepting from him the creation of the world, existence of God, and His oneness without separate rational proof is sufficient. This is a lenient view that allows taqlid in these foundational matters once prophethood is established. The author then notes that Abu ‘Abdullah al-Halimi inclined to this, and some Hadith scholars sufficed with the proof of prophecy combined with general acceptance (tasdiq). Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub extended this to say that building belief on any text or sunna is sufficient because all such sources count as proofs. Next, the well-known Ash‘ari position is stated: faith requires rational proof for each fundamental belief; however, the condition is internal conviction (ma‘rifa bi-l-qalb), not verbal expression, and the believer must be capable of defending his beliefs through debate and refuting doubts. Finally, the passage begins a quote from ‘Abd al-Qahir al-Baghdadi but is cut off.

Misconception: Some think that Maturidis require rational proof for every detail of faith.
Correction: This passage shows that al-Rustughfani, a key Maturidi figure, allows acceptance of core beliefs on prophetic authority without separate rational proof in each case.

Checkpoint
According to al-Rustughfani, what is sufficient for believing in the creation of the world and God's oneness?
A) Independent rational proof for each matter
B) Acceptance from the Prophet after knowing his prophethood through miracles
C) Blind imitation without any basis
D) Consensus of the community

Key Takeaway
For al-Rustughfani and some other scholars, once prophethood is established, accepting the Prophet's statements on core beliefs is sufficient for faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt anxious that your faith is incomplete because you cannot rationally prove every single belief? How does al-Rustughfani's concession that prophetic authority suffices for core doctrines speak to that anxiety? Will you now entrust your heart more fully to the Prophet's words, even where your intellect hesitates?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you still insist on rational proof before submitting, contrary to the example of accepting the Prophet's statements in this passage? Which beliefs do you hold solely on authority, and which do you demand to verify intellectually? What does that imbalance reveal about your actual trust in the Prophet?
?
If you truly accept that the Prophet's word is enough for the greatest matters like the existence of God and creation, why do you still doubt or seek evidence for other teachings of Islam? Could it be that you have not fully established in your heart that he is indeed a messenger of God through his miracles? What would it take for you to reach that certainty?

The Ash'ari Position: Rational Proof Required for Every Fundamental
Ash'ari's requirement of rational proof for fundamental beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari requirement that every fundamental belief must be known through a rational proof, though only internal knowledge is required, not verbal articulation.

Core Idea
For Ash'aris, rational proof for each core belief is necessary, but internal conviction suffices without verbal expression.

While the Maturidi lenient view allows faith based on prophetic authority, the well-known Ash'ari position demands rational proof for every fundamental issue. However, this requirement is internal: the believer must know the proofs in his heart, not necessarily articulate them. This lesson explores the Ash'ari view as presented by Imam Nasafi.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari position passage
Read the Arabic passage and English translation. Focus on the statement: 'المشهور من مذهب أبي الحسن الأشعري أنه لا يكون مؤمنا ما لم يعتقد كل مسألة من مسائل الأصول عن دليل عقلي'.
2
Identify the two conditions in the Ash'ari view
Al-Ash'ari requires (1) that one believes every fundamental (usul) from a rational proof, and (2) that one knows it in his heart, without needing to express it verbally. Also, the person must be able to debate opponents and refute doubts.
Note that verbal articulation is not required.
3
Compare with the lenient view from the previous lesson
Consider how the Ash'ari requirement is stricter than al-Rustughfani's, but still has a concession by not requiring verbal expression. Think about the practical implications for a believer.
This highlights a major difference between Maturidi and Ash'ari schools.
4
Reflect on the role of debate and apologetics
The Ash'ari view adds that the believer must be skilled in debating and refuting doubts. Why might this be part of the requirement?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

?
