Should every belief be proven rationally? Al-Rustughfani's view
Al-Nasafi reports the view that faith does not require rational proof in every matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some early Maturidi scholars allowed faith to be based on prophetic authority without requiring rational proof for each specific doctrine.

Core Idea
Faith can be built on prophetic authority once prophethood is confirmed.

What happens when a person accepts the Prophet's message but cannot reason through every theological point? Al-Rustughfani, a close associate of Imam al-Maturidi, argued that this is sufficient. In this passage, al-Nasafi presents this permissive view alongside similar opinions from later hadith scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the different scholarly positions mentioned.
2
Identify al-Rustughfani's key claim
Find where al-Rustughfani says rational reasoning is not required in every issue. Note his qualification: the Prophet must be known through miracles.
3
See the extension by later scholars
Notice how al-Halimi and some hadith scholars agree, and then Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub extends it to relying on any text or sunna.
This shows a spectrum of views on the role of reason.
4
Reflect on the diversity
Ask yourself what this tells us about the range of opinion within Maturidi and hadith circles on the necessity of rational proof.
This passage does not give a single ruling; it reports multiple views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب أكثر المتكلمين إلى أنه لابد لثبوت الإيمان، أو لكونه نافعا من دليل بني عليه اعتقاده، غير أن الشيخ أبا الحسن الرستغفني صاحب الإمام أبي منصور الماتريدي رحمه الله يقول: لا يشترط أن يبني اعتقاده على الاستدلال العقلي في كل مسألة، بل إذا بنى اعتقاده على قول الرسول، وعرف أنه رسول الله وأنه ظهرت على يديه المعجزات ثم قبل منه القول في حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع، ووحدانيته من غير أن عرف صحة كل من ذلك بدليل عقلي كان كافيا – وكذا أبو عبد الله الحليمي من متأخري أهل الحديث في ديارنا، مال إلى هذا، وبعض الحديث اكتفوا بذلك الدليل المقترن بالتصديق أن كون إجماعا. وفرع على هذا أبو منصور بن أيوب من متكلمي أهل الحديث، وقال على قياس هذا ينبغي أن كون لو بني اعتقاده على نص أو سنة كان كافيا لأن كل ذلك دليل.
And most of the mutakallimun held that for the establishment of faith, or for it to be beneficial, a proof upon which one's belief is based is necessary. However, Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani, the companion of Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (may Allah have mercy on him), says: It is not required that one build his belief on rational reasoning in every issue. Rather, if he builds his belief on the saying of the Messenger, and he knows that he is the Messenger of Allah and that miracles were manifested at his hands, then he accepts from him the statement about the origination of the world, the existence of the Maker, and His oneness, without having known the validity of each of these by rational proof, it is sufficient. Similarly, Abu Abd Allah al-Halimi, among the later people of hadith in our region, inclined to this. And some of the people of hadith sufficed with that proof coupled with affirmation, that it be consensus. And Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub, from the mutakallimun of the people of hadith, derived a branch from this and said: By analogy with this, it follows that if one builds his belief on a text (nass) or sunna, it is sufficient, because all of that is proof.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a report of differing views on the necessity of rational proof for the validity of faith. Most mutakallimun require a proof on which belief is based. But al-Rustughfani holds that rational reasoning is not required in every issue; it is sufficient to accept the Prophet's statements after knowing his prophethood through miracles. He thus accepts the core beliefs (origination, existence of God, oneness) on prophetic authority. Al-Halimi and some hadith scholars agree, considering the proof of the Prophet's miracles coupled with belief to be sufficient, and some even consider it consensus. Abu Mansur ibn Ayyub extends this: if one bases belief on a textual proof (nass) or sunna, it is sufficient, because all these are proofs. The author presents these views without endorsement, as part of a broader discussion on the role of reasoning in faith.

Key Terms
دليل عقلي
استدلال
معجزات
تصديق
نص
سنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Rustughfani rejects all rational proofs in faith.
Correction: He only says rational proof is not required in every specific issue; the initial recognition of the Prophet through miracles is still rational.

Checkpoint
According to al-Rustughfani, what is sufficient for believing in the origination of the world and the oneness of God?
A) A detailed rational proof for each issue.
B) Accepting the Prophet's statement after knowing his prophethood through miracles.
C) Rejecting all rational evidence.
D) Only following the consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Al-Rustughfani and some hadith scholars held that it is sufficient to base one's belief in core doctrines on the authority of the Prophet, after confirming his prophethood through miracles, without requiring separate rational proofs for each issue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly examined the evidence for the Prophet's miracles yourself, or do you rely on the testimony of others? If your belief in prophethood is borrowed, what would happen if you encountered a doubt that shook it? Will you investigate those proofs today?
?
You may have accepted the existence of God and His oneness on the authority of your teachers without rational reflection. But al-Rustughfani still requires that you know the Prophet is true through miracles. Do you personally know that? If not, your faith may be built on habit rather than conviction. What would it take for you to verify this foundation?
?
If someone challenges one of your core beliefs—like the origin of the world—could you defend it with evidence, or would you only say 'the Prophet said so'? Al-Rustughfani says that is enough if you have already proven prophethood. But have you? Or are you avoiding the hard work of personal certainty? Answer honestly: are you satisfied with second-hand faith?

Ash'ari vs Mu'tazili on Refuting Doubts
Nasafi contrasts Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views on the necessity of personal ability to refute doubts for valid belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili positions on whether a believer must personally be able to refute objections to hold valid knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires either personal rebuttal ability or trust in reliable scholars.

This passage by Nasafi presents a key methodological debate between Ash'ari and Mu'tazili theologians. At issue is whether a believer's conviction based on rational proof is sufficient if he cannot personally refute every objection. Read on to see the arguments and the distinction between doctrinal and discretionary matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's own words present the debate.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the Ash'ari claim and the Mu'tazili counter. Notice how each school defines the condition for knowledge.
The Ash'ari says rational evidence suffices; the Mu'tazili says ability to refute doubts is needed.
3
Understand key terms
Note terms like 'ilm istidlali (inferential knowledge), zann (opinion), and daf' al-shubah (repelling doubts). These are central to the argument.
These terms distinguish knowledge from mere opinion.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this debate relates to the distinction between doctrinal matters (masa'il i'tiqadiyya) and discretionary matters (masa'il ijtihadiyya).
The Mu'tazili criterion applies to doctrinal matters, implying a higher standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير أن الأشعري يقول: إذا بنى اعتقاده على دليل عقلي كان كافيا، وإن كان لا يقدر على دفع الشبه إذا كان لا يرتاب في عقيدته عند ورود الشبهة عليه بل يعلم أن وراءها ما يبطلها من الحجة، وأن من العلماء القائمين بنصرة الملة الذابين عنها من يقوم بدفعها، وهذا لأن باقتران الدليل باعتقاده صار علما استدلاليا. وصار به مدخلا نفسه في الأمان، من أن يكون مكذوبا أو مخدوعا أو ملبسا عليه فكان مؤمنا، فلا معنى لاشتراط قدرته على دفع الشبه وحل الإشكالات. والمعتزلة يقولون: إن الرأي المبني على ما تصور عند الرائي أنه دليل لن يكون علما ما لم يقدر على دفع الشبه المعترضة على الدليل بل يكون ظنا إذ العلم إنما يمتاز من الظن بالقدرة على دفع ما يوجب ضد هذا الرأي. ويعترض على هذا الرأي بالبطلان، فإن ثبتت القدرة كان ذلك علما، وإن لم تثبت كان ظنا، وبهذا تمتاز المسائل الاعتقادية عن المسائل الاجتهاديةويجري
Except that al-Ash'ari says: If he builds his belief on a rational evidence, it is sufficient, even if he is not able to repulse doubts, as long as he does not doubt in his belief when an objection occurs to him; rather, he knows that behind it there is something that invalidates it from the proofs, and that among the scholars who stand up for the support of the religion and defend it there is someone who undertakes to repulse them. This is because by the accompaniment of evidence with his belief, it becomes inferential knowledge ('ilm istidlali), and he thereby becomes one who has entered himself into security (adkhala nafsahu fi al-aman) from being lied to, deceived, or confused, so he is a believer. Hence there is no meaning to stipulating his ability to repulse doubts and solve problems. The Mu'tazila say: Indeed an opinion built on what the observer thinks is evidence will not be knowledge until he is able to repulse the objections that arise against the evidence; rather, it is opinion (zann), since knowledge is distinguished from opinion only by the ability to repulse what necessitates the opposite of this opinion. And this opinion is objected to as invalid: if the ability is established, it is knowledge; if not established, it is opinion. And by this, the doctrinal matters (masa'il i'tiqadiyya) are distinguished from the discretionary matters (masa'il ijtihadiyya). And it proceeds...
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents a theological-methodological debate between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili schools regarding the conditions for valid faith-knowledge. The Ash'ari position: a believer who bases his conviction on rational proof has sufficient knowledge; he need not personally be able to rebut every objection. As long as he is confident that there is a valid rebuttal and that qualified scholars can provide it, his belief counts as inferential knowledge ('ilm istidlali) and he has entered security. Therefore, requiring personal ability to refute doubts is unnecessary. The Mu'tazili counter: a belief formed on what the person considers evidence does not reach the level of knowledge unless he can actually repulse objections raised against that evidence; otherwise it remains mere opinion (zann). The distinction between knowledge and opinion is precisely the ability to defend against counter-arguments. The author then notes that this Mu'tazili view is objected to as invalid (ya'taridu 'ala hadha al-ra'y bi al-batlan), and then gives a brief dialectic: if ability is established, it is knowledge; if not, it is opinion. Finally, the author ties this to the distinction between doctrinal matters (masa'il i'tiqadiyya) and discretionary matters (masa'il ijtihadiyya), implying that the Mu'tazili standard applies to the former. The passage ends abruptly with 'wa yajri' (and it proceeds), indicating the discussion continues.

Key Terms
دليل عقلي
دفع الشبه
علم استدلالي
ظن
شبهة
مسائل اعتقادية
مسائل اجتهادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris believe that rational defense is unnecessary for faith.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that personal refutation of every doubt is not required, but the existence of scholars who can refute them, and the believer's confidence in that, suffices.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the Ash'ari justification for not requiring a believer to personally be able to repulse all doubts?
A) Because rational proof alone guarantees infallibility.
B) Because the believer knows that behind the doubt there is a valid proof and that scholars can refute it.
C) Because doubts are irrelevant to the validity of faith.
D) Because the believer's intention suffices for knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari-Mu'tazili debate on refuting doubts hinges on whether individual capability is necessary for certain knowledge or whether trust in scholarly defense suffices.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a doubt about your faith, do you rely on your own ability to refute it, or do you trust that there are scholars who can? How does that affect your certainty? Are you willing to 'enter into security' as described, or do you feel the need to personally conquer every objection?
?
Consider a time you encountered a powerful argument against your belief. Did you feel threatened, or did you have confidence that a valid response exists? What does your reaction reveal about where you stand between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili positions?
?
If you were unable to answer a specific doubt about Aqeedah, would you still consider yourself a true believer? According to the passage, what is the condition for genuine belief? How does that challenge your own assessment of your faith?

Why Rational Proof Must Accompany Prophetic Testimony
Author argues that rational proof must accompany prophetic testimony to attain knowledge of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational proof must accompany prophetic testimony to attain knowledge of God's existence and attributes.

Core Idea
Prophetic authority itself depends on rational proof; neither alone suffices.

This passage by Imam al-Nasafi tackles a foundational question: Can we know God solely through the words of a prophet? He argues that it is impossible, because recognizing the prophet's authority already requires prior knowledge of God's existence and attributes. Since that prior knowledge is not innate, rational proof is necessary. Only then can prophetic testimony serve as a valid proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
Notice the compact style; each phrase carries logical weight.
2
Identify the initial claim
The author begins by stating that distinguishing truth from falsehood in religions is impossible (if based solely on prophetic testimony). Mark this claim.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace why the prophet's word alone cannot give knowledge: it depends on prior knowledge of God's existence and attributes. Ask yourself: if you don't already know God, how can you trust the prophet?
The phrase 'cognitions are not necessary' means they are not self-evident.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that a rational proof must accompany the prophetic testimony. See how this solves the circularity problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إظهار ما يحق من الأديان، والتمييز بين الحق والباطل منها وهذا محال. كما يذهب إليه القائلون أن السفه ما نهي عنه وإذا كان الأمر هكذا لن يتصور حصول المعرفة بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع وكمال حكمته وقدرته بقول الرسول لكون المعرفة بكون قوله حجة مترتبة على هذه المعارف - وإذا خرج هذا من أن يصلح أن يكون دليلا يقترن بالتصديق - والمعارف ليست بضرورة – فلابد من القول باقتران دليل عقلي به.
Displaying what is correct among religions, and distinguishing truth from falsehood among them—and this is impossible. As those who claim that foolishness is prohibited say. And if the matter is such, then knowledge of the origination of the world, the establishment of the Maker, the perfection of His wisdom and power cannot be conceived through the statement of the Messenger, because knowledge that his statement is proof depends on these cognitions. And if this is excluded from being suitable as a proof that accompanies affirmation—and cognitions are not necessary—then it is necessary to assert the accompaniment of an intellectual proof with it.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the necessity of rational proof (dalil aqli) accompanying prophetic testimony in matters of faith. The author states that it is impossible to determine true religion and distinguish truth from falsehood solely based on the prophet's word. He critiques a view (possibly of some literalists) that would make such knowledge possible through the prophet's statement alone. The reasoning is that recognizing the prophet's statement as authoritative (hujja) is itself dependent on prior knowledge of God's existence, attributes, and the fact that the prophet is truthful. Since these cognitions (ma'arif) are not innate or necessary, they require rational proof. Without such proof, the prophet's word cannot serve as a self-sufficient proof accompanying the act of affirmation (tasdiq). Therefore, a rational proof must be conjoined with the prophetic testimony.

Key Terms
دليل عقلي
تصديق
معرفة
حدوث العالم
ثبوت الصانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can know God's existence and attributes solely through the prophet's words without any rational foundation.
Correction: The passage shows that accepting the prophet's authority already presupposes knowledge of God, so rational proof is necessary to avoid circularity.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue is necessary for attaining knowledge of God's existence and attributes?
A) The prophet's word alone
B) A rational proof accompanied with the prophet's word
C) Blind imitation of scholars
D) Philosophical speculation without revelation

Key Takeaway
Rational proof must accompany prophetic testimony to avoid circularity in establishing knowledge of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely more on rational arguments or on the authority of the Prophet when affirming the existence of God? How does this passage challenge your default approach? What change must you make starting today?
?
When you listen to a religious text, do you first establish its rational basis, or do you accept it uncritically? What might you be missing if you do the latter? How can you train yourself to think differently?
?
If someone asked you why you believe in God, would you cite a prophetic statement first? Why might that be insufficient according to this lesson? How would you now answer differently, and what will you do to ensure your faith is grounded in both rational proof and revelation?

Ash'ari's View on Knowledge and Doubts
Al-Ash'ari states his condition for valid knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that faith based on a rational proof is sufficient even without personal ability to refute every doubt.

Core Idea
Knowledge does not require personal refutation of every doubt.

Al-Ash'ari presents a relatively lenient condition for valid faith: a single rational proof suffices. This lesson explores his reasoning and why he does not require every believer to be a skilled debater.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari passage
Read the Arabic passage of al-Ash'ari's statement. Notice his key claim: building belief on a rational proof is enough.
The passage begins with 'غير أن الأشعري يقول'.
2
Identify Ash'ari's justification
Al-Ash'ari gives two reasons: the believer knows a rebuttal exists, and scholars will defend the faith. Find these in the text.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Ash'ari concludes there is no need to stipulate personal ability to refute doubts. Consider how this differs from a more demanding stance.
This connects to the common misunderstanding that all believers must be able to refute doubts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[الأشعري يشترط نوعا من الاستدلال والمعتزلة يشترطون استدلالا كاملا وقدرة على الرد]:
غير أن الأشعري يقول : إذا بنى اعتقاده على دليل عقلي كان كافيا، وإن كان لا يقدر على دفع الشبه إذا كان لا يرتاب في عقيدته عند ورود الشبهة عليه بل يعلم أن وراءها ما يبطلها من الحجة، وأن من العلماء القائمين بنصرة الملة الذابين عنها من يقوم بدفعها، وهذا لأن باقتران الدليل باعتقاده صار علما استدلاليا. وصار به مدخلا نفسه في الأمان، من أن يكون مكذوبا أو مخدوعا أو ملبسا عليه فكان مؤمنا، فلا معنى لاشتراط قدرته على دفع الشبه وحل الإشكالات.
والمعتزلة يقولون : إن الرأي المبني على ما تصور عند الرائي أنه دليل لن يكون علما ما لم يقدر على دفع الشبه المعترضة على الدليل بل يكون ظنا إذ العلم إنما يمتاز من الظن بالقدرة على دفع ما يوجب ضد هذا الرأي.
ويعترض على هذا الرأي بالبطلان، فإن ثبتت القدرة كان ذلك علما، وإن لم تثبت كان ظنا، وبهذا تمتاز المسائل الاعتقادية عن المسائل الاجتهادية (ويجري التعليل على من خالفنا في المسائل الاعتقادية دون من خالفنا في المسائل الاجتهادية) وبهذا يمتاز الحق من الباطل لأن الحق ما غلبت حجته، وأظهرت أسباب التمويه في غيره، فأما بدون إظهار أسباب التمويه فيما يقابل هذا الرأي، فلا يوصف بكونه حقا إلا على غالب الرأي والظن هذا هو زبدة كلام المعتزلة.
[Al-Ash'ari stipulates a type of inference, while the Mu'tazila stipulate complete inference and ability to respond]:
But al-Ash'ari says: If one builds his belief on an intellectual proof, it is sufficient, even if he cannot refute doubts, because he does not doubt his belief when a doubt occurs to him; rather he knows that behind the doubt there is an argument that invalidates it, and that among the scholars who support the religion and defend it there are those who will refute it. This is because by the conjunction of the proof with his belief, it becomes demonstrative knowledge. And by it he enters himself into safety from being lied to, deceived, or confused, so he is a believer. Thus there is no point in stipulating his ability to refute doubts and solve problems.
And the Mu'tazila say: An opinion built on what the viewer considers to be a proof will not be knowledge unless he is able to refute the objections that arise against the proof; rather it is mere opinion, because knowledge is only distinguished from opinion by the ability to refute that which opposes this opinion.
And this opinion is objected to as invalid. For if the ability is established, it is knowledge; if not established, it is opinion. And by this, creedal matters are distinguished from jurisprudential matters (and the charge of falsehood is made against those who oppose us in creedal matters, not against those who oppose us in jurisprudential matters). And by this, truth is distinguished from falsehood, because truth is that whose proof prevails and the causes of obfuscation are manifest in others. But without manifesting the causes of obfuscation in what opposes this opinion, it is not described as truth except by predominant opinion and conjecture. This is the essence of the Mu'tazila's speech.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage presents a comparative discussion between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili schools regarding the conditions for religious knowledge ('ilm). The author first states al-Ash'ari's position: It is sufficient for one's belief to be based on a rational proof (dalil aqli). Even if the believer cannot personally refute every doubt (shubha), the proof he has grounds him in certainty; he trusts that scholars can defend the faith. Thus he enters into safety and is a true believer. There is no need to require personal ability to refute doubts. The Mu'tazila disagree: An opinion built on what one thinks is a proof is not knowledge unless one can refute the objections to that proof; otherwise it is mere opinion (zann). Knowledge is distinguished from opinion by the ability to refute contrary arguments. The author then objects to the Mu'tazili view: If one does have the ability, it is knowledge; if not, it is opinion. This criterion distinguishes creedal matters (masa'il i'tiqadiyya) from jurisprudential matters (masa'il ijtihadiyya). In creedal matters, dissenters are charged with falsehood (ta'lil), but not in jurisprudential ones. Truth is that whose proof prevails and whose opponents' arguments are shown to be fallacious. Without such exposure, one's view is only probable truth, not certain. The author concludes that this is the essence of the Mu'tazila's argument.

Key Terms
دليل عقلي
علم
شبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that every believer must personally be able to refute all doubts.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari shows that reliance on a rational proof and trust in scholars suffices; personal refutation is not required.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'ari as presented, what makes a believer's knowledge valid?
A) Ability to personally refute every doubt
B) Building belief on an intellectual proof, even if unable to refute doubts
C) Following the majority opinion
D) Having memorized all arguments

Key Takeaway
For al-Ash'ari, a single rational proof is enough to secure knowledge and faith; personal refutation of doubts is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a doubt about your faith enters your mind, do you panic and feel your faith shaken because you cannot immediately answer it? How can al-Ash'ari's reassurance that scholars can handle the refutation change your response? Commit now to seek the answer or trust the scholars instead of despairing.
?
Do you consider yourself 'safe from being lied to or deceived' if you have built your belief on a single intellectual proof? Reflect on one proof that grounds your faith. Is it truly sufficient for you, or do you need more? Write down that proof and why it gives you certainty.
?
Al-Ash'ari says that the proof becomes demonstrative knowledge and you enter safety. Are you living in that safety, or do you still doubt? What action will you take today to strengthen that proof in your mind?

Mu'tazili View and Its Critique
Mu'tazili statement and author's critique of their condition for knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili condition that knowledge requires ability to refute objections, and the author's refutation of that view.

Core Idea
Knowledge is distinguished from opinion by the ability to refute contrary arguments.

The Mu'tazila set a higher bar for knowledge: unless one can refute doubts, one's belief is mere opinion. This lesson examines their argument and the author's objection that such a criterion leads to a problematic distinction between creedal and jurisprudential matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazili passage
Read the Arabic text of the Mu'tazili position. Identify their key claim: an opinion based on a proof is not knowledge unless the person can refute objections.
The passage begins with 'والمعتزلة يقولون'.
2
Analyze the objection
The author objects that if the ability is present it is knowledge, if not it is opinion. Notice how this criterion is then applied to distinguish creedal from jurisprudential matters.
Consider what this means for the status of dissenters.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The author states that truth is that whose proof prevails and obfuscation is exposed. Without such exposure, the view is only probable. Understand the implication for certainty.
This passage is the essence of the Mu'tazila's argument as presented.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والمعتزلة يقولون : إن الرأي المبني على ما تصور عند الرائي أنه دليل لن يكون علما ما لم يقدر على دفع الشبه المعترضة على الدليل بل يكون ظنا إذ العلم إنما يمتاز من الظن بالقدرة على دفع ما يوجب ضد هذا الرأي.

[Al-Ash'ari stipulates a type of inference, while the Mu'tazila stipulate complete inference and ability to respond]:
But al-Ash'ari says: If one builds his belief on an intellectual proof, it is sufficient, even if he cannot refute doubts, because he does not doubt his belief when a doubt occurs to him; rather he knows that behind the doubt there is an argument that invalidates it, and that among the scholars who support the religion and defend it there are those who will refute it. And by it he enters himself into safety from being lied to, deceived, or confused, so he is a believer. Thus there is no point in stipulating his ability to refute doubts and solve problems.

And this opinion is objected to as invalid. For if the ability is established, it is knowledge; if not established, it is opinion. And by this, creedal matters are distinguished from jurisprudential matters (and the charge of falsehood is made against those who oppose us in creedal matters, not against those who oppose us in jurisprudential matters). And by this, truth is distinguished from falsehood, because truth is that whose proof prevails and the causes of obfuscation are manifest in others. But without manifesting the causes of obfuscation in what opposes this opinion, it is not described as truth except by predominant opinion and conjecture. This is the essence of the Mu'tazila's speech.

Commentary
This passage presents a comparative discussion between the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili schools regarding the conditions for religious knowledge ('ilm). The author first states al-Ash'ari's position: It is sufficient for one's belief to be based on a rational proof (dalil aqli). Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila as presented, what distinguishes knowledge from opinion?
A) The number of proofs one can cite
B) The ability to refute objections against the proof
C) Consensus of the community
D) Following a qualified scholar

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila tie knowledge to personal defensive capability, but the author argues this leads to a problematic criterion that reduces some truths to mere probability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief, do you actively seek out objections to test it, or do you avoid them? What does your answer reveal about your intellectual honesty and trust in your faith?
?
The Mu'tazila require the ability to refute objections for knowledge. But the author argues this reduces many truths to mere probability. Are you comfortable with the possibility that some of your deepest convictions might only be probable? How does that affect your certainty in worship?
?
The passage distinguishes creedal from jurisprudential matters. In your heart, do you treat disagreements in fundamental beliefs with the same tolerance as differences in practice? What does your treatment of others who disagree with you in matters of faith say about your understanding of truth and humility?

Two Types of Prophetic Teachings: Reasonable and Revealed
Third group distinguishes teachings knowable by reason from those only revealed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi theologians distinguish between teachings that human reason can deduce and those that require prophetic revelation.

Core Idea
Reason grasps the essentials; revelation provides the details.

This passage presents the view of a third group (Maturidi scholars) who argue that faith requires a proof, which can be the miracle-supported report of a prophet. They then categorize the laws brought by prophets into two types: those reason can reach through contemplation, and those beyond reason's reach, which must be learned from revelation. This classification shows the complementary roles of intellect and divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the two-part classification.
2
Identify the two types of teachings
The passage explicitly says shara'i' are of two types. Find and list them.
The first type includes fundamental beliefs; the second includes worship specifics.
3
Understand the role of miracles
The passage states that the prophet's speech, when supported by miracles, becomes a cause of knowledge. Reflect on why this matters for the validity of faith.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Consider why this classification was important in the context of arguments about the necessity of revelation and the limits of reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القائلون: إن الشرط لصيرورة الإيمان نافعا أو صيرورة التصديق إيمانا، هو اقتران الدليل به غير أن ذلك الدليل قد يكون كلام الرسول الذي عرف ما المعتقد له قيام المعجزات على يده فيثبت له العلم بخبره بجميع يجب اعتقاده في حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع وغيره، إذ خبر من تأيد بالمعجزة من أسباب العلم، وكان الاعتقاد مبنيا على الدليل الموجب للعلم فإنهم يقولون ما ورد الرسل صلوات الله عليهم به من الشرائع المتضمنة لصالح العاجلة والآجلة فهو على نوعين: أحدهما في ما في العقول إمكان الوقوف عليه، والوصول إليه بالتأمل والفكر كمعرفة حدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع ووحدانيته واتصافه بصفات الكمال، وتنزيهه عن سمات النقص، وثبوت النبوات، وأشباه ذلك . والآخر: ما ليس في العقول إمكان الوقوف عليه ككيفيات العبادات وشروط جوازها، وأوقات أدائها، وتقدير الحدود والكفارات وأشباه ذلك.
And as for those who say: The condition for faith becoming beneficial or affirmation (tasdiq) becoming faith is the accompaniment of a proof, except that that proof may be the word of the Messenger whose miracles have been established, so that knowledge of his report is attained regarding all that must be believed concerning the origination of the world, the establishment of the Maker, and other things. For the report of one supported by miracles is among the causes of knowledge, and the belief is built upon a proof that necessitates knowledge. For they say: What the Messengers (peace be upon them) brought of laws containing the benefit of this world and the next is of two types: One: That which it is possible for intellects to ascertain and reach through contemplation and thought, such as knowledge of the origination of the world, the establishment of the Maker, His oneness, His attribution with attributes of perfection, His transcendence above attributes of deficiency, the establishment of prophethoods, and the like. The other: That which intellects cannot possibly ascertain, such as the modalities of worship, their conditions of validity, their times of performance, the determination of legal punishments and expiations, and the like.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents the view of a third group (likely the Maturidi or earlier scholars) who agree that a proof (dalil) must accompany the act of affirmation for it to be beneficial faith. However, they allow that the proof can be the prophet's speech, once the prophet's truthfulness is established through miracles (mu'jizat). The report of a prophet supported by miracles is a cause of knowledge, and belief based on it is built on a proof. This group then classifies the teachings (shara'i') brought by prophets into two categories: (1) Those that human reason can arrive at through contemplation and thought, such as the origination of the world, the existence of the Creator, His oneness, His attributes of perfection, His transcendence above deficiency, the validity of prophethood, etc. (2) Those that reason cannot independently ascertain, such as the specifics of worship (how to pray, etc.), conditions of validity, times, legal punishments (hudud), expiations (kaffarat), and similar matters. This classification highlights the complementary roles of reason and revelation: reason can know the fundamentals, while revelation provides details beyond reason's reach.

Key Terms
شرائع
عقل
دليل
معجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that all religious knowledge must come purely from revelation, with no role for reason.
Correction: This passage shows that reason can independently arrive at foundational truths like God's existence and oneness; revelation provides specifics beyond reason's scope.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a teaching that reason CAN ascertain?
A) The number of rak'ahs in prayer
B) The occurrence of the final judgment
C) The existence of the Creator
D) The amount of zakat

Key Takeaway
Revelation and reason work together: reason establishes fundamental beliefs, while revelation provides specific details of worship and law.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage teaches that faith must be accompanied by a proof. Is your belief in God's existence based on a proof you have personally understood, or is it inherited and unexamined? What would it take for you to build it on a solid dalil?
?
The passage divides prophetic teachings into those reason can grasp and those it cannot. Which revealed command (e.g., prayer timings, fasting rules) have you been negligent in precisely following because you thought it was 'just a detail'? How will you honor that command starting today?
?
The report of a prophet is a cause of knowledge because of miracles. Do you treat the Quran and authentic hadith as certain knowledge that demands immediate action, or as recommendations you can take or leave? Will you choose one verse this week and act on it as if it were a physical proof?

Classification of People by Intellect
Begins classification of people into two groups based on intellectual capacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-fold classification of people according to intellectual capacity as presented by Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
People are divided by intellect, not social status.

Before discussing how different people engage with theological arguments, al-Nasafi first establishes that humanity is not uniform in intellectual ability. He asserts that despite varying capacities, people fall into two fundamental categories. This lesson examines the first category—those blessed with sound intellect and sharp intelligence—and hints at its counterpart.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analysis. Notice the key terms: رجاحة العقول, وفورها, جودة الخواطر, حدة الذكاء. Then read the English translation.
The passage is brief; let its rhythm guide you.
2
Identify the two groups
The author states that people are divided into two groups (قسمين). The first group is described. Try to infer what the second group might be based on the contrast.
The second group is the logical opposite of the first.
3
Analyze the terms used for the first group
Break down the descriptors: رجاحة العقول means soundness/preponderance of intellects; وفورها means abundance; جودة الخواطر means quality of thoughts; حدة الذكاء means sharpness of intelligence. Notice how these traits form a profile of intellectual strength.
4
Reflect on the classification's purpose
Ask yourself: Why would a theologian begin with this classification? How might it affect one's capacity for understanding theological proofs?
This sets the stage for discussions on who can attain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[أقسام الناس]:
وطبقات الناس مع اختلاف أقدارهم يقسمون إلى قسمين: أحدهما: الموصفون برجاحة العقول ووفورها الموسومون بجودة الخواطر وحدة الذكاء
[Divisions of people]:
And the classes of people, despite the difference in their capacities, are divided into two groups: One: those described with soundness and abundance of intellects, characterized by the quality of thoughts and sharpness of intelligence.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This short passage begins a classification of people based on intellectual capacity. The author states that despite differences in human aptitudes, people can be divided into two categories. Only the first category is given: those who possess sound (rajaha) and abundant (wufur) intellects, characterized by good thinking (joodat al-khawatir) and sharp intelligence (hiddat al-dhaka'). The second category is implied as its opposite—presumably those with weaker intellects. This classification likely sets up a discussion about how different people can attain knowledge or fulfill religious obligations, but the text breaks off here.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
You have been given the blessing of hearing this classification. If you are among the first group, what weight of responsibility do you now bear to seek knowledge and act upon it? What is one concrete step you will take today to sharpen your intellect (حدة الذكاء) in pursuing the truth of this aqeedah?

Classification of People by Intellectual Capacity
Nasafi divides people into two main intellectual categories with subgroups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main categories of people based on intellectual capacity and their further subdivisions as presented by Imam Nasafi.

Core Idea
People differ in intellect and pursuit, shaping their need for divine guidance.

This lesson presents a foundational classification of people by their intellectual abilities and life pursuits. Recognizing these differences is essential to understanding how divine guidance addresses each group.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
Read the first part of the passage where the author divides people into two broad groups based on intellectual sharpness. Note the descriptions of each.
The first group has sound intellects, the second has dullness.
2
Examine the first category's subgroups
Within the first group, the author distinguishes two subgroups: truth-seekers and worldly-occupied. Understand what characterizes each.
3
Reflect on the purpose of classification
Ask yourself why the author categorizes people this way. What does this reveal about human diversity and its relevance to divine guidance?
This classification sets up the discussion of divine mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[أقسام الناس]:
وطبقات الناس مع اختلاف أقدارهم يقسمون (۹) إلى قسمين: أحدهما: الموصفون برجاحة العقول ووفورها الموسومون بجودة الخواطر وحدة الذكاء (٦) والأذهان . والثاني (۱) : الموصوفون بغلظ الأفهام وبلادة الخواطر.
والقسم الأول فرقتان أحدهما المتفرغة للتأمل والتفكر والبحث عن الحقائق واليقين (۲)، سمت (۳) بهم (4) همتهم إلى استخراج ودائع العقول وخزائن الأفهام، والوقوف على ما به الفوز لهم بالسعادة الأبدية، والوصول إلى مرضاة خالق البرية جل وتعالى. والأخرى من الفريقين المشتغلة باكتساب أسباب المعاش، المعرضة عن التأمل في المعالم النظرية () ، الراضية لنفسها درجة البهائم، لاقتصارها (٦) على استجلاب اللذات الحاضرة، ونيل شهواتها الطبيعية المقبلة على تكثير الأموال النفيسة، والعقود الخطيرة بأنواع التجارات، وصنوف الزراعات. ثم إن الله تعالى ترحم على الناس كافة ببعث الرسول، وبيان ما يحتاجون إليه في الدارين، فكانت مرحمته تعالى لهم في القسم الذي ليس في قوة العقول الوقوف عليها بإثبات طريق الوصول في حق طبقات الناس كلهم ) (۷) ورحمته تعالى وتفضله في حق القسم الذي العقول إمكان الوقوف عليه في حق الموصوفين بكمال العقول وحدة الخواطر والأذهان () ، المتفرغين للبحث (۹) عن الحقائق، بتسهيل سبيل الوصول، والتنبيه لكيفية الاستدلال، وفي حق الموصوفين بالبلادة وغلط الأفهام بإثبات طريق الوصول. وكذا في حق المعرضين عن طلب الحقائق المشتغلين باكتساب أسباب المعاش، فمن يثبت (١٠) من هؤلاء في حقه (۱۱) معجزة الرسول بالمشاهدة أو بالنقل
[The divisions of people]:
And the classes of people, with the difference of their capacities, are divided into two categories: One of them: those described with soundness of intellects and their abundance, marked by quality of thoughts and sharpness of intelligence and minds. And the second: those described with thickness of understandings and dullness of thoughts.
And the first category is two groups: one of them is those who devote themselves to reflection, thought, investigation of truths and certainty, their aspiration raising them to extract the deposits of intellects and treasuries of understandings, and to understand that through which they attain eternal happiness and reach the pleasure of the Creator of creation, glorified and exalted. And the other of the two groups is those occupied with acquiring means of livelihood, turning away from reflection in theoretical matters, contenting themselves with the rank of beasts, due to their restriction to seeking present pleasures and attaining their natural desires, proceeding to multiply precious wealth and weighty contracts through types of trades and kinds of agriculture.
Then God the Exalted had mercy on all people by sending the Messenger and clarifying what they need in both worlds. So His mercy was for them regarding the category which is not within the power of intellects to comprehend by establishing the path of access for all classes of people. And His mercy and grace regarding the category which the intellects have the possibility of comprehending—for those described with perfection of intellects and sharpness of thoughts and minds, who devote themselves to investigating truths—by facilitating the path of access and alerting to the method of reasoning; and for those described with dullness and thickness of understandings by establishing the path of access. And likewise for those who turn away from seeking truths and are occupied with acquiring means of livelihood; so whoever among these has the miracle of the Messenger established in his case by direct observation or by transmission...
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a classification of people and an explanation of divine mercy. The author first divides people into two broad categories based on intellectual capacity: (1) those with sound, abundant intellects and sharp minds, and (2) those with thick understandings and dull thoughts. Within the first category, he identifies two subgroups: (a) those who devote themselves to reflection and the search for truth, aiming for eternal happiness and divine pleasure; (b) those who are absorbed in earning a livelihood and worldly pleasures, content with a bestial rank. Then the author discusses God's mercy through sending the Prophet. God's mercy encompasses both categories: For matters beyond reason, He provides the path (revelation) for all people. For matters accessible to reason, He facilitates the path for the intellectually perfect who seek truth by clarifying methodology; for the dull, He provides the path directly. Even for those absorbed in worldly pursuits, if they witness the prophet's miracle or hear authentic transmission, they can benefit. The passage emphasizes that prophetic guidance is tailored to human diversity.

Key Terms
رجاحة العقول
جودة الخواطر
بلادة الخواطر
التأمل
التفكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, into how many main categories are people divided?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
People vary in intellectual capacity and life direction, setting the stage for varied divine mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you were to honestly evaluate the soundness of your own intellect in light of this classification, would you say you are using it for its ultimate purpose? What would need to change for you to move toward the truth-seeking group?
?
This lesson divides people by intellectual capacity and direction. Does your current life reflect a struggle for eternal happiness, or have you settled for a 'bestial rank'? What concrete step will you take today to realign your pursuit?

Divine Mercy Tailored to Human Diversity
Nasafi explains divine mercy through prophets reaches all classes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate how divine mercy through prophets is customized to each class of people based on their intellectual capacity and circumstances.

Core Idea
Prophetic guidance adapts to every human condition.

After classifying people, the author explains how God's mercy through the Prophet reaches all. This lesson shows that revelation accommodates both the intelligent seekers, the dull, and the worldly absorbed.

Guided Path
1
Read the mercy passage
Start from 'ثم إن الله تعالى ترحم على الناس كافة' and read through the end of the chunk. Understand that God sent the Prophet with all needed guidance.
2
Identify the categories of mercy
Notice how mercy is described for three groups: matters beyond reason, the intelligent truth-seekers, and the dull/worldly.
Each group receives a different form of assistance.
3
Reflect on the inclusiveness
Consider how this teaching emphasizes that prophetic guidance is available to all, regardless of intellect or distraction.
This counters the idea that only intellectuals can benefit.

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Claim: Kalām Is Not a Condition for Faith
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that kalam is a condition for valid faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imām al-Nasafī argues from the practice of the Prophet and his successors that kalām is not obligatory for valid faith.

Core Idea
The Prophet's acceptance of faith without kalām proves it is not a condition.

The Mu'tazilites held that rational inference (kalām) is necessary for genuine faith. But how could that be, when the Prophet and his successors accepted the faith of simple villagers who only knew of miracles via tawātur? Al-Nasafī uses this historical practice to expose the absurdity of the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Notice the structure: first an observation, then a hypothetical, then two options, then a conclusion.
2
Identify the two alternatives
If the Mu'tazilites were correct that faith requires kalam, then the Prophet and successors would have had to either reject the faith of villagers or teach them kalam. Find these two options in the text.
The two options are marked by 'إما... وإما'.
3
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
The author then shows that neither option was taken, so the Mu'tazilite view contradicts the practice of the Prophet. Worse, it would imply the Prophet was foolish and negligent—an impossibility.
This is a classic reductio argument.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this argument establishes that faith does not require formal study of kalam. This does not diminish the value of kalam for those who can engage, but it rejects it as a condition for all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لاشتغلوا الفلاحة وعمارة الأراضي وكري الأنهار. وكذا إيمان غيرهم من أهل القرى والرساتيق من المجوس وغيرهم مع معرفتهم بخلوهم عن صناعة الاستدلال، لما أنهم رأوهم قد صدقوا رسولهم بعدما بلغهم بطريق التواتر ظهور المعجزات على يده، ولو لم يكن ذلك إيمانا لفقد شرطه وهو الاستدلال العقلي بأحد أمرين: إما بالإعراض عن قبول إسلامهم وحكموا ببقائهم على ما كانوا عليه من الكفر، وضربوا الجزية على جماجمهم، والخراج على أراضيهم وعاملوهم معاملة أهل الذمة. وإما بنصب متكلم حاذق بصير بالأدلة، عالم بكيفية المحاجة، ليعلمهم جميعا صناعة الكلام، وعند امتناعهم وامتناع كل من الأئمة في البلاد أجمع إلى يومنا هذا عن ذلك وتركهم جميع العوام يشتغلون بالمكاسب، ولا يتعلمون الكلام ظهر أن ما ذهب إليه الخصوم خلاف صنع رسول الله وخلفائه وأئمة أمته وجميع المسلمين، وما هذا سبيله فهو باطل، والله الموفق.

بيان فساد قول المعتزلة

يحقق هذا أن الصنيع من الرسول وخلفائه وصحابته وأئمة أمته على رأي الخصوم داخل في حيز السفه، وبقيت على الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم عهدة التكليف بتعليم من بلغ إليهم الرسالة نهاية علم الأصول، وإذا فارق الدنيا ولم يفعل ذلك وحكم بإيمانهم كان مخطئا في الحكم بإيمانهم مقصرا في أداء ما أرد بأدائه إلى الناس، غير مبلغ ما وجب عليه تبليغه، كاذبا في دعواه التبليغ، مرتكبا المآثم في الامتناع عن التبليغ على وجه يصير من اتبعه مؤمنا خارجا عن الإيمان بارتكابه هذه الكبيرة وهي الامتناع عن التبليغ على مذهبهم مستحقا الخلود في النار، وكل قول هذا عقباه ففساده لا يخفى على المجانين فضلا عن العقلاء
They would have occupied themselves with agriculture, cultivation of lands, and digging canals. And likewise the faith of others among the people of villages and rural districts from the Magians and others, despite their knowledge that they lack the craft of inference, because they saw that they had believed their messenger after reaching them via tawātur the appearance of miracles at his hand. And if that were not faith due to the absence of its condition, which is rational inference, then one of two things: either to turn away from accepting their Islam and judge them to remain upon their previous disbelief, and impose the jizya on their heads and the kharāj on their lands and treat them as dhimmīs; or to appoint a skilled mutakallim discerning of proofs, knowledgeable in the manner of disputation, to teach them all the craft of kalām. And when they refrained, and every one of the imams in all lands up to this day refrained from that, and left all the common people to occupy themselves with livelihoods and not learn kalām, it became evident that what the opponents have gone to is contrary to the practice of the Messenger of Allah, his successors, the imams of his nation, and all Muslims. And whatever is such is false. And Allah is the Granter of success.

Statement of the invalidity of the Mu'tazilite claim

This confirms that the conduct of the Messenger, his successors, his Companions, and the imams of his nation, according to the opinion of the opponents, falls within the realm of foolishness. And the responsibility of obligation would remain upon the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) to teach the ultimate science of principles to those whom the message reached. And when he left the world without doing that, and he judged their faith to be valid, he would be mistaken in judging their faith, negligent in performing what he intended to convey to the people, not delivering what was obligatory upon him to deliver, lying in his claim of delivery, committing sins in refraining from delivery in a way that the one who follows him becomes a believer, but he himself would exit from faith by committing this major sin, which is refraining from delivery according to their school, deserving eternal punishment in the Fire. And every statement whose consequence is such, its corruption is not hidden from the insane, let alone the rational.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that knowledge of kalām is a necessary condition for valid faith. The author, Imām al-Nasafī, argues from the actual practice of the Prophet and his successors. He first notes that many people, such as villagers and Magians, believed in the messenger simply because they heard via tawātur that miracles appeared at his hand, without any knowledge of kalām. If the Mu'tazilites claim that such belief is invalid due to lack of rational inference, then the Prophet and his successors would have had to either reject their Islam or appoint theologians to teach them kalām. Since the Prophet and the imams did not do this, it shows that their view contradicts the practice of the Prophet and the consensus of the Muslims. The author then drives the point home by showing the absurd consequences of the Mu'tazilite position: it would imply that the Prophet was foolish, negligent, and even sinful in his mission, which is impossible. Therefore, the Mu'tazilite view is false.

Correction: The Prophet and successors accepted faith without kalām; therefore, it is not a condition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the Mu'tazilite claim that faith requires knowledge of kalam false?
A) Because the Prophet explicitly forbade learning kalam.
B) Because the Prophet and his successors accepted the faith of those who did not know kalam, and did not teach it to everyone.
C) Because the Mu'tazilites relied on weak hadiths.
D) Because kalam is a modern innovation.

Key Takeaway
The practice of the Prophet and his successors demonstrates that kalām is not a condition for valid faith; faith can be based on tawātur and acceptance of miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Prophet accepted the faith of simple villagers who had no kalam training, what does that say about your own tendency to over-intellectualize your belief? Have you ever doubted your own faith because you felt you didn't have enough arguments?
?
The Mu'tazilites made rational inference a condition for salvation – a demand the Prophet himself never made. Are there any conditions you have silently added to faith in your heart, demanding that you or others meet some intellectual standard before being confident in iman? Will you repent from that today?
?
Nasafi shows that claiming the Prophet failed to teach kalam leads to attributing negligence to him. Do you realize that by doubting the validity of simple faith, you are implicitly criticizing the Prophet's own mission? How will you humble yourself and accept that Allah accepts the belief of the sincere, even without theological sophistication?

Miracles as Proof of Prophethood and Basis of Certain Knowledge
Nasafi refutes Mu'tazilite objection and explains miracle-based knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a prophet's statement, supported by miracles, is a valid basis for certain knowledge about God and the world, even without understanding the technical proof mechanism.

Core Idea
A miracle-backed report gives certainty, even without understanding the mechanics of proof.

This passage addresses a Mu'tazilite objection: how can a prophet's mere statement prove God's existence? Nasafi refutes this by showing it leads to absurdity. He then presents the correct method—prophets support their call with miracles, and the reliable transmission of these miracles (tawātur) produces certain knowledge. Importantly, he argues that one need not understand how the miracle proves truthfulness for the belief to be valid knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the Mu'tazilite claim
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that the Mu'tazilites deny the prophet's statement can serve as proof for God and creation. Nasafi says this leads to impossible consequences.
The claim is that a human statement lacks ultimate authority, but Nasafi counters that it leads to corruption of all rational knowledge.
2
Outline the prophet's method of calling
Find the part where the prophet explains the basic beliefs—God's existence, oneness, attributes, prophethood—and then presents a miracle as proof of his truthfulness.
The miracle is a break in the natural order, beyond human capacity.
3
Understand the role of tawātur and report
The listener either witnesses the miracle or learns of it through tawātur (widespread transmission). This report, backed by the miracle, is a cause for certain knowledge.
Knowledge does not require direct observation; reliable testimony suffices.
4
Reflect on the final point about ignorance of mechanism
Nasafi emphasizes that not knowing how the miracle indicates truthfulness does not invalidate the knowledge, because the belief is established on a basis that excludes doubt.
This distinguishes the common believer's certainty from that of a mutakallim who understands the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما اعتمده من الدليل أن قول الرسول لا يصلح دليلا لثبوت الصانع وحدوث العالم، يفضي إلى هذه المعاني المستحيلة، وكل معقول يؤدي إلى فساد فهو فاسد، فكذا هذا. ثم نقول: إن الرسول متى دعا أحدا إلى دينه يبين له جميع ما يعترض اعتقاده فيقول: إن هذا العالم محدث وله صانع واحد لا شريك له، ولا شبيه له موصوف بصفات الكمال متبرئ عن سمات النقص، وأنه بعثني رسولا، وأوجب على عباده كذا ونهاني عن كذا، ثم جعل هذا الناقض للعادة، الخارج عن وسع الخلق دليلا على صدقي، وشاهد هذا المدعو المعجزة أو دعا بعض أئمة الأمة وعرف المدعو ثبوت المعجزة بالتواتر، فصدقه هذا المدعو، واعتقد جميع ما دعاه إليه بناء على خبر، وخبر من دلت المعجزة على صدقه سبب لثبوت العلم فيبني الرجل اعتقاده في جميع ما دعا إليه على ما هو دليل، غير أنه لا يعرف كيفية دلالة المعجزة على صدق الآتي بها وهو قولنا: إنه حكيم لا يقيم الدلالة على يدي المتنبي، أو هو كامل القدرة لا يتصور عجزه عن إثبات التفرقة بين الحق والباطل والجهل بكيفية دلالة الدليل يوجب خروج اعتقاده عن كونه علما، لثبوته على وجه ينافي جميع أضداد العلم لا
And what they relied upon as evidence, that the statement of the messenger is not valid as a proof for establishing the existence of the Maker and the temporal origination of the world, leads to these impossible meanings. And every intelligible that leads to corruption is corrupt, so likewise this. Then we say: Whenever the messenger calls someone to his religion, he explains to him all that concerns his belief. He says: 'This world is temporally originated and has one Maker, no partner for Him, no likeness to Him, described with attributes of perfection, free from signs of deficiency, and that He sent me as a messenger, and He obligated upon His servants such-and-such and forbade me from such-and-such. Then He made this miracle, which breaks the usual course, beyond the capacity of creation, a proof of my truthfulness.' And this caller witnesses the miracle, or some imam of the nation invites him, and the caller comes to know the establishment of the miracle via tawātur. So the caller believes him, and believes all that he called him to based on report. And the report of one whose truthfulness is proven by miracle is a cause for the attainment of knowledge. So the man builds his belief in all that he was called to upon what is proof, except that he does not know the manner of the miracle's indication of the truthfulness of its bringer. And our statement is: He is wise, He does not establish proof upon the hand of a false prophet, or He is perfect in power, His inability to establish the distinction between truth and falsehood is inconceivable. And ignorance of the manner of indication of the proof does not necessitate the removal of his belief from being knowledge, because it is established in a way that contradicts all opposites of knowledge. No.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim. The author first states that the Mu'tazilite argument that the prophet's statement cannot serve as evidence for God's existence and the world's creation leads to impossible consequences, and is thus invalid. He then presents the correct method: when a prophet calls people, he explains foundational beliefs—God's oneness, attributes, and his own prophethood—and then supports his call with a miracle that breaks the natural order. The listener either witnesses the miracle directly or learns of it through tawātur. This report, backed by a miracle, produces certainty. The author emphasizes that even if the believer does not understand the technical mechanism of how the miracle proves truthfulness, his belief remains valid knowledge because it is based on a reliable foundation that excludes doubt. The passage concludes with a statement affirming the wisdom and power of God in distinguishing truth from falsehood through miracles.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must understand the rational proof of how a miracle indicates truthfulness for belief to be considered certain knowledge.
Correction: The author states that ignorance of the manner of indication does not remove the belief from being knowledge, as long as the belief is based on a reported miracle that is established reliably.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes the report of the prophet a cause for certain knowledge?
A) The inherent authority of the prophet's statement.
B) The miracle that proves his truthfulness and the reliable transmission of that miracle.
C) The listener's ability to perform rational arguments.
D) Direct observation of God.

Key Takeaway
Belief in a prophet's message becomes certain knowledge through the miraculous proof and its reliable transmission, not through understanding the logical mechanism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you base your faith on the miracle-backed report of the Prophet, or do you secretly feel that your belief is incomplete until you understand the philosophical mechanics? Examine your heart: is your certainty anchored in the external proof or in your own reasoning?
?
When you hear a message from the Prophet today, do you respond with the same immediate acceptance as someone who witnessed the miracle? What would it take for you to surrender your doubts and build your entire creed on the report of a truthful messenger?
?
If you were to meet a claimant to prophethood tomorrow, would you demand a miracle before believing? And if you saw one, would you base your entire worldview on that single event? Reflect: is your faith currently built on such a foundation, or on something less certain?

Doubt, Knowledge, and al-Jubba'i's Inconsistency
Analyzes doubt's effect on knowledge and refutes al-Jubba'i's inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jubba'i's view on doubt negating knowledge contradicts his own definition of knowledge.

Core Idea
A doubt only negates knowledge if attended to, and al-Jubba'i's definition proves him wrong.

In this passage, the author examines the relationship between doubt and knowledge, first clarifying when doubt actually negates knowledge, then exposing a critical inconsistency in al-Jubba'i's position. This argument is a classic example of internal critique in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: first a conditional analysis, then a charge of contradiction.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two scenarios
The author presents two scenarios regarding doubt-engendering suggestions. Find them and see how each affects knowledge.
3
Understand the contradiction
The author points out that al-Jubba'i's definition of knowledge implies that belief in prophetic news is knowledge, yet he denies it. See how the author uses al-Jubba'i's own definition against him.
This is a powerful refutation technique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يخطر بباله الشبهة المؤكدة للشك أو اعترضت ولم يلتفت إليها فلم يحصل له الشك المنافي للعلم حصل له العلم. ولو خطرت بباله الشبهة المولدة للشك، ووقع له الشك انتفى العلم عنه لوجود ما يضاده. على أن هذا من الجبائي مناقضة ظاهرة فإنه قال في تحديد العلم إنه اعتقاد الشيء على ما هو به عن ضرورة أو دليل، وهذا اعتقاد الشيء عن دليل لأن خبر المؤيد بالمعجزة دليل يوجب العلم ذلك لم يجعل هو هذا الاعتقاد علما، والله الموفق.
If the doubt-engendering suggestion (shubha) occurs to his mind and he does not pay attention to it, then the doubt (shakk) contrary to knowledge does not occur to him, and he obtains knowledge. But if the doubt-engendering suggestion occurs to him and doubt occurs, knowledge is negated from him due to the existence of that which opposes it. Yet this from al-Jubba'i is an apparent contradiction, for he said in defining knowledge that it is the affirmation of a thing as it is on the basis of necessity or proof, and this is affirmation of a thing on the basis of proof because news supported by a miracle is a proof necessitating knowledge – yet he did not make this affirmation knowledge. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of al-Jubba'i's view on doubt and knowledge. The author presents a hypothetical: if a doubt-inducing suggestion occurs but the person does not attend to it, no doubt contrary to knowledge arises, so knowledge remains. But if he attends to it and doubt arises, knowledge is negated. Then the author points out al-Jubba'i's inconsistency: al-Jubba'i defines knowledge as belief based on necessity or proof, and the news of a prophet supported by miracles is a proof that produces knowledge. Yet al-Jubba'i does not consider this belief (tasdiq) as knowledge. The author concludes that this is a clear self-contradiction.

شك
علم
اعتقاد
دليل
ضرورة
مناقضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage is primarily about defining knowledge.
Correction: The passage is actually about the effect of doubt on knowledge and exposing al-Jubba'i's inconsistency, not about providing a definition of knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which condition does knowledge remain despite the occurrence of a doubt-engendering suggestion?
A) When the doubt-engendering suggestion is strong and persistent.
B) When the person does not pay attention to the suggestion.
C) When the person actively seeks evidence to refute it.
D) When the suggestion is from a reliable source.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's inconsistency undermines his position because his own definition of knowledge would require him to accept that prophetic news yields knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a doubt about your faith crosses your mind, do you immediately attend to it, letting it grow, or do you disregard it as the passage advises? What would change if you intentionally ignored such doubts?
?
Al-Jubba'i contradicted his own definition. Are there areas in your life where your actions contradict what you claim to believe? What practical step can you take today to align them?
?
The passage shows that attending to a doubt can negate knowledge. Is there a doubt you have entertained that has weakened your certainty? What concrete act of worship or study will you perform this week to reaffirm your belief?

Ash'ari's Self-Contradiction in Defining Jawhar
Nasafi points out two ways Ash'ari's definition of jawhar contradicts his own principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two ways in which Nasafi demonstrates that Ash'ari's definition of jawhar contradicts his own theological principles.

Core Idea
Ash'ari's definition of jawhar contradicts his own principles in two clear ways.

This passage is a direct critique by Nasafi of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's definition of jawhar. Nasafi shows that Ash'ari's definition violates his own stated principles about definitions and about the uniqueness of self-subsistence. Let's examine the two contradictions and Nasafi's suggested alternative.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two contradictions
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that Nasafi explicitly says there are two contradictions (من وجهين).
Focus on the words 'qa'im bi al-dhat' and 'tarkib al-hadd'.
2
Understand Ash'ari's principles
Nasafi refers to two of Ash'ari's principles: (1) Only Allah is self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat). (2) A definition should be a single attribute, not composed. Understand why these are foundational.
These principles are stated in the passage itself.
3
Consider Nasafi's alternative definition
Nasafi suggests that Ash'ari could have defined jawhar as simply 'receptive of opposites' (al-qabil lil-mutadaaddat). This would avoid both contradictions while still being effective.
Nasafi adds that this alternative would pass the test of tard wa 'aks.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبو الحسن الأشعري ذكر هذا التحديد بهذه العبارة وهذا منه نقض لأصله من وجهين: أحدهما أنه يقول: لا قائم بالذات إلا الله تعالى وقد وصف الجوهر بأنه القائم بالذات. والآخر أن من أصله أن الحد ينبغي أن يكون مستقلا بصفة واحدة، وهو يأبى تركيب الحد من الوصفين فصاعدا، ويزعم أن الحد هو الإبانة عن حقيقة المحدود، والشيء الواحد لن يكون له إلا حقيقة واحدة وغيره من المتكلمين والفلاسفة لا يبالون من تركيب الحد من الوصفين فصاعدا فيقولون: الغرض من التمييز بين المحدود بجميع أجزائه وبين غيره مما ليس هو ولا جزء منه، فلا هذا يبالي بعد حصول الغرض من كونه متركبا من وصفين وزيادة، ثم هو مع الأصل ذكر الحد المتركب، ولو اكتفى بقوله: القابل للمتضادات لكان كافيا ولم ينتقض شيء من أصوله، وظهرت صحته بالامتحان بالطرد والعكس، ولعله قال هذا على طريق المساهلة.
And Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari mentioned this definition with this expression, and this is from him a contradiction of his own principle (naqd li-aslihi) in two respects: One of them is that he says: 'There is no self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat) except Allah the Exalted,' yet he described jawhar as being self-subsistent. The other is that it is part of his principle that a definition (hadd) ought to be independent with a single attribute, and he rejects composing a definition from two attributes or more, and he claims that the definition is the clarification (al-ibana) of the reality (haqiqa) of the defined thing, and one thing has only one reality. But other mutakallimun and philosophers do not mind composing a definition from two attributes or more, saying: The purpose is to distinguish the defined thing with all its parts from others that are not it nor any part of it. So this one does not mind after the purpose is achieved if it is composed of two attributes or more. Then he, despite his principle, mentioned a composite definition. If he had sufficed with his statement 'receptive of opposites' (al-qabil lil-mutadaaddat), it would have been sufficient and nothing of his principles would have been contradicted, and its correctness would have appeared by testing with inclusion and exclusion (al-tard wa al-'aks). Perhaps he said this by way of leniency.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a critique. Nasafi argues that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari contradicted his own theological principles in two ways when he defined jawhar as 'self-subsistent and receptive of opposites.' First, Ash'ari holds that only Allah is self-subsistent (qa'im bi al-dhat), yet he applied that attribute to jawhar, a created substance. Second, Ash'ari believes that a definition should be a single attribute, not composed, because the reality of a thing is one. However, his definition of jawhar uses two attributes: 'self-subsistent' and 'receptive of opposites.' Nasafi notes that other scholars allow composite definitions as long as they serve to distinguish the defined. He further suggests that Ash'ari could have sufficed with 'receptive of opposites' alone to avoid contradiction, and that his use of the composite definition may have been for convenience.

Key Terms
النقض (al-naqd)
الأصل (al-asl)
الحد (al-hadd)
التركيب (al-tarkib)
الطرد والعكس (al-tard wa al-'aks)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ash'ari's definition is flawed simply because it is composite.
Correction: The real issue is that Ash'ari's definition contradicts his own methodological positions, not just that it is composite.

Checkpoint
How many contradictions does Nasafi find in Ash'ari's definition of jawhar?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
Ash'ari's definition of jawhar as 'self-subsistent and receptive of opposites' contradicts his own principles that only Allah is self-subsistent and that definitions should be single-attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a principle you profess to live by—like honesty, generosity, or consistency. In what specific situation have you recently contradicted that principle? What will you do before tonight to realign your action with your principle?
?
Nasafi suggests Ash'ari may have been lenient (musahala) in composing his definition. Where in your own life have you relaxed a standard out of convenience or habit, knowing it contradicted a deeper conviction? What is one step you can take today to restore that standard?
?
The passage shows that even a great scholar like Ash'ari can fall into self-contradiction. What contradiction in your own beliefs or actions have you been ignoring? Will you confront it now and seek correction, or continue in inconsistency?
