Maturidi's Strategic Use of Definitions
Nasafi describes Maturidi's method of leniently presenting definitions to opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Maturidi employs a rhetorical strategy in presenting definitions—neither fully adopting nor rejecting them, but using them to lead opponents to their own refutation.

Core Idea
Maturidi mentions definitions not as a proponent, but as a strategist.

In this passage, Nasafi reveals al-Maturidi's unique approach to theological definitions. Rather than engaging in a direct refutation, Maturidi presents a definition of 'body' in a seemingly lenient manner, throwing the reins to his opponents. This lesson explores his method and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the contrast between 'clinging to' and 'leniently throwing the reins'.
The Arabic uses vivid metaphors like 'throwing the reins'.
2
Identify Maturidi's rhetorical stance
Identify that Maturidi does not adopt the definition but presents it as a target for his opponents. Ask: Why would he do this?
He is confident of his ability to refute it.
3
Reflect on the methodological principle
Notice Nasafi's comment about Maturidi's habit: he does not define things unless necessary for religion. Relate this to the current case.
This shows his pragmatic approach.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the MCQ about Maturidi's method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي ذكر هذا الحد لا كالمتشبث به، بل كالمتساهل الملقي زمام كلامه إلى خصومه ليقوده إلى مرامه ثقة منه بضعفه وعجزه عن مقاومته في محل النزاع والجدال، وقال: إن كان الجسم في الشاهد اسم ذي الجهات، أو اسم محتمل النهايات أو اسم ذي الأبعاد الثلاثة، فغير جائز القول به في الله تعالى. وربما يشير في أثناء كلامه إلى معنى التأليف. وذلك لأن من دأبه أنه لا يشتغل بتعريف حقيقة شيء ما إذا لم يكن به إلى ذلك حاجة في أمر دينه، وهذا من هذا القبيل، فإن الجسم لابد أنه اسم لما له التركيب، فبعد ذلك لا فرق في استحالته على الله تعالى بين أن يكون اسما لمطلق التركيب أم لتركيب مخصوص يحصل به الأبعاد الثلاثة.
And Sheikh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi mentioned this definition not as one who clings to it, but as one who is lenient, throwing the reins of his speech to his adversaries to lead him to his goal, confident in his weakness and inability to resist them in the place of dispute and argumentation. And he said: 'If body in the witnessed world is a name for that which has directions, or a name for that which admits of ends, or a name for that which has three dimensions, then it is not permissible to say it of Allah, exalted.' And perhaps he indicates in the course of his speech the meaning of composition (al-ta'lif). And that is because his habit is that he does not occupy himself with defining the reality of anything if he has no need for that in a matter of his religion, and this is of that category. For body necessarily is a name for that which has composition, and after that there is no difference in its impossibility regarding Allah, exalted, between its being a name for absolute composition or for a specific composition by which the three dimensions come about.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage describes al-Maturidi's approach to the definition of body. Nasafi portrays him as not adopting the definition but rather presenting it leniently to his opponents, confident in his ability to refute it. Maturidi's key point is that regardless of how body is defined in the witnessed world—whether by directions, ends, or three dimensions—it cannot be applied to Allah. He hints that body necessarily implies composition (ta'lif), and once composition is admitted, both absolute composition and specific composition yielding three dimensions are equally impossible for Allah. This reflects Maturidi's methodological principle: he does not engage in defining realities unnecessarily, but when needed, he focuses on the essential point (composition) rather than specifics.

Key Terms
ماتريدي
جسم
جهات
نهايات
أبعاد ثلاثة
تأليف
تركيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidi was unsure or indecisive about the definition of body.
Correction: Maturidi is not indecisive; he strategically presents the definition to his opponents, confident that it cannot apply to Allah, thus refuting them.

Checkpoint
What is al-Maturidi's primary purpose in mentioning the definition of body in a 'lenient' manner?
A) To show that he does not know the correct definition
B) To lead his opponents into accepting a false definition
C) To let the definition lead to its own refutation when applied to Allah
D) To avoid discussing the topic altogether

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's method is strategic: he presents definitions not as accepted truths but as tools to expose the impossibility of applying them to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you present an argument about your faith, do you ever allow the other person's premises to lead the discussion? How confident are you that ultimate truth will prevail, like Maturidi's confidence in letting opponents 'lead' to his goal?
?
Maturidi avoided defining things unless necessary for religion. In what areas of your own belief or practice do you obsess over definitions that are not required, and how might that distract from the core of your faith?
?
Are you more invested in winning theological debates or in reaching the truth? Reflect on Maturidi's strategic leniency: would you be willing to temporarily adopt an opponent's view to guide them to the correct conclusion, or does your pride prevent that?

Why Allah Cannot Be Called a Body – The Composition Argument
Maturidi argues that body implies composition, which is impossible for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Maturidi's argument that body necessarily implies composition, making it impossible to apply to Allah regardless of the specific definition used.

Core Idea
Body = composition; composition is impossible for Allah.

Having established his rhetorical approach, al-Maturidi now delivers the core logical argument: body, by its very nature, implies composition (tarkib). Whether one defines body as 'absolute composition' or as 'a specific composition yielding three dimensions', the result is the same—it is impossible for Allah. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the logical connection: 'body necessarily is a name for that which has composition'.
The passage uses the particle 'فإن' indicating a result/conclusion.
2
Identify the core claim
Identify that Maturidi asserts body = composition (tarkib). This is the key premise.
He says 'لا بد أنه اسم لما له التركيب'.
3
Understand the 'no difference' clause
Notice that Maturidi says there is no difference between absolute composition and specific composition (yielding three dimensions) in terms of impossibility for Allah.
This closes any loophole for defining body differently.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why composition is impossible for Allah. In kalam, composition implies need and contingency, which contradict divine simplicity and necessity.
This principle is central to Sunni theology.
5
Test yourself with the checkpoint
Answer the MCQ to confirm your understanding.

Source Text

Maturidi's Cautious Methodology
Maturidi's evaluation of a prior opinion and his methodological principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's principle of refraining from theological inquiry that is not necessary for establishing the fundamentals of religion.

Core Idea
Maturidi prioritizes religious necessity over speculative curiosity.

In this passage, the author presents the stance of Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi on a controversial view about the composition of bodies. Maturidi shows a slight inclination but ultimately refrains from adopting it, laying down a key methodological principle: avoid what is not needed for the foundations of faith. This lesson unpacks his cautious approach.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's evaluation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice that Maturidi 'inclines slightly' but then distances himself from the view.
The key phrase: 'he did not adopt it as his own school.'
2
Identify the reason for refraining
Find the part where Maturidi states why it is safer to refrain. It is because there is no religious obligation concerning ignorance of it.
He cites his book al-Maqalat.
3
Understand the methodological principle
The text explains that the Hanafi-Maturidi school does not investigate realities not needed for correcting the fundamentals of religion. Ask yourself: what is needed? (Affirming accidents and using them to prove temporal origination).
The unknown is whether a body is something beyond accidents – this is unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور - رحمه الله - وإن ركن إلى هذا القول قليل ركون لما يزعم أرباب هذا القول إذ إثبات شيء قائم بالذات في الشاهد ليس بمتركب من هذه الأعراض التي بيناها، خروج عن الحس، إذ لا إدراك لشيء بالحواس سوى هذه الأعراض التي بيناها فقضى لهذا الرأي بضرب رجحان إلا أنه هذا لم يرض به لنفسه مذهبا فإنه نص في كتابه المقالات وقال في الكلام في هذه الأقاويل : هو أمر الكف عنه أسلم لما لم يعرف فيه فرضا في الجهل به بصنيعه، وهذا لأن المشهور من مذاهب أصحابنا أنهم لا يشتغلون بالبحث عن حقائق الأشياء التي لا حاجة بهم إلى معرفتها في تصحيح أصول الدين، وهذا من هذا القبيل إذ الحاجة في الباب إلى إثبات الأعراض واستحالة انفكاك ما قام بالذات أجزاء العالم عنها، والاستدلال بذلك على حدوث الكل، فأما معرفة كون ما من قام بالذات من ذلك شيئا وراء هذه الأعراض أو راجعا إلى هذه الأعراض التي لا ينفك عنها الجسم فلا حاجة بنا إلى معرفته، والله ولي التوفيق. هذا هو الكلام في الجوهر والأجسام. [أقسام الأعراض]: فأما في الأعراض فنقول : إن الأعراض ثابتة عند أكثر العقلاء، وهي الألوان والأكوان والطعوم والروائح، والعلوم والقدر والإرادات والاعتقادات
And the Shaykh, the Imam Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him), though he inclined slightly towards this opinion due to what its proponents claim—since affirming something self-subsistent in the observable world that is not composed of these accidents we have mentioned would be a departure from sensory evidence, for nothing is perceived by the senses except these accidents we have mentioned—so he granted some weight to this view. However, he did not adopt it as his own school, for he stated in his book al-Maqalat, and said regarding discussion of these sayings: 'Refraining from it is safer, since no religious obligation is known concerning ignorance of it, due to one's own action.' This is because the well-known position of our companions is that they do not engage in investigating the realities of things that they do not need to know for the correction of the fundamentals of religion. This matter is of that kind, for the need in this subject is to affirm the accidents and the impossibility of the self-subsistent parts of the world being free from them, and to deduce from that the temporal origination of the whole. As for knowing whether that which is self-subsistent is something beyond these accidents or reducible to these accidents from which the body is never free, we have no need to know it. And God is the Grantor of success. This is the discussion concerning substance (jawhar) and bodies. [Sections of Accidents]: Regarding accidents, we say: Accidents are established according to most rational people. They are colors, modes (akwan), tastes, smells, knowledges, powers, wills, and beliefs.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage begins by discussing the stance of the great scholar Abu Mansur al-Maturidi regarding the previously mentioned heretical view. The author notes that Maturidi inclined slightly towards that view because, in the observable world, we perceive only accidents, and positing something self-subsistent beyond those accidents seems to go beyond sensory evidence. Therefore, he gave the view some credence. However, Maturidi did not adopt it as his own doctrine. He stated in his book al-Maqalat that refraining from such inquiry is safer because there is no religious obligation concerning ignorance of it. The reason is that the established practice of the Hanafi-Maturidi scholars is to avoid investigating the realities of things that are not needed for establishing the fundamentals of religion. In this case, what is needed is to affirm accidents, to show that the parts of the world cannot be free from them, and to use that as a proof for the temporal origination of the entire world. But whether the self-subsistent entity is something beyond these accidents or is reducible to them—this we do not need to know. The passage ends by concluding the discussion on substance (jawhar) and bodies. Then a new section begins: 'Sections of Accidents'. The author states that accidents are confirmed by most rational people, and lists them: colors, modes (akwan – such as motion, rest, and other spatial states), tastes, smells, knowledges, powers, wills, and beliefs.

Key Terms
أبو منصور الماتريدي
المقالات
الأعراض
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi endorsed the view that bodies are composed of accidents.
Correction: He only inclined slightly but explicitly refrained from adopting it as his school.

Checkpoint
Why did Maturidi refrain from adopting the view that a body is something beyond accidents?
A) Because it is impossible to know.
B) Because the view is clearly false.
C) Because such knowledge is not required for establishing the fundamentals of religion.
D) Because the heretics might be correct.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that theological inquiry should focus on what is necessary for faith, avoiding speculation about unnecessary realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your religious knowledge where you are tempted to investigate something merely out of curiosity, but which is not needed for your faith? Will you choose to refrain today, as Maturidi teaches? What specific unnecessary question are you holding onto?
?
Maturidi set aside a view he found plausible because he had no religious obligation to know it. What is one theological question you have been wrestling with that you realize you do not need to answer for your salvation? Are you ready to let it go and focus on what is necessary?
?
The lesson shows the wisdom of not overstepping the bounds of needed knowledge. How does this challenge your own intellectual habits? What change will you make to align your study with this methodology?

The Categories of Accidents
Enumeration of the categories of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the main categories of accidents as affirmed by most rational people according to the Maturidi tradition.

Core Idea
Accidents are real and include colors, modes, tastes, smells, and mental attributes.

After concluding the discussion on substance, the text introduces the section on accidents. The author lists the types of accidents that are established by rational consensus: colors, modes (such as motion and rest), tastes, smells, and also mental attributes like knowledge, power, will, and belief. This lesson focuses on that classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of accidents
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify each category mentioned: colors, modes, tastes, smells, knowledges, powers, wills, beliefs.
The list is concise; note that 'akwan' (modes) includes motion, rest, etc.
2
Group the accidents
Notice that the first four are sensible qualities perceived by the senses, while the last four are mental attributes. This grouping is implicit.
3
Reflect on their classification as accidents
These are all considered accidents because they are temporally occurring and inhere in substances. Ask yourself why each is an accident.
Accidents are contrasted with substance (jawhar).

Al-Maturidi on Refraining from Speculation on Atoms
Al-Maturidi explains why the school refrains from unnecessary investigation into atoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam al-Maturidi and his school refrained from unnecessary metaphysical speculation on the nature of atoms, and how this reflects a broader methodological principle in kalam.

Core Idea
The Maturidi school limits inquiry to what is needed for religious foundations.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi discusses a specific view about atoms and explains why he did not adopt it. He then articulates a key methodological principle of the Maturidi school: they do not investigate realities that are not required for establishing the foundations of religion (usul al-din). Understanding this principle helps us appreciate the practical and focused approach of classical Sunni kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice the structure: first the view, then al-Maturidi’s stance, then the school’s principle.
The passage is dense but clear.
2
Identify the debated view
What is the claim that al-Maturidi discusses? It affirms a thing subsisting in the essence not composed of accidents. Why does he consider it a departure from sense perception? Because we only perceive accidents.
This view is not adopted.
3
Understand al-Maturidi’s rationale
Why does he refrain? Because it’s safer, there’s no obligation, and ignorance of it does not harm practice. The school avoids investigation into realities not needed for usul al-din.
The needed investigation is to prove accidents and the temporality of the world.
4
Apply the principle
Ask yourself: What other questions might the Maturidi school avoid? How does this principle focus kalam on what is necessary for faith?
This reflects a pragmatic and protective approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشيخ الإمام أبو منصور - رحمه الله - وإن ركن إلى هذا القول قليل ركون لما يزعم أرباب هذا القول إذ إثبات شيء قائم بالذات في الشاهد ليس بمتركب من هذه الأعراض التي بيناها، خروج عن الحس، إذ لا إدراك لشيء بالحواس سوى هذه الأعراض التي بيناها فقضى لهذا الرأي بضرب رجحان إلا أنه هذا لم يرض به لنفسه مذهبا فإنه نص في كتابه المقالات وقال في الكلام في هذه الأقاويل : هو أمر الكف عنه أسلم لما لم يعرف فيه فرضا في الجهل به بصنيعه، وهذا لأن المشهور من مذاهب أصحابنا أنهم لا يشتغلون بالبحث عن حقائق الأشياء التي لا حاجة بهم إلى معرفتها في تصحيح أصول الدين، وهذا من هذا القبيل إذ الحاجة في الباب إلى إثبات الأعراض واستحالة انفكاك ما قام بالذات أجزاء العالم عنها، والاستدلال بذلك على حدوث الكل، فأما معرفة كون ما من قام بالذات من ذلك شيئا وراء هذه الأعراض أو راجعا إلى هذه الأعراض التي لا ينفك عنها الجسم فلا حاجة ابنا إلى معرفته، والله ولي التوفيق. هذا هو الكلام في الجوهر والأجسام.
And the Sheikh Imam Abu Mansur - may God have mercy on him - (said): And if one inclines to this statement, little inclination to what the proponents of this statement claim, since affirming a thing subsisting in the essence in the witnessed [world] that is not composed of these accidents which we have explained is a departure from sense perception, for nothing is perceived by the senses except these accidents which we have explained. So this opinion was given a certain preponderance, except that he (Abu Mansur) did not accept it for himself as a doctrine, for he stated in his book Al-Maqalat and said regarding speech about these sayings: 'It is a matter of refraining from which is safer, since no obligation is known in it, and ignorance of it is not detrimental to one's practice.' And this is because the well-known of the doctrines of our companions is that they do not occupy themselves with investigating the realities of things which they have no need to know for the correction of the foundations of religion. And this is of that category, since the need in this chapter is to prove accidents and the impossibility of that which subsists in the essence - the parts of the world - becoming separated from them, and to use that as evidence for the temporal origination of the whole. As for knowing whether that which subsists in the essence is something beyond these accidents or reducible to these accidents from which the body cannot separate, we have no need to know it. And God is the giver of success. This is the discussion on the atom and bodies.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement from the Maturidi school. The author discusses the view that affirms a thing subsisting in the essence that is not composed of accidents. He says that Imam Abu Mansur al-Maturidi did not adopt this view because it goes beyond sense perception—since we only perceive accidents. Although the view has some preponderance, al-Maturidi considered it safer to refrain from it because there is no religious obligation to investigate it, and ignorance of it does not harm one's practice. The author then explains the school's principle: they do not engage in research into realities that are not needed for correcting the foundations of religion. What is needed in this context is to prove accidents and that substances (the parts of the world) cannot be without them, in order to prove the world's temporality. But whether the substance itself is something beyond accidents or reducible to them is not needed. Thus, the school's scope is pragmatic and focused on what is necessary for theological proof.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Maturidi school rejects all metaphysical inquiry about atoms.
Correction: They only avoid speculation unnecessary for establishing the fundamentals of religion, such as the question of whether a substance is distinct from its accidents.

Checkpoint

C) Because he was uncertain about the nature of atoms.
D) Because he favored the view of the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi school refrains from speculating on the intrinsic nature of atoms because such inquiry is neither religiously obligatory nor necessary for establishing the foundations of faith.

?
This lesson teaches that the school only investigates what is necessary for the foundations of religion. Are you willing to honestly examine your own theological inquiries and cut off those driven by pride or curiosity rather than genuine need for your faith and action?

The Two Divisions of the World
Nasafi enumerates the two divisions of the world as foundational classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the world is divided into two categories—substances and accidents—and why this division is essential for proving the world's temporal origin.

Core Idea
The world's parts are either substances or accidents; nothing else exists.

Before proving that the world originated, we must establish its basic building blocks. Imam Nasafi states that the world's parts are either substances (a'yan) or accidents (a'rad). This two-fold division is not arbitrary—it is proven by evidence and forms the necessary premise for the argument for temporality. In this lesson, we will examine this foundational classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: first, a recap of previous work, then the statement of division.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two terms 'عرض' (accident) and 'عين' (substance). Understand what they mean in this context.
Notice that Nasafi says 'ليس وراء هذين القسمين شيء آخر'—nothing beyond these two.
3
Connect to the bigger argument
Consider why this division matters. How does proving that each part is originated lead to the whole world being originated?
This is the premise for the upcoming proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إثبات حدوث العالم بجميع أجزائه: وبعد وقوفنا على جميع أجزاء العالم، وعلى أقاويل القائلين بقدم العالم أو قدم شيء من أجزائه، يجب أن نصرف العناية إلى إثبات حدوث العالم بجميع أجزائه فنقول: إنا أثبتنا بالدليل أن من أجزاء العالم ما هو عرض ومنه ما هو عين، ليس وراء هذين القسمين شيء آخر من أجزاء العالم، فيجب أن نبحث عن كل
Establishing the origination of the world in all its parts: And after we have reviewed all the parts of the world and the statements of those who claim the pre-eternity of the world or of something of its parts, we must direct our attention to establishing the origination of the world in all its parts. So we say: We have proven by evidence that among the parts of the world there is that which is an accident and that which is a substance; there is nothing beyond these two divisions among the parts of the world. So we must investigate each...
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement that begins the proof for the world's origination. Nasafi first recalls that the world is divided into two categories: accidents (a'rad) and substances (a'yan). He claims this division has been established by proof. From this, he proceeds to examine each category to prove its temporality, implying that the temporal initiation of each part will lead to the conclusion that the whole world is originated.

Misconception: Some may think 'عين' means 'essence' in a philosophical sense, but here it refers to substance (jawhar) as opposed to accident.
Correction: In this context, 'عين' is synonymous with 'جوهر' (substance), meaning an independent entity that can exist without being attached to another, as opposed to an accident which needs a substance to exist.

Key Takeaway
The world consists only of substances and accidents; proving each originated proves the whole world originated.

Conclusion: Temporality of the World through Induction
The author summarizes the proof of the world's temporality and describes the inductive method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the temporality of the entire world is proven through induction on its two fundamental components: accidents and substances.

Core Idea
Divide the world into accidents and substances; prove each temporal; the whole world is temporal.

The author now concludes the extensive proof of the world's temporality. He summarizes that since accidents and substances have been proven temporal, everything composed of them—natures, spheres, time, void—must also be temporal. Then he encapsulates the argument in a crisp logical form: the world is only accidents and substances; each has been proven temporal; therefore the whole world is temporal. This is the method of induction, which derives universal conclusions from complete particulars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation to get the flow of the conclusion.
Notice how the author lists entities that become temporal once accidents and substances are proven temporal.
2
Identify the logical structure
The author presents a two-step argument: (1) the world is only accidents and substances, (2) both are temporal. Trace how this implies the temporality of the whole world.
This is a complete induction: covering all parts of the world.
3
Reflect on the inductive method
Consider what 'induction' (istiqra') means here. It is not a probabilistic generalization but a complete survey of all particulars (accidents and substances) leading to a universal conclusion.
The text ends mid-sentence; the original likely explained that induction is established for defining universals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت بما بينا حدوث الأعراض والجواهر كلها ثبت حدوث الطبائع والهيولى وجميع ما تسميه الدهرية والطبيعيون عناصر وإسطقسات وحدوث الأفلاك (بما فيها ) من البروج والكواكب والشمس والقمر وحدوث الزمان والخلاء إذ ذاك كله داخل تحت ما أقمنا الدلالة على حدوثه. والله الموفق. والعبارة عن جملة ما ذكرنا أن العالم قسمان: أعراض وأعيان لا ثالث لهما وقد أقمنا الدلالة على حد وشكل واحد منهما ، فعرف بذلك حدوث كلية العالم، وهذا هو طريقة الاستقراء فإنها وضعت لتعريف الكليات بواسطة الجزئيات وهي
And if it is established, by what we have explained, that all accidents and substances are originated, then the origination of natures, matter, and all that the materialists and naturalists call elements and fundamentals, and the origination of the spheres (with what they contain) of constellations, planets, sun and moon, and the origination of time and void is established, for all that is included under what we have established the proof of its origination. And God is the giver of success. And the expression of the sum of what we have mentioned is that the world is two categories: accidents and substances, with no third. And we have established the proof for each one of them in its specific way. Thus the origination of the entire world is known. And this is the method of induction, for it is established for defining universals by means of particulars, and it is...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is the concluding summary of the proof of the temporality (origination) of the world. The author states that since it has been established that all accidents and substances are originated, then everything composed of them—natures, matter, the elements of the materialists, the celestial spheres, planets, sun, moon, time, and void—must also be originated because they fall under the same proof. Then the author provides a concise formulation: the world is divided into accidents and substances only, and proof for each has been given. From this, the temporality of the entire world is known. This is the method of induction (istiqra'), which defines universals by means of their particulars. The text ends mid-sentence, likely indicating the original source continued.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Induction (istiqra') here means probabilistic generalization from a sample.
Correction: No, the induction used here is complete induction (istiqra' tamm), which surveys all particulars (accidents and substances) to derive a universal conclusion. It is not a probabilistic argument but a logical proof.

Key Takeaway
The temporality of the entire world is proven by establishing that both accidents and substances are temporal, as the world consists only of these two categories.

Induction here is complete—every part is examined. But in your own heart, have you thoroughly examined where you place your trust? Are there small idols you haven't yet surrendered because you haven't fully realized their temporality?

Induction Proves the World's Temporality
Summary of the world's temporality proof using induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Nasafi uses induction to prove the temporality of the entire world from the temporality of its two categories.

Core Idea
Induction from parts to whole yields certainty in proving the world's temporality.

Imam Nasafi now sums up the entire proof that the world is temporal. He reiterates that the world consists only of accidents and substances, each proven temporal, so the whole world is temporal. He then highlights the method of induction, which derives universal truths from particular instances, and claims it achieves certainty like demonstration.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the rhythm, then read the translation carefully.
Notice the concise style of the summary.
2
Break down the logical components

3
Understand the method of induction
Notice that the author calls this reasoning 'induction' (استقراء). Reflect on how induction moves from particulars to universals.
Induction is a key methodological tool in kalam.
4
Consider the claim of certainty
The author claims induction produces certainty like demonstration. Think about why this is important for kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعبارة عن جملة ما ذكرنا أن العالم قسمان: أعراض وأعيان لا ثالث لهما وقد أقمنا الدلالة على حدوث كل واحد منهما، فعرف بذلك حدوث كلية العالم، وهذا هو طريقة الاستقراء فإنها وضعت لتعريف الكليات بواسطة الجزئيات وهي مضاهية لطريق البرهان في إفادة اليقين وإظهار بطلان ما يقابله من الرأي، وقد حصل ذلك بحمد الله.

And this has been achieved, praise be to Allah.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a summary of the previous arguments. The author states that the world consists only of two categories: accidents (non-substantial attributes) and substances (atoms/bodies). Since both have been proven to be temporal (created in time), it follows that the entire world is temporal. The author identifies this reasoning as the method of induction (استقراء), which uses particular instances to establish a universal conclusion. He notes that this method is akin to demonstration (برهان) in generating certainty and refuting opposing views. The passage ends with praise to God for achieving this proof.

Methodological Principle for Debating Dualists
Nasafi states a methodological principle for debating dualists with sequential proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's recommended sequential method for debating dualists, which builds from the temporality of the world to the truth of prophetic teachings.

Core Idea
Debate dualists by proving creation, then the Creator, then prophethood.

In the study of Islamic theology, discussions with dualists (such as the Magians and Thunawiyya) require a clear, logical order of argument. Imam Nasafi outlines the proper sequence: first establish that the world is temporally created, then prove the existence, oneness, and wisdom of the Creator, and finally confirm prophethood and the infallibility of the messenger. Once these foundational premises are accepted, the truth of all prophetic teachings follows, even if we cannot comprehend every detail of divine wisdom. This passage provides the methodological key to cutting off the arguments of opponents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the overall argument. Notice the logical flow from premises to conclusion.
The passage is dense; read it twice if needed.
2
Identify the three premises
Nasafi lists three sequential proofs: huduth al-alam (temporality of the world), attributes of the Creator (existence, oneness, wisdom), and prophethood (message and infallibility).
These are the building blocks of the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
Once these premises are established, everything the prophet conveyed is necessarily true and wise, even if we cannot grasp the specific wisdom.
The conclusion is inevitable from the premises.
4
Apply to debate
This sequence is the recommended method for debating dualists because it effectively refutes their arguments and cuts off their clamor.
Follow this order in discussions with dualists.
5
Reflect on the misconception
A common mistake is thinking one must explain the wisdom behind every ruling. Nasafi clarifies that establishing the general framework suffices.
The proof itself is enough; detailed wisdom may be beyond us.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنا إذا بينا بالدليل حدوث العالم، وثبوت الصانع ووحدانيته وحكمته، وثبوت الرسالة وعصمة الرسول، ثبت بهذه المقدمات الصادقة أن جميع ما بينه الرسول حق وصدق، وفيه حكمة بليغة، وإن قصرت عقولنا وعجزت أفهامنا عن الوقوف على جهة الحكمة، فمن تكلم مع المجوس أو الثنوية ينبغي أن يتكلم على هذا الوجه فإنه أقطع لشغب الخصوم والله المحمود على التوفيق
For if we prove by evidence the temporality of the world, and the establishment of the Maker and His oneness and wisdom, and the establishment of the message and the infallibility of the messenger, then by these true premises it is established that all that the messenger explained is truth and veracity, and in it is profound wisdom, even if our intellects fall short and our understandings are incapable of comprehending the aspect of wisdom. So whoever speaks with the Magians or the dualists should speak in this manner, for it cuts off the clamor of opponents. And Allah is to be praised for granting success.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage serves as a methodological conclusion and practical advice for debating dualists (Majus and Thunawiyya). The author, Nasafi, outlines the proper order of argumentation: first, prove the temporality (huduth) of the world; second, establish the existence of the Creator (al-Sani'), His oneness (wahdaniyya), and wisdom (hikma); third, establish the truth of prophethood (al-risala) and the infallibility of the prophet ('isma). Once these true premises are accepted, it follows necessarily that everything the prophet conveyed is true and contains profound wisdom, even if human intellects cannot fully grasp the specific wisdom behind each command or prohibition. Therefore, when debating dualists, one should argue in this sequence, as it most effectively cuts off their arguments and confusion. The passage ends with praise to God for granting success.

Key Terms
حدوث العالم
صانع
وحدانية
حكمة
رسالة
عصمة
مجوس
ثنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One must explain the wisdom behind every revealed ruling in debates.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that establishing the general framework (temporality, Creator, prophethood) suffices; detailed wisdom may be beyond human comprehension.

Checkpoint

A) Start by arguing about specific rulings.
B) First prove the temporality of the world, then the Creator, then prophethood and infallibility.
C) Focus only on the oneness of God.
D) Accept their premises and argue from them.

Key Takeaway
The sequential proof of creation, Creator, and prophethood is the most effective way to establish the truth of Islam against dualist opponents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a command of Allah that you find difficult to accept or understand, do you submit fully to the truth that the Prophet (peace be upon him) conveyed, or do you demand a rational explanation before obeying? This lesson teaches that even if your intellect falls short, the message is true and full of wisdom. Will you now obey with complete trust?
?

?
This passage says that once we establish the premises, it follows that all the messenger said is true, even if we cannot grasp the specific wisdom. Is there an area of Islam that you internally struggle with because it doesn't make sense to you? Will you now accept it as truth on the authority of the Prophet, relying on the established proof, rather than your limited understanding?
